Allah's Existence
advanced
10 min
The Infinite Regress Objection Against States and Its Resolution
Sanusi presents the objection of infinite regress against states and the first answer of acceptance, followed by al-Razi's criticism and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proponents of states (ahwal) answer the objection of infinite regress by accepting it and then defending against Imam al-Razi's criticism that this blocks the proof of the Creator, using the distinction between regress in existents and regress in non-existent entities.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible in existents but possible in non-existent states.

The theory of states (ahwal) faces a powerful objection: if each state requires a common and distinguishing feature, and those features themselves are states, an infinite regress results. The proponents boldly accept the regress, but Imam al-Razi argues this undermines the proof of the Creator. This lesson explores the defense: the proof of application only blocks regress in existing things, not in non-existent states, relations, or negations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's two formulations
Read the passage where the objector argues that states lead to infinite regress using two formulations: one based on shared and distinguishing features, another based on the concept of state.
The objector wants to force an infinite chain of states.
2
Identify the first answer: acceptance of regress
The proponents answer by accepting the regress in states. Note why they are willing to do so: states are not existent, so regress is harmless.
They do not deny the regress; they embrace it.
3
Examine al-Razi's refutation
Imam al-Razi says this acceptance blocks the proof of the Creator, which relies on the impossibility of regress. Understand his reasoning using the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq).
Al-Razi thinks accepting any regress invalidates the proof.
4
Understand the commentator's defense
The commentator refutes al-Razi by pointing out that the proof only applies to existents, not to non-existent states, relations, or negations. Thus, the proof of the Creator remains intact.
This distinction is crucial for saving the theory of states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كانت واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم فلها حظ من الطرفين، فإنهم يجعلونه قد تجاوز في التحقق حد العدم ولم يبلغ حد الوجود، ولذلك جوزوا أن يكون الحال مقوماً للحقائق الموجودة ولم يجوزوا أن يكون المعدوم مقوماً الخاصة فلا يكون لمفهوم الحال حال زائد على نفسه حتى يتسلسل، كما سيذكره الشارح. فإن قلت : يتم النقض في الأحوال القائمة بالأعراض إذ لو كان إحدى مقوماتها موجودة لزم قيام العرض بالعرض؛ إذ لا شك أن مقوم الشيء يقوم بما يقوم به ذلك الشيء كما مر . قلت : إن كان الكلام في الأحوال المقومة للأعراض فليست بقائمة بها، بل بمحالها، وإن كان في الأحوال الخارجة القائمة بها فقد عرفت أن الاستدلال لا يتم بجواز تقوم الحال بالمعدوم فتأمل. قوله : ( وفيه نظر إلخ ) رده الشارح في حواشي التجريد بما حاصله : أن برهان التطبيق يدل على امتناع التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة، والتزامهم لا ينافي هذا الامتناع . With particularity, and nothing of the shared and the distinguishing is existent nor non-existent, so another state is established, and (thus the states) would regress indefinitely. Or we say: And each one of the particularities is a state sharing with the rest of the states in the concept of state and distinguished from them by another particularity, and so on. (And it was answered by two ways: the first: accepting the regress) in states, (and Imam al-Razi refuted it: because it blocks the door of proving the Maker. And there is a consideration in it; because proving the Maker depends only on...) His statement: (And nothing is etc.) is as previously mentioned. His statement: (Or we say etc.) means that it is possible that the pronoun refers to the particularities. His statement: (And nothing of the shared and the distinguishing is existent nor non-existent, so another state is established, because they are two attributes subsisting in that which the state subsists in, namely the existent; because the constituent of a thing subsists in that which the thing subsists in, so the objection of al-Abhari that there is no necessity of another state based on non-subsistence in the existent is repelled. His statement: (Or we say and it is etc.) has investigation: because the refutation in either of the two ways is only complete if the concept of state is essential for the particularities under it so that their differentiation by differentiae which are also states sharing in the concept of absolute state becomes necessary, and regress becomes necessary, which is denied; because it is permissible that it be a general accident for them, and their differentiation be by their essences, so regress does not become necessary. In summary: the basis of the second way for the affirmers of states is that that by which sharing and distinction occur are among the constituents of existing realities and their essentials, as they affirmed; otherwise, there is no harm in them being non-existent, so the refutation is not directed except after proving that each of the distinguishing and the shared are essential for the states. For if you say: If it is conceded that they are essential for them, the refutation still would not apply, because it is permissible that one of them or both be privative, and the subsistence of the existent by the non-existent is not necessary; rather, the subsistence of that which is neither non-existent nor existent by the non-existent is necessary, and we do not admit its impossibility. For since the state is an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, it has a share from both sides, for they make it have surpassed the limit of non-existence in actuality and not reached the limit of existence, and therefore they allowed the state to be a constituent of existing realities and did not allow the non-existent to be a constituent of them, so they are not prohibited from allowing the state's subsistence by the non-existent. I say: Our discussion is about the states which they affirmed for hypothetical existing realities as constituents of them, and it is not permissible for them to subsist by the non-existent; otherwise, those realities would subsist by it because the constituent of a constituent is a constituent. And it may be answered for the refutation by choosing that the common matter, which is the concept of state, is a state, and the particular matter is existent, so neither the subsistence of an accident by an accident nor subsistence by a non-existent follows. And the discourse cannot be transferred to the concept of state; because it is shared between itself and the particular states, so the concept of state does not have a state additional to itself such that regress would occur, as the commentator will mention. For if you say: The refutation applies to states subsisting in accidents, for if one of their constituents were existent, the subsistence of an accident by an accident would follow; for there is no doubt that the constituent of a thing subsists in that which that thing subsists in, as previously. I say: If the discussion is about states that are constituents of accidents, they do not subsist in them, but rather in their subjects; and if about external states that subsist in them, you have known that the inference is not complete due to the permissibility of the state's subsistence by the non-existent, so reflect. His statement: (And there is a consideration in it etc.) The commentator refuted it in his glosses on al-Tajrid, the gist being: that the proof of application indicates the impossibility of regress in existing things, and their acceptance does not contradict this impossibility, (because regress may be impossible in existents, but not impossible in states which are not existent, just as it is not impossible in relations and negations) unanimously.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the theory of states (ahwal) and its defense. The objector argues that if each state requires both a common and a distinguishing feature, and those features themselves must be states, an infinite regress results. The proponents answer by accepting the regress. Imam al-Razi then refutes this answer, claiming it blocks the proof of the Creator (which relies on the impossibility of infinite regress in existents). The commentator (al-Sharh) defends the proponents by pointing out that the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) only proves the impossibility of regress in existing things, not in states, which are non-existent like relations and negations. Extensive sub-arguments are explored, including whether the common and distinguishing features are essential or accidental, whether they can be privative, and whether states can subsist in non-existents. The passage concludes by affirming that regress in states is not impossible, and thus the proof of the Creator remains intact.

Key Terms
أحوال (ahwal)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
برهان التطبيق (burhan al-tatbiq)
المشترك (al-mushtarak)
المميز (al-mumayyiz)
حال (hal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accepting infinite regress in states would invalidate the proof of the Creator because the proof relies on the impossibility of infinite regress.
Correction: The proof of application only prohibits regress in existents, not in non-existent entities like states, so accepting regress in states does not harm the proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does accepting infinite regress in states not block the proof of the Creator?
A) Because the proof of the Creator does not rely on the impossibility of regress.
B) Because regress in states is impossible, so the objection fails.
C) Because states are not existent, and the proof only applies to existents.
D) Because the regress is only apparent, not real.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress is only impossible for existents, not for non-existent entities like states, so accepting regress in states does not undermine the proof of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical objection to your belief in Allah, do you immediately reject it or do you patiently seek the nuanced distinctions that resolve it? Which approach does this lesson call you to adopt, and why is intellectual humility essential for faith?
?
The passage shows that accepting a harmless regress in non-existents does not weaken the proof of the Creator. Is there an area of your faith where you fear that conceding a logical point might cause doubt? How does this lesson challenge you to trust the precision of the scholars rather than your own fear?
?
The distinction between existents and non-existents is subtle yet crucial. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse what is real (existents) with what are mere mental considerations (states)? How can this lesson help you avoid such confusion when thinking about Allah's attributes?

The Condition for Existence of the Abstracted Essence in the Mind and Its Criticism
Examines the condition for the abstracted essence's existence in the mind and critiques it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition for the abstracted essence's existence in the mind and the criticism of that condition based on the definitions of external and mental accidentals.

Core Idea
The existence of an abstracted essence in the mind depends on how we define 'accidental'.

This passage presents a technical objection and its critique in the context of mental existence. The objection states that if an essence is abstracted only from external accidentals, it can exist in the mind; if abstracted absolutely, it cannot. The critique then examines different meanings of 'external' and 'mental' accidentals, showing that the objection is not straightforward.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two conditions: abstraction from external accidentals only vs absolute abstraction.
2
Identify the two conditions
The objection says: if the essence is abstracted only from external accidentals, it exists in the mind; if abstracted absolutely (from both external and mental), it does not.
3
Follow the critique
The commentator questions whether existence in the mind is an accidental. Then two interpretations of 'external' and 'mental' accidentals are examined, each leading to different conclusions.
4
Understand the two interpretations
First interpretation: external accidentals are what attach to external entities; mental accidentals to mental entities. Second: external accidentals are according to reality; mental are mind-imposed restrictions.
The first interpretation fails to prove impossibility; the second leads to impossibility but also makes being in the mind an external accidental.
5
Consider the third possibility
A third interpretation is suggested: external accidentals only occur to external existents. This makes existence itself an external accidental, supported by the definition of 'state' (al-ḥāl).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إن شرط تجردها عن الأمور واللواحق الخارجية وجدت في الذهن بلا اشتباه ) وإن شرط تجردها مطلقاً) أي من العوارض الخارجية والذهنية معاً (فلا توجد فيه؛ لأن الوجود الذهني من العوارض كما مر . ( وفيه نظر، فإن كونه ) أي كون الشيء ( موجوداً في الذهن ليس من العوارض قوله : (كما مر من أن الماهية في نفسها ليست بموجودة . قوله: (ليس من العوارض الذهنية فيه بحث : أما أولاً فلأنه سيصرح في المقصد السادس بأن العوارض الذهنية ما يعرض للشيء باعتبار وجوده في الذهن نحو الذاتية والعرضية والكلية قوله : ( وقيل إن شرط تجردها إلخ) قيل فيه بحث؛ لأن هذا القائل إن أراد بالعوارض الخارجية ما يلحق الأمور الحاصلة في الأعيان، وبالذهنية ما يلحق الأمور القائمة بالأذهان لا يثبت امتناع وجود المجرد في الخارج بما ذكره، لأن الكون الخارجي أيضاً من العوارض الذهنية بهذا المعنى، لأن زيادته في التعقل. وإن أراد بالعوارض الخارجية ما يكون عروضه بحسب نفس الأمر وبالذهنية ما يجعلها الذهن قيداً فيها، واعتبر عروضها لها من غير أن يكون ذلك بحسب نفس الأمر، يلزم امتناع وجود المجردة عن اللواحق الخارجية في الذهن أيضاً؛ لأن الكون في الذهن أيضاً من العوارض الخارجية بهذا المعنى، ويمكن أن يقال : أراد بالعوارض الخارجية ما لا يعرض إلا للموجود الخارجي سواء كان المعروض موجوداً قبل عروض هذا العارض أو حال عروضه، فعلى هذا يكون الوجود من العوارض الخارجية ويؤيده أنهم اعتبروا في تعريف الحال القيام بالوجود الخارجي ثم جعلوا الوجود من الأحوال كما سبق تحقيقه.
And it was said: If the condition of its abstraction from external things and accidentals, it is found in the mind without doubt; and if the condition of its absolute abstraction, i.e., from both external and mental accidentals together, then it is not found therein, because mental existence is among the accidentals as previously. (And there is consideration in it, for that it) i.e., that a thing (exists in the mind is not among the accidentals. His statement: (as previously that the essence in itself is not existent. His statement: (is not among the mental accidentals) contains a discussion: As for first, because he will state explicitly in the sixth intent that mental accidentals are what occur to a thing by virtue of its existence in the mind, such as essence-ness, accidental-ness, and universality. His statement: (And it was said: if the condition of its abstraction etc.) It was said there is a discussion in it, because this speaker if he intends by external accidentals what attaches to things occurring in external entities, and by mental accidentals what attaches to things subsisting in minds, then he does not prove the impossibility of the existence of the abstracted in the external by what he mentioned, because external being is also among the mental accidentals in this sense, because its addition is in intellection. And if he intends by external accidentals that whose occurrence is according to reality, and by mental accidentals what the mind makes a restriction in them, and considers their occurrence to it without that being according to reality, then it necessitates the impossibility of the existence of the abstracted from external accidentals in the mind as well, because being in the mind is also among the external accidentals in this sense. And it can be said: he intended by external accidentals what does not occur except to the external existent, whether the subject was existent before the occurrence of this accidental or at the time of its occurrence; accordingly, existence is among the external accidentals, and it is supported that they considered in the definition of the state (al-ḥāl) its subsistence by external existence, then they made existence among the states, as previously investigated.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its critical analysis. The objection states that if the essence is abstracted only from external accidentals, it can exist in the mind without doubt; but if abstracted absolutely (from both external and mental accidentals), it cannot exist in the mind because mental existence itself is an accidental. The commentator then says 'there is consideration in it' because existence in the mind is not from the accidentals? The text then discusses the meaning of 'mental accidentals' and 'external accidentals' based on what was previously stated. Two interpretations are examined: (1) If external accidentals are what attach to external entities and mental accidentals are what attach to mental entities, then the objection fails because external being itself would be a mental accidental in that sense. (2) If external accidentals are those that occur in reality and mental accidentals are those that the mind makes as restrictions, then the impossibility of existence of the abstracted in the mind would still follow because being in the mind would be an external accidental. A third possibility is suggested: external accidentals are those that only occur to external existents, making existence itself an external accidental, which is supported by the definition of 'state' (al-ḥāl). Thus the objection is shown to depend on ambiguous definitions.

Key Terms
تجرد (tajarrud)
العوارض الخارجية (al-'awāriḍ al-khārijiyyah)
العوارض الذهنية (al-'awāriḍ al-dhihniyyah)
الوجود الذهني (al-wujūd al-dhihnī)
الحال (al-ḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main focus of the criticism in this passage?
A) The impossibility of the essence's existence in the mind.
B) The definition of 'external accidentals' and 'mental accidentals'.
C) The impossibility of the essence's existence in the external world.
D) The proof that mental existence is not an accidental.

Key Takeaway
The validity of the condition for mental existence depends on the definition of accidentals, and the critique reveals that the objection is not conclusive without clarifying these terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study these logical disputes about definitions, do you ever stop to consider that your own most certain beliefs might rely on unexamined definitions, and that a change in definition could collapse your entire framework? Are you willing to trace your convictions back to their root and test them?
?
You often rely on mental categories to organize your faith and understanding. But have you considered that these categories themselves may be accidental constructions that you have not scrutinized? How would your relationship with Allah change if you realized that even the mental existence of concepts is a matter of debate among scholars? Will you still hold fast to your beliefs with the same certainty?
?
This passage shows that the very condition for something to exist in the mind is disputed. In your own life, you assume that your thoughts and ideas are simply 'there'. But what if the structure of your thoughts is not self-evident? Will you humbly re-examine the foundations of your most cherished ideas, or will you cling to them out of habit?

Allah's Existence

The First School: Quiddities Are Not Created
Al-Jurjani presents the first school's argument and its defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first school's argument that quiddities are absolutely not created, and the logical objection and response that follow.

Core Idea
If quiddities were created, they would cease when creation ceases, which is self-contradictory.

Al-Jurjani begins by stating there are three schools on whether possible quiddities are created. The first school holds they are absolutely not created. The argument is that if quiddities were effects of creation, they would cease when creation ceases, but a thing cannot negate itself. An objection and answer refine this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools
Read the opening sentence to see that there are three positions. This lesson focuses on the first one.
Only the first school is elaborated in the passage.
2
Understand the first school's claim
The first school states quiddities are absolutely not created. Note the example of humanity used to illustrate the argument.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the reasoning: if humanity were created, then without creation it would not be humanity. This is impossible because a thing is necessarily itself.
The argument uses the principle that an effect ceases with its cause.
4
Consider the objection and answer
An objector suggests the non-existence of creation might be impossible. Al-Jurjani answers that if non-existence were impossible, creation would be necessary, which is false. So the objection fails.
This shows the robustness of the first school's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : الماهيات هل هي مجعولة أم لا ]
(الماهيات) الممكنة هل هي مجعولة) بجعل جاعل (أم لا؟ ففيه مذاهب ثلاثة : الأول أنها غير مجعولة مطلقا، سواء كانت بسيطة أو مركبة؛ إذ لو كانت الإنسانية مثلاً ( بجعل جاعل لم تكن الإنسانية عند عدم) جعل (الجاعل إنسانية)؛ لأن ما يكون أثراً للجعل يرتفع بارتفاعه قطعاً، وسلب الشيء عن نفسه
قوله : (إذا لم تجعل الإضافات) أي مطلقاً، .
قوله : ( الماهيات الممكنة إلخ بعد اتفاق الكل على أن الماهيات الممكنة محتاجة كونها موجودة إلى الفاعل، وإلا لم تكن ممكنة اختلفوا في أن الماهيات في حد ذواتها مع قطع النظر عن الوجود وما يتبعه والعدم وما يلزمه أثر للفاعل ومعنى التأثير استتباع المؤثر الأثر حتى لو ارتفع المؤثر ارتفع الأثر بالمرة، فيكون الوجود انتزاعياً محضاً، وإليه ذهب الأشعري والإشراقيون القائلون بعينية الوجود، أم لا بل الماهيات في حد ذواتها ماهيات والتأثير والجعل باعتبار كونها موجودة وما يتبع الوجود، ومعنى التأثير جعل شيء شيئاً، فيكون الاتصاف بالوجود حقيقياً سواء كان موجوداً أو معدوماً ، وإليه ذهب جمهور المتكلمين القائلين بزيادة الوجود، هذا تحرير محل النزاع على ما هو الحق الحقيق بالقبول . قوله مجعولة بجعل جاعل اختاروا هذه العبارة ولم يقولوا : إنها بتأثير المؤثر أو بفعل الفاعل؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ شائعة الاستعمال في الوجود. قوله: (إذ لو كانت الإنسانية إلخ) تصوير للاستدلال الكلي في صورة جزئية للتوضيح. وحاصله : أنه لو كانت الماهيات في ذواتها مجعولة لارتفعت الماهيات بالمرة على تقدير ارتفاع الجعل، ولو كان كذلك لزم أن لا تكون الماهيات في حد ذواتها ماهيات، لكن التالي باطل؛ لأن ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ضروري. وأورد عليه أنه يجوز أن يكون عدم الجعل محالاً مستلزماً للمحال والجواب : أن عدم الجعل ليس ممتنعاً بالذات، وإلا لكان الجعل واجباً بالذات، فنقول : لو كان الجعل ممكناً بالذات لا مكن عدمه نظراً إلى ذاته، ولو أمكن في ذاته لما حكمنا باستلزامه المحال
قوله : (إذا لم تجعل الإضافات أي مطلقاً، وإلا فلا امتناع في ذلك التقسيم بناء على وجودية
بعضها.
The Sixth Objective: Are Quiddities Created or Not?
(Are possible quiddities) created by the creation of a creator, or not? There are three schools: The first: they are absolutely not created, whether simple or composite; for if humanity, for example, were by the creation of a creator, then humanity would not be humanity in the absence of the creator's creation, because what is an effect of creation would definitely cease with its cessation, and the self-negation of a thing...
His saying: 'When the relations are not made': i.e., absolutely.
His saying: 'Possible quiddities, etc.' After all agree that possible quiddities need the agent for their existence, otherwise they would not be possible, they differed concerning whether quiddities in themselves, abstracting from existence and its concomitants and non-existence and its entailments, are an effect of the agent. The meaning of causal efficacy is the effect's following the cause such that if the cause ceased, the effect would entirely cease, making existence purely abstract (intiza'i). This is the view of al-Ash'ari and the Illuminationists who affirm the sameness of existence (ayniat al-wujud). Or rather, quiddities in themselves are quiddities, and the causal efficacy and creation are with respect to their being existent and what follows existence; the meaning of causal efficacy is making a thing a thing, so the attribution of existence is real whether it exists or is non-existent. This is the view of the majority of theologians who affirm the superaddition of existence (ziyadat al-wujud). This is the formulation of the point of contention according to what is true and worthy of acceptance. His saying: 'Created by the creation of a creator' – they chose this expression and did not say 'by the efficacy of the cause' or 'by the act of the agent', because these terms are commonly used for existence. His saying: 'For if humanity, etc.' is a depiction of the universal argument in a particular form for clarification. Its summary: If quiddities in themselves were created, then quiddities would entirely cease upon the supposition of the cessation of creation. If that were so, then quiddities in themselves would not be quiddities, but the consequent is false because the self-affirmation of a thing is necessary. An objection was raised: It is possible that the non-existence of creation is impossible, entailing a contradiction. The answer: The non-existence of creation is not impossible in essence, otherwise creation would be necessary in essence. So we say: If creation were possible in essence, its non-existence would be possible in terms of its essence; and if it were possible in its essence, we would not judge that it entails a contradiction.
His saying: 'When the relations are not made': i.e., absolutely; otherwise there is no impossibility in that division based on the existentiality of some of them.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage addresses the sixth objective: whether possible quiddities are created (maj'ula) by a creator. The author states there are three schools (though only the first is elaborated in this passage). The first opinion is that quiddities are absolutely not created, whether simple or composite, because if humanity were an effect of creation, it would cease to be humanity when creation ceases—a self-negation. The commentary then explains the point of contention: all agree that possible quiddities need an agent for existence, but differ on whether quiddities in themselves (apart from existence and non-existence) are effects. Al-Ash'ari and Illuminationists hold that existence is purely abstract (intiza'i) and quiddities are effects, while the majority of theologians (mutakallimun) hold that quiddities in themselves are not effects; only their existence is, and existence is superadded (za'id). The passage also clarifies why the phrasing 'by the creation of a creator' is used rather than 'by the efficacy of the cause'—to avoid confusion with common terms for existence. An argument is given: if quiddities were created in themselves, then if creation ceased, quiddities would cease, but that is impossible because a thing's self-affirmation is necessary. An objection and answer follow: the objector suggests that the non-existence of creation might be impossible (thus avoiding the entailment), but the answer is that non-existence of creation is not essentially impossible, otherwise creation would be necessary. The last line briefly comments on an earlier division regarding relations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the debate is about whether quiddities exist or not, but it is about whether they are created in themselves, apart from existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that all agree quiddities need an agent for existence; the dispute is about quiddities in themselves being effects.

Checkpoint
What is the core argument of the first school against quiddities being created?
A) If quiddities were created, they would be composite and therefore dependent.
B) If quiddities were created, they would cease when creation ceases, leading to self-negation.
C) If quiddities were created, they would have to be eternal.
D) If quiddities were created, they would be multiple, which is impossible for a simple being.

Key Takeaway
The first school holds that quiddities cannot be created because their creation would entail self-negation, a logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument that if humanity were created, then without creation humanity would not be humanity. Do you truly live as if your very essence depends on God's continual creation? In what ways do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The objection suggests that perhaps creation is necessary and cannot cease. Have you ever treated something in your life as necessary—your health, status, relationships—as if God could not remove it? How does this lesson's response (that such necessity would contradict contingency) challenge your attachments?
?
The principle of self-negation shows that a thing cannot be what it is not. Where in your own life do you find self-contradictions—professing faith but living in doubt, or claiming trust in God while clinging to control? What would it mean to resolve those contradictions in light of this logical clarity?

The Point of Contention: Two Views on Quiddities
Al-Jurjani formulates the point of contention and presents two theological views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main theological positions on whether quiddities in themselves are effects of God, and the meaning of 'creation' in this context.

Core Idea
The dispute is whether quiddities in themselves are effects, or only their existence is.

Al-Jurjani clarifies the precise point of contention: all agree quiddities need an agent for existence, but differ on whether quiddities abstracting from existence are effects. He presents two views: the first, held by al-Ash'ari and the Illuminationists, treats existence as purely abstract and quiddities as effects; the second, held by the majority of theologians, treats quiddities in themselves as non-effects and existence as superadded.

Guided Path
1
Understand the agreed premise
All agree that possible quiddities need a cause for their existence. Read the opening sentence to see this common ground.
2
Digest the first view: quiddities as effects
Al-Ash'ari and the Illuminationists hold that quiddities in themselves are effects, making existence purely abstract (intiza'i). Note what that means.
3
Digest the second view: quiddities as non-effects
The majority (جمهور المتكلمين) hold that quiddities are not effects in themselves; only their existence is created. Existence is superadded (za'id).
4
Note the phrasing choice
Al-Jurjani explains why the author used 'by the creation of a creator' instead of 'by the efficacy of the cause'—to avoid confusion with existence.
This clarifies the terminology.

Uniformity of Simple Bodies and the Role of Accidents
Al-Sharīf responds to an objection about uniformity of simple bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that water and tree are indistinguishable in terms of homogeneous parts is answered by appealing to the role of accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents differentiate uniform simples.

An objector claims that if water and tree are both made of homogeneous atoms, there is no real difference between them. Al-Sharīf responds that accidents entering the simples can account for the distinction. This lesson focuses on that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that since both tree and water are composed of uniform individual atoms, they should be identical. Underline the key premise: homogeneity of parts implies no difference.
2
Examine the answer
Al-Sharīf replies that accidents (a‘rāḍ) can enter and differentiate them. Consider how this preserves the simplicity of the atoms while allowing diversity in composites.
3
Reflect on the significance
This exchange shows how kalam deals with the problem of differentiation in a world of uniform simples. The answer relies on the ontological status of accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لكنها متوافقة الحقيقة قيل : وحينئذ لا فرق بين الشجر والماء، فإن الشجر أيضاً عند من يقول بالجزء ينقسم إلى أجزاء هي جواهر فردة متجانسة وأجيب بجواز دخول الأعراض

واحتياجه إليه فرع الإمكان (وإنه ) أي الإمكان ) لا يعرض للبسيط، فإنه نسبة ) بل كيفيه عارضة لنسبة ) لا تتصور إلا بين شيئين والبسيط لا شيئين فيه، فلا يتصور عروضه له ( وقد اعترض عليه بأنه لو صح ما ذكرتم لم تكن المركبات أيضاً مجعولة؛ لأنه إذا لم تكن البسائط مجعولة لم تكن المركبات مجعولة؛ إذ ليس المركب إلا مجموع البسائط كما مر في مباحث التعريف. فإذا لم يكن شيء من أجزائه حتى الجزء الصوري مجعولاً ، لم يكن المركب أيضاً مجعولاً، ( وأنه يفضي إلى نفي المجعولية بالكلية وأنتم لا تقولون .به ( لا يقال في دفع هذا الاعتراض: ( المجعول انضمامها ) أي انضمام بسائط المركب بعضها إلى بعض، (أو وجودها) أي وجود الماهية المركبة منها ، فلا يلزم مما ذكرناه ارتفاع المجعولية بالكلية؛ لأنا مثلاً، والجسم
عندهم، فالجسم المركب منقسم إلى أجزاء مقدارية غير متشابهة كالعناصر البسيط إلى أجزاء مقدارية متشابهة .
قوله : ( وأنه لا يعرض للبسيط) لا يخفى أنه لو حمل على ظاهره يلزم أن تكون البسائط واجبة فيلزم تعدد الواجب أو ممتنعة فيلزم امتناع وجود المركب أو واسطة فيلزم بطلان الحصر العقلي بين الأمور الثلاثة وسيأتي تحقيقه في تحرير المذاهب .
He said: (But they are uniform in reality) it was said: Then there is no difference between the tree and water, for the tree also, according to the one who affirms the part, divides into parts that are homogeneous individual atoms. And it was answered by the possibility of accidents entering. And its need for it is a branch of contingency. And that (i.e., contingency) does not attach to the simple, for it is a relation, rather it is a quality accident to a relation not conceivable except between two things, and the simple has no two things in it, so its attaching to it is inconceivable. And it was objected against it that if what you mentioned were correct, then composites would not be made either; because if simples are not made, then composites are not made; since a composite is nothing but a sum of simples, as has passed in the discussions of definition. So if nothing of its parts, even the formal part, is made, then the composite also is not made. And it leads to the negation of made-ness entirely, and you do not affirm it. Do not say in repelling this objection: (The made thing is their joining) i.e., the joining of the simples of the composite to each other, (or their existence) i.e., the existence of the composite essence from them, so it does not follow from what we mentioned the removal of made-ness entirely; because we, for example, and the body according to them, so the composite body is divided into quantitative parts not similar, like the elements, and the simple into quantitative parts similar. He said: (And that it does not attach to the simple) it is not hidden that if it is taken literally, it would entail that simples are necessary, entailing multiplicity of the necessary, or impossible, entailing the impossibility of the composite, or intermediate, entailing the invalidity of the rational division between the three things, and its verification will come in the elaboration of the doctrines.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage contains multiple objections and answers. First, an objection that if water is composed of homogeneous atoms, then there is no difference between a tree and water because both are ultimately made of homogeneous atoms. The answer is that accidents may enter, making the tree's parts differ. Then the text discusses contingency (imkān) and asserts that it does not apply to the simple (al-basīṭ), because contingency is a relation requiring two things, whereas the simple has no duality. An objection follows: if simples are not made (majʿūla), then composites also cannot be made, since composites are just sums of simples. The answer is that what is made is either the joining of simples or the existence of the composite essence. Finally, a note on the implication: if taken literally, the claim that contingency does not apply to the simple would force the simples to be either necessary (implying multiple necessary beings) or impossible (implying composites cannot exist) or something else, which is problematic and will be resolved later.

بسيط
أعراض

A) The tree has a different form.
B) Accidents can enter the simples.
C) The water is not actually composed of atoms.
D) The objection is invalid because parts are not uniform.

Key Takeaway
Accidents differentiate composites made of uniform simples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objector assumed uniformity implies identity. Where in your own life do you wrongly assume that superficial sameness negates difference? How does this passage challenge that assumption?
?
Consider one accident—such as anger, or kindness—that enters your soul. Is it accidental to you, or has it become second nature? What does your answer reveal about your self-awareness before Allah?

Why Contingency Does Not Attach to the Simple
Al-Sharīf argues that contingency does not attach to the simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that contingency (imkān) cannot apply to what is simple (basīṭ) because it is a relation requiring two things.

Core Idea
Contingency requires duality; simples have no duality.

The passage turns to the nature of contingency (imkān). It argues that contingency is a relation, and a relation requires two distinct relata. Since the simple (al-basīṭ) has no internal duality, contingency cannot be predicated of it. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts that contingency (imkān) does not attach to the simple (al-basīṭ). Find that claim in the text.
2
Understand the reasoning
Contingency is described as a quality accidental to a relation. A relation only exists between two things. The simple has no two things (lā shay’ayn fīh), so contingency is inconceivable for it.
3
Consider implications
If contingency does not apply to simples, they must be either necessary or impossible. This connects to a broader discussion about modalities.
This will be taken up in later lessons.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لكنها متوافقة الحقيقة قيل : وحينئذ لا فرق بين الشجر والماء، فإن الشجر أيضاً عند من يقول بالجزء ينقسم إلى أجزاء هي جواهر فردة متجانسة وأجيب بجواز دخول الأعراض

واحتياجه إليه فرع الإمكان (وإنه ) أي الإمكان ) لا يعرض للبسيط، فإنه نسبة ) بل كيفيه عارضة لنسبة ) لا تتصور إلا بين شيئين والبسيط لا شيئين فيه، فلا يتصور عروضه له ( وقد اعترض عليه بأنه لو صح ما ذكرتم لم تكن المركبات أيضاً مجعولة؛ لأنه إذا لم تكن البسائط مجعولة لم تكن المركبات مجعولة؛ إذ ليس المركب إلا مجموع البسائط كما مر في مباحث التعريف. فإذا لم يكن شيء من أجزائه حتى الجزء الصوري مجعولاً ، لم يكن المركب أيضاً مجعولاً، ( وأنه يفضي إلى نفي المجعولية بالكلية وأنتم لا تقولون .به ( لا يقال في دفع هذا الاعتراض: ( المجعول انضمامها ) أي انضمام بسائط المركب بعضها إلى بعض، (أو وجودها) أي وجود الماهية المركبة منها ، فلا يلزم مما ذكرناه ارتفاع المجعولية بالكلية؛ لأنا مثلاً، والجسم
عندهم، فالجسم المركب منقسم إلى أجزاء مقدارية غير متشابهة كالعناصر البسيط إلى أجزاء مقدارية متشابهة .
قوله : ( وأنه لا يعرض للبسيط) لا يخفى أنه لو حمل على ظاهره يلزم أن تكون البسائط واجبة فيلزم تعدد الواجب أو ممتنعة فيلزم امتناع وجود المركب أو واسطة فيلزم بطلان الحصر العقلي بين الأمور الثلاثة وسيأتي تحقيقه في تحرير المذاهب .
He said: (But they are uniform in reality) it was said: Then there is no difference between the tree and water, for the tree also, according to the one who affirms the part, divides into parts that are homogeneous individual atoms. And it was answered by the possibility of accidents entering. And its need for it is a branch of contingency. And that (i.e., contingency) does not attach to the simple, for it is a relation, rather it is a quality accident to a relation not conceivable except between two things, and the simple has no two things in it, so its attaching to it is inconceivable. And it was objected against it that if what you mentioned were correct, then composites would not be made either; because if simples are not made, then composites are not made; since a composite is nothing but a sum of simples, as has passed in the discussions of definition. So if nothing of its parts, even the formal part, is made, then the composite also is not made. And it leads to the negation of made-ness entirely, and you do not affirm it. Do not say in repelling this objection: (The made thing is their joining) i.e., the joining of the simples of the composite to each other, (or their existence) i.e., the existence of the composite essence from them, so it does not follow from what we mentioned the removal of made-ness entirely; because we, for example, and the body according to them, so the composite body is divided into quantitative parts not similar, like the elements, and the simple into quantitative parts similar. He said: (And that it does not attach to the simple) it is not hidden that if it is taken literally, it would entail that simples are necessary, entailing multiplicity of the necessary, or impossible, entailing the impossibility of the composite, or intermediate, entailing the invalidity of the rational division between the three things, and its verification will come in the elaboration of the doctrines.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage contains multiple objections and answers. First, an objection that if water is composed of homogeneous atoms, then there is no difference between a tree and water because both are ultimately made of homogeneous atoms. The answer is that accidents may enter, making the tree's parts differ. Then the text discusses contingency (imkān) and asserts that it does not apply to the simple (al-basīṭ), because contingency is a relation requiring two things, whereas the simple has no duality. An objection follows: if simples are not made (majʿūla), then composites also cannot be made, since composites are just sums of simples. The answer is that what is made is either the joining of simples or the existence of the composite essence. Finally, a note on the implication: if taken literally, the claim that contingency does not apply to the simple would force the simples to be either necessary (implying multiple necessary beings) or impossible (implying composites cannot exist) or something else, which is problematic and will be resolved later.

بسيط
نسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency can be applied to simple substances because they are not necessary.
Correction: The passage argues that contingency is a relation requiring two relata, and since the simple has no duality, it cannot receive contingency.

Checkpoint
Why does contingency not attach to the simple?
A) Because the simple is necessary by itself.
B) Because contingency is a relation and the simple has two things in it.
C) Because contingency is a relation requiring two relata, and the simple has no two things.
D) Because the simple is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Contingency is a relation; since the simple lacks duality, it cannot be contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If contingency is a relation, then your very existence is constantly dependent on Allah. In what specific moments today did you unconsciously act as if you were self-sufficient, as if you were 'simple' and not in need of relation to your Creator?
?
The simple has no duality, but your heart is often divided between love of Allah and love of the world. What is one attachment you can sever to move towards the singleness of intention (ikhlāṣ) that mirrors the simplicity of the Necessary Being?
?
This passage teaches that contingency requires two relata. Your existence is contingent: you are not a simple self-cause. How would your daily routine change if you truly internalized that every breath you take is a relation of need to Allah?

If Simples Are Not Made, Can Composites Be Made?
Al-Sharīf answers the objection about composite creation and warns about literal interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if simples are not made (maj‘ūla), then composites cannot be made, and the answer that what is made is the joining or the existence of the composite.

Core Idea
The made thing is the relation or existence of the composite.

A further objection: if simples are not made (maj‘ūla), then composites—which are sums of simples—cannot be made either. Al-Sharīf counters by distinguishing what is made: it is either the joining (indimām) of the simples or the existence (wujūd) of the composite essence. This lesson also notes a potential problematic consequence if the earlier claim is taken literally.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says: if simples are not made, composites cannot be made because composites are just sums of simples. Even the formal part would not be made.
2
Understand the answer
The answer redefines 'made' (maj‘ūl) as either the joining of simples or the existence of the composite essence. This saves the possibility of composite creation.
3
Consider the final remark
The text notes that if the claim 'contingency does not attach to the simple' is taken literally, it leads to problematic consequences. This warns against overextending the argument.
The resolution is deferred to a later section.

The Threefold Division of Accidents
Philosophers' threefold division of accidents relative to mental and external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers divide accidents into three categories based on the possible quiddity's receptivity to mental and external existence and their removal.

Core Idea
Accidents are divided by the existence they attach to: mental, external, or both.

The philosophers begin by establishing a fundamental division of existence itself—mental and external. Then, considering the possible quiddity as receptive to both, they classify the accidents that attach to it into three kinds. This classification is essential for understanding how attributes relate to entities both in the mind and in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two types of existence.
First, read the Arabic passage to recognize that existence is divided into mental and external. This sets the stage for the classification of accidents.
Mental existence is in the mind; external existence is in reality.
2
Note the threefold division of accidents.
Identify the three categories: accidents that belong to both existences, those specific to external existence, and those specific to mental existence.
The passage explicitly states this division.
3
Understand the exceptions for impossibilities and the Necessary Being.
Note that impossibilities (like a partner for God) only have mental accidents, while the Necessary Being (God) only has external accidents. This highlights the scope of the division.
The commentary explains these exceptions.
4
Reflect on why this division matters for theology.
Consider that understanding how accidents relate to existence clarifies debates about divine attributes and the nature of reality.
This connects to broader theological discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسموا الوجود إلى ذهني وخارجي، وجعلو (الماهية الممكنة قابلة لهما ولرفعهما رأوا العوارض) أي الأمور التي تعرض لتلك الماهية ) ثلاثة أقسام: قسم لهما بمعنى .

قوله: (وجعلوا أي اعتقدوا كما في قوله تعالى : وجعلوا لله شركاء الجن ﴾ [الأنعام: 100] ...

قوله : ( الماهية الممكنة قابلة لهما وأما الممتنعات فلعدم قبولها الوجود الخارجي لا يكون لها إلا العوارض الذهنية، ولذا الواجب لاقتضائه الوجود الخارجي لا يكون له إلا العوارض الخارجية، وأما العوارض التي تلحقه في الذهن، فباعتبار أنه من حيث الوجود الذهني ممكن؛ إذ يجوز أن يحصل فيه وأن لا يحصل.

قوله: (ولرفعهما) إنما اعتبر قبولها لرفع الوجودين ليظهر اختصاص بعض العوارض بالوجود الخارجي، وبعضها بالوجود الذهني .

قوله : (أي الأمور التي تعرض إلخ) أي ليس المراد بالعارض الخارج المحمول بل ما يعرضه ويلحقه. الشارح قدس سره سواء وجدت الأربعة في الخارج أو في الذهن وصرح به في شرح التجريد وليس المراد به مفهوم الوجود ولا الوجود من غير اعتبار خصوصية معه، حتى لا تنحصر القسمة فتدبر. ثم اعلم أنه إن أريد بمدخلية الوجود المطلق أو الخارجي أو الذهني في العروض أن يكون ذلك شرطاً فيه، فالوجود المطلق وكذا الخارجي والذهني خارج من الأقسام الثلاثة؛ إذ قيام الوجود إنما هو بالماهية من حيث هي على ما نص عليه في التجريد ،وغيره لا بشرط الوجود، وإلا لزم تقدم الوجود على الوجود وإن أريد به أن يكون ظرفاً له ومصححاً لعروضه فالوجود داخل في القسم الثالث، لأن الاتصاف بالوجود وإن لم يستدع حينئذ تقدم المعروض بالوجود، لكنه يقتضي أن لا يكون المعروض مخلوطاً بذلك العارض في ذلك الظرف، وظاهر أن الماهية في الوجود الخارجي مخلوطة بالوجود الخارجي، وكذا في الوجود في نفس الأمر مخلوطة به بحسب نفس الأمر، وكذا في الوجود الذهني مخلوطة به بحسب نفس الأمر، لكن للعقل أن يأخذها غير مخلوطة بشيء من العوارض فهو في هذا الاعتبار معرى عن جميع العوارض حتى عن هذا الاعتبار، فهذا النحو من الوجود ظرف للاتصاف به، وهو نحو من أنحاء الوجود في نفس الأمر. كذا أفاده المحقق الدواني. وهذا على ما اختاره من أن ثبوت الشيء للشيء مستلزم لثبوت المثبت له وأما على ما هو المشهور من الفرعية فنقول : اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ليس اتصافاً حقيقياً، فإن زيادة الوجود خارجياً كان أو ذهنياً إنما هو في التصور، فهو انتزاعي محض، فإذا لاحظها العقل وانتزع منها الوجود ووصفها به كان ذلك فرعا لحصولها في الذهن بوجود هو نفسها، ثم إذا لاحظها مرة ثانية وانتزع منها وجوداً ذهنياً ووصفها به كان ذلك فرعاً لحصولها في الذهن مرة ثالثة بوجود هو نفسها وهكذا وليس هذه الملاحظة والالتفات لازمة للنفس فتنقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار والملاحظة، وهذا تحقيق ما ذكره صاحب التجريد من أن الوجود من المعقولات الثانية. وبما حررنا لك يندفع الشكوك التي عرضت للناظرين في هذا المقام لا نطول الكلام بذكرها ودفعها، فإنك بعد الاحاطة بما ذكرنا يظهر لك جلية الحال من غير حاجة إلى القيل والقال .
They divided existence into mental and external, and they considered the possible quiddity to be receptive to both and to their removal, so they saw that accidents – i.e., the things that attach to that quiddity – are three divisions: a division that is for both, meaning...

His statement: (They made, i.e., they believed, as in His statement: 'And they made for Allah partners among the jinn' [Sūrat al-An‘ām: 100]...)

His statement: (The possible quiddity is receptive to both. As for impossibilities, due to their not accepting external existence, they only have mental accidents. Likewise the Necessary Being, because it necessitates external existence, only has external accidents. As for the accidents that attach to it in the mind, that is because in terms of mental existence it is possible, since it may or may not occur in it.)

His statement: (and to their removal) It only considered its receptivity to the removal of the two existences so that the specificity of some accidents to external existence and others to mental existence would appear.

His statement: (i.e., the things that attach etc.) That is, what is meant by 'accident' is not the external predicate, but rather what attaches and follows it. The commentator (may God sanctify his soul) said: whether the four are found in external existence or in the mind, and he stated it explicitly in his commentary on al-Tajrid. What is meant is not the concept of existence nor existence without considering a particularity with it, so that the division would not be limited. So ponder. Then know: If it is intended that the involvement of absolute existence or external or mental existence in the attachment is a condition for it, then absolute existence, and likewise external and mental existence, are outside the three divisions; because the standing of existence is only with the quiddity as such, as stated in al-Tajrid and elsewhere, not under the condition of existence, otherwise the priority of existence over existence would be necessary. And if it is intended that it is a locus for it and a rectifier of its attachment, then existence is included in the third division, because the attribution of existence, although it does not then require the priority of the subject with existence, it does require that the subject not be mixed with that accident in that locus. It is apparent that the quiddity in external existence is mixed with external existence, and likewise in the actual state it is mixed with it in relation to the actual state, and likewise in mental existence it is mixed with it in relation to the actual state, but the mind can take it unmixed with any accident, so in this consideration it is stripped of all accidents, even of this consideration. So this kind of existence is a locus for the attribution of it, and it is one of the kinds of existence in the actual state. Thus spoke the investigator al-Dawānī. This is according to his choice that the establishment of a thing for a thing necessitates the establishment of that which is established for it. As for the well-known view of subordination, we say: The attribution of quiddity to existence is not a real attribution, for the addition of existence, whether external or mental, is only in mental conception; it is pure intellection. So when the mind observes it and abstracts existence from it and describes it with it, that is subsequent to its occurrence in the mind with an existence that is itself. Then when it observes it a second time and abstracts a mental existence from it and describes it with it, that is subsequent to its occurrence in the mind a third time with an existence that is itself, and so on. This observation and attention are not necessary for the soul; they cease with the cessation of consideration and observation. This is a verification of what the author of al-Tajrid mentioned: that existence is one of the secondary intelligibles. By what we have written for you, the doubts that have occurred to the investigators in this topic are repelled. We do not lengthen the speech by mentioning and repelling them, for after comprehending what we have mentioned, the clarity of the situation appears to you without need for argument and counterargument.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanatory discussion. The commentator presents the philosophers' division of existence into mental and external, and their identification of three categories of accidents that attach to the possible quiddity based on its receptivity to both kinds of existence and their removal. The first category is for both existences (i.e., common accidents like universality), the second for external existence only (e.g., being in a place), and the third for mental existence only (e.g., being abstract). The commentator clarifies that impossibilities have only mental accidents, and the Necessary Being has only external accidents. He then discusses the role of existence in accidental attribution: if existence is considered a condition, then existence itself falls outside the three divisions because existence stands with the quiddity as such; if it is considered a locus for attribution, then existence falls within the third division because the quiddity in external existence is mixed with external existence, but the mind can abstract it. The commentary also engages with al-Dawānī's view on the issue of existence being a secondary intelligible and the subordination theory. The passage concludes by asserting that this analysis dispels doubts.

Key Terms
وجود ذهني (wujūdh dhihnī)
وجود خارجي (wujūd khārijī)
عوارض (a‘rāḍ)
ماهية (māhiyya)
ممكنة (mumkina)
ممتنعات (mumtani‘āt)
واجب (wājib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence itself is an accident falling under the three divisions.
Correction: The passage implies that existence is not an accident but a division of being; accidents attach to the quiddity, not to existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for dividing accidents into three categories?
A) Whether they are mental or external.
B) The quiddity's ability to receive both mental and external existence and their removal.
C) The quiddity's simplicity or composition.
D) Whether they are accidents of identity or quiddity.

Key Takeaway
The threefold division of accidents based on the possible quiddity's receptivity to existence is foundational for analyzing attributes in theology and philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that accidents can be mental or external. In your own life, are you more attached to mental constructs about Allah's attributes or the external reality of His existence? How does this affect your worship and reliance on Him?
?
The passage states that the Necessary Being only has external accidents. How does this truth challenge any assumption you held that God fits human categories of thought? What must you change about how you think about Allah?
?
Reflect on your concept of 'possible quiddity'—are you living as if your existence is merely possible and contingent? What would it mean to truly live in constant awareness that only Allah is necessary, and how would that change your daily priorities?

Existence as Condition or Locus for Accidents
Analysis of existence as condition or locus for accidental attribution and its relation to the threefold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the role of existence (as condition or locus) determines whether existence itself falls inside or outside the threefold division of accidents.

Core Idea
Existence is either a condition or a locus for accidents; this changes its place in the division.

The passage now turns to a deeper analysis: is existence a condition for the attachment of accidents, or is it a locus (container) for them? The answer affects whether existence itself is included in one of the three accident categories, and it leads into discussions about the nature of existence as a secondary intelligible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Focus on the two interpretations of 'involvement of existence' in accidental attachment: as a condition or as a locus.
The passage uses terms like ٍشرط (sharṭ) and ظرف (ẓarf).
2
Identify the two scenarios.
First, if existence is a condition, then existence itself is outside the three divisions. Second, if existence is a locus, then it falls into the third division.
The passage argues each case.
3
Understand the implications for mental and external existence.
Consider how in external existence, the quiddity is mixed with existence, but the mind can abstract it. That abstraction creates a locus (the mental existence) for attribution.
This connects to the idea of secondary intelligibles.
4
Reflect on the resolution of doubts.
The passage concludes that this analysis dispels doubts, showing existence as a secondary intelligible and not a real predicate.
This is a key theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسموا الوجود إلى ذهني وخارجي، وجعلو (الماهية الممكنة قابلة لهما ولرفعهما رأوا العوارض) أي الأمور التي تعرض لتلك الماهية ) ثلاثة أقسام: قسم لهما بمعنى .

قوله: (وجعلوا أي اعتقدوا كما في قوله تعالى : وجعلوا لله شركاء الجن ﴾ [الأنعام: 100] ...

قوله : ( الماهية الممكنة قابلة لهما وأما الممتنعات فلعدم قبولها الوجود الخارجي لا يكون لها إلا العوارض الذهنية، ولذا الواجب لاقتضائه الوجود الخارجي لا يكون له إلا العوارض الخارجية، وأما العوارض التي تلحقه في الذهن، فباعتبار أنه من حيث الوجود الذهني ممكن؛ إذ يجوز أن يحصل فيه وأن لا يحصل.

قوله: (ولرفعهما) إنما اعتبر قبولها لرفع الوجودين ليظهر اختصاص بعض العوارض بالوجود الخارجي، وبعضها بالوجود الذهني .

قوله : (أي الأمور التي تعرض إلخ) أي ليس المراد بالعارض الخارج المحمول بل ما يعرضه ويلحقه. الشارح قدس سره سواء وجدت الأربعة في الخارج أو في الذهن وصرح به في شرح التجريد وليس المراد به مفهوم الوجود ولا الوجود من غير اعتبار خصوصية معه، حتى لا تنحصر القسمة فتدبر. ثم اعلم أنه إن أريد بمدخلية الوجود المطلق أو الخارجي أو الذهني في العروض أن يكون ذلك شرطاً فيه، فالوجود المطلق وكذا الخارجي والذهني خارج من الأقسام الثلاثة؛ إذ قيام الوجود إنما هو بالماهية من حيث هي على ما نص عليه في التجريد ،وغيره لا بشرط الوجود، وإلا لزم تقدم الوجود على الوجود وإن أريد به أن يكون ظرفاً له ومصححاً لعروضه فالوجود داخل في القسم الثالث، لأن الاتصاف بالوجود وإن لم يستدع حينئذ تقدم المعروض بالوجود، لكنه يقتضي أن لا يكون المعروض مخلوطاً بذلك العارض في ذلك الظرف، وظاهر أن الماهية في الوجود الخارجي مخلوطة بالوجود الخارجي، وكذا في الوجود في نفس الأمر مخلوطة به بحسب نفس الأمر، وكذا في الوجود الذهني مخلوطة به بحسب نفس الأمر، لكن للعقل أن يأخذها غير مخلوطة بشيء من العوارض فهو في هذا الاعتبار معرى عن جميع العوارض حتى عن هذا الاعتبار، فهذا النحو من الوجود ظرف للاتصاف به، وهو نحو من أنحاء الوجود في نفس الأمر. كذا أفاده المحقق الدواني. وهذا على ما اختاره من أن ثبوت الشيء للشيء مستلزم لثبوت المثبت له وأما على ما هو المشهور من الفرعية فنقول : اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ليس اتصافاً حقيقياً، فإن زيادة الوجود خارجياً كان أو ذهنياً إنما هو في التصور، فهو انتزاعي محض، فإذا لاحظها العقل وانتزع منها الوجود ووصفها به كان ذلك فرعا لحصولها في الذهن بوجود هو نفسها، ثم إذا لاحظها مرة ثانية وانتزع منها وجوداً ذهنياً ووصفها به كان ذلك فرعاً لحصولها في الذهن مرة ثالثة بوجود هو نفسها وهكذا وليس هذه الملاحظة والالتفات لازمة للنفس فتنقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار والملاحظة، وهذا تحقيق ما ذكره صاحب التجريد من أن الوجود من المعقولات الثانية. وبما حررنا لك يندفع الشكوك التي عرضت للناظرين في هذا المقام لا نطول الكلام بذكرها ودفعها، فإنك بعد الاحاطة بما ذكرنا يظهر لك جلية الحال من غير حاجة إلى القيل والقال .
They divided existence into mental and external, and they considered the possible quiddity to be receptive to both and to their removal, so they saw that accidents – i.e., the things that attach to that quiddity – are three divisions: a division that is for both, meaning...

His statement: (They made, i.e., they believed, as in His statement: 'And they made for Allah partners among the jinn' [Sūrat al-An‘ām: 100]...)

His statement: (The possible quiddity is receptive to both. As for impossibilities, due to their not accepting external existence, they only have mental accidents. Likewise the Necessary Being, because it necessitates external existence, only has external accidents. As for the accidents that attach to it in the mind, that is because in terms of mental existence it is possible, since it may or may not occur in it.)

His statement: (and to their removal) It only considered its receptivity to the removal of the two existences so that the specificity of some accidents to external existence and others to mental existence would appear.

His statement: (i.e., the things that attach etc.) That is, what is meant by 'accident' is not the external predicate, but rather what attaches and follows it. The commentator (may God sanctify his soul) said: whether the four are found in external existence or in the mind, and he stated it explicitly in his commentary on al-Tajrid. What is meant is not the concept of existence nor existence without considering a particularity with it, so that the division would not be limited. So ponder. Then know: If it is intended that the involvement of absolute existence or external or mental existence in the attachment is a condition for it, then absolute existence, and likewise external and mental existence, are outside the three divisions; because the standing of existence is only with the quiddity as such, as stated in al-Tajrid and elsewhere, not under the condition of existence, otherwise the priority of existence over existence would be necessary. And if it is intended that it is a locus for it and a rectifier of its attachment, then existence is included in the third division, because the attribution of existence, although it does not then require the priority of the subject with existence, it does require that the subject not be mixed with that accident in that locus. It is apparent that the quiddity in external existence is mixed with external existence, and likewise in the actual state it is mixed with it in relation to the actual state, and likewise in mental existence it is mixed with it in relation to the actual state, but the mind can take it unmixed with any accident, so in this consideration it is stripped of all accidents, even of this consideration. So this kind of existence is a locus for the attribution of it, and it is one of the kinds of existence in the actual state. Thus spoke the investigator al-Dawānī. This is according to his choice that the establishment of a thing for a thing necessitates the establishment of that which is established for it. As for the well-known view of subordination, we say: The attribution of quiddity to existence is not a real attribution, for the addition of existence, whether external or mental, is only in mental conception; it is pure intellection. So when the mind observes it and abstracts existence from it and describes it with it, that is subsequent to its occurrence in the mind with an existence that is itself. Then when it observes it a second time and abstracts a mental existence from it and describes it with it, that is subsequent to its occurrence in the mind a third time with an existence that is itself, and so on. This observation and attention are not necessary for the soul; they cease with the cessation of consideration and observation. This is a verification of what the author of al-Tajrid mentioned: that existence is one of the secondary intelligibles. By what we have written for you, the doubts that have occurred to the investigators in this topic are repelled. We do not lengthen the speech by mentioning and repelling them, for after comprehending what we have mentioned, the clarity of the situation appears to you without need for argument and counterargument.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanatory discussion. The commentator presents the philosophers' division of existence into mental and external, and their identification of three categories of accidents that attach to the possible quiddity based on its receptivity to both kinds of existence and their removal. The first category is for both existences (i.e., common accidents like universality), the second for external existence only (e.g., being in a place), and the third for mental existence only (e.g., being abstract). The commentator clarifies that impossibilities have only mental accidents, and the Necessary Being has only external accidents. He then discusses the role of existence in accidental attribution: if existence is considered a condition, then existence itself falls outside the three divisions because existence stands with the quiddity as such; if it is considered a locus for attribution, then existence falls within the third division because the quiddity in external existence is mixed with external existence, but the mind can abstract it. The commentary also engages with al-Dawānī's view on the issue of existence being a secondary intelligible and the subordination theory. The passage concludes by asserting that this analysis dispels doubts.

معقولات ثانية (ma‘qūlāt thāniya)
وجود (wujūd)
ماهية (māhiyya)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that existence is a real predicate added to quiddity externally.
Correction: The passage argues that existence is a secondary intelligible abstracted by the mind, not a real accident.

Checkpoint

A) Outside the division.
B) In the first division (common to both).

D) In the second division (specific to external existence).

Key Takeaway
The role of existence (condition or locus) determines its place in the accident classification, ultimately leading to the view that existence is a secondary intelligible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If this is true, then what is the real ground of your accountability before Allah? Is your existence merely a condition for your actions, or is it the locus where divine commands meet you? Examine your answer, and let it change how you perform the next prayer.
?
The passage shows that the mind can strip away all accidents, even the accident of existence, from quiddity. Have you ever stripped away your own accidental attributes—your wealth, status, health—to see your bare quiddity? If you did, would you find anything that is truly yours? This lesson forces you to ask: what can you present to Allah that is not borrowed from Him? Resolve to identify one attribute you have been taking credit for and repent of that assumption today.

Why Matter is Individuated by Form, Not Essence
Commentary on why matter's individuation is through form, not essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why medieval Islamic philosophers held that the individuation of matter is caused by form, not by its essence, and how the absolute form serves as a condition for the agent's influence.

Core Idea
Form individualizes matter not by being specific, but by being a condition for the agent's effect.

This passage from the commentary on a kalam work addresses a key metaphysical question: What makes a piece of matter this particular piece? The commentator explains that matter is not individuated by its own essence, but by the form that inheres in it, drawing on arguments from al-Razi and al-Muhaqqiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms: تشخص (individuation), هيولى (matter), صورة (form), ماهية (essence).
2
Identify the main claim
The commentator states that matter's individuation is caused by form, not essence. Then he quotes authorities to refine this: form is needed as a condition, but it is not the particularizer; the particularizer is the essence.
The distinction between form as condition and form as particularizer is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخص واحد، بل تعينه عندهم بصورته؛ فإن تشخص الهيولى معلل عندهم بالصورة الحالة فيها لا بماهية الهيولى. ومن هاهنا يظهر جواز تشخص الماهية بما يحل فيها، وقد بنوا دليلهم على عدم جوازه) وإن كان تشخص القابل بما حل فيه لزم. إذ يكفي حينئذ أن يقال: إن كان تشخصه بماهيته فهم لا يقولون به. قوله: (معلل بالصورة (الحالة قال المحقق في شرح الإشارات: الهيولى إنما تصير هذه الهيولى بعينها لأجل صورة تعينها لا من حيث إنها هذه الصورة بل من حيث إنها صورة ما. وتفصيله ما قاله الإمام في المباحث المشرقية: المؤثر في وجود الهيولى المعينة هو وجود المفارق وهو شيء معين الذات مثل تعين ذات الهيولى المعينة، وأما الصورة فإنها كما عرفت شرائط لوصول تأثير المفارق، والحاجة إلى الصورة ليست من حيث هي تلك الصورة، بل من حيث إنها صورة ما. والمعلول المعين الشخصي وإن كان يستدعي علة معينة شخصية، ولكن لا يستدعي أن تكون شرائط التأثير أموراً بأعيانها. انتهى. وبما نقلنا ظهر أن الصورة المطلقة شريكة فاعل الهيولى المعينة، وأنها معتبرة في جانب الفاعل وليست مخصصة للهيولى تعين دون آخر، لأن الصورة المطلقة لا دخل لها في التخصيص، وكلامنا في العلة المخصصة. بل مخصصها بتعين دون آخر ذاتها، وأن هيولى كل فلك وهيولى العناصر نوعها منحصر في فرد. فاندفع إيراد بعض الفضلاء بالنظر إلى نفس الهيولى، وأما بالنظر إلى تعدد أشخاصها باعتبار تبعضها، وحصصها باعتبار أشخاص الأجسام العنصرية وباعتبار أشخاص خوارج المراكز والتداوير والكواكب، فيسيجيء أنه باعتبار العوارض المكتنفة بها. وكذا اندفع ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره بقوله: ومن هاهنا يظهر جواز إلخ لأن علية الصورة من حيث ذاتها لا من حيث حلولها، ولأنها ليست مخصصة والكلام في المخصص.
It is a single individual, but rather its determination according to them is through its form; for the individuation of matter is caused according to them by the form that inheres in it, not by the essence of matter. From here appears the permissibility of individuation of essence by what inheres in it, and they based their proof on its impermissibility. And if the individuation of the receiver by what inheres in it is necessary, then it suffices to say: If its individuation is by its essence, they do not say so. His statement: (Caused by the form (the inhering) said the muhaqqiq in his commentary on al-Isharat: Matter only becomes this very matter because of a form that individuates it, not insofar as it is this form but insofar as it is some form. Its detail is what the Imam said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: The effective cause in the existence of individuated matter is the existence of the separate being, which is something individuated in essence like the individuation of the essence of individuated matter. As for form, as you know, it is a condition for the arrival of the effect of the separate being, and the need for form is not insofar as it is that specific form but insofar as it is some form. The individuated individual effect, even if it requires an individual specific cause, does not require that the conditions of effect be exactly the same things. End. And from what we have quoted, it appears that the absolute form is a partner to the agent of individuated matter, and that it is considered on the side of the agent and is not a particularizer of matter for a particular determination over another, because the absolute form has no role in particularization, and our speech is about the particularizing cause. Rather, its particularizer for a particular determination over another is its essence, and the matter of each sphere and the matter of the elements, its type is confined to one individual. Thus the objection of some virtuous people is repelled regarding the matter itself. As for regarding the multiplicity of its individuals by virtue of their division, and its portions by virtue of individuals of elemental bodies and by virtue of individuals of eccentric spheres, epicycles, and planets, it will come that it is by virtue of the surrounding accidents. Similarly, what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) said in his statement: 'And from here appears the permissibility etc.' is repelled because causation of the form is from its essence, not from its inherence, and because it is not a particularizer, and the speech is about the particularizer.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn that states that the determination of matter is through form, not essence. The commentator begins by confirming that the individuation of matter (hayula) is caused by the form inhering in it, not by its essence. He then quotes al-Muhaqqiq (likely Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) from his commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat, explaining that matter becomes this specific matter because of a form that individuates it, but the form is considered only insofar as it is some form, not this particular form. Then he quotes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: the efficient cause of individuated matter is a separate being that is itself individuated (like intellects), while form is a condition for the arrival of that influence. The need for form is generic, not specific. Though the individual effect requires an individual cause, it does not require that the conditions of influence be specific individuals. The commentator concludes that the absolute form (al-sura al-mutlaqa) is a partner to the agent of individuated matter, but it is not the particularizer. The particularizer that assigns determination is the essence (dhat) of the matter itself, and each type of matter (e.g., of spheres, elements) is confined to one individual. This repels an objection concerning the matter itself. Regarding the multiplicity of individuals within elemental bodies and celestial spheres, that depends on surrounding accidents. The comment by the commentator (Sharh) about permissibility is also repelled because causation belongs to the form's essence, not its inherence, and because it is not a particularizer.

ماهية
علة مخصصة
صورة مطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Form alone individualizes matter.
Correction: Form only conditions the agent's influence; the essence itself is the particularizer.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the role of the absolute form in the individuation of matter?
A) It is the sole cause of individuation.
B) It is a partner to the agent of individuated matter, but not the particularizer.
C) It has no role in individuation.
D) It determines matter as a specific individual.

Key Takeaway
The individuation of matter is caused by form as a condition, not by the form's specificity, and the ultimate particularizer is the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You credit your appearance, status, or circumstances (forms) for defining who you are. After reading this lesson, what would it mean to instead see your essence as the true particularizer? How can you shift your focus from external forms to your inner reality today?
?
Consider a habit or belief you attribute to your environment or upbringing (a 'form'). Could it be that your essence, not these conditions, truly determines your response? What would change if you acted from that conviction?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the agent (the separate being) and the condition (form). In your worship, do you sometimes confuse the means with the source? How can this lesson refine your tawheed by pushing you to attribute all effect to Allah alone, not to the forms He created?
