Wujub as a Single Attribute and the Objection of Its Cessation
Author states wujub is a single attribute and answers the objection of its possible cessation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the view that wujub is a single attribute by distinguishing between possibility in itself and impossibility due to the essence of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Wujub is possible in itself but impossible to cease due to the essence of the Necessary.

The passage presents a precise theological argument about the attribute of wujub (necessity). The author states that wujub is a single attribute, not a cause-effect relation. Then he anticipates an objection: if wujub is possible, it could cease, making the Necessary not necessary. His answer hinges on a distinction between two perspectives. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the terms wujub and wajibiyya.
Notice the structure: claim, objection, response.
2
Identify the author's position
The author says wujub is a single attribute. Understand that he distinguishes between wujub and wajibiyya (necessity-hood).
This is important for those who believe in states (al-ahwal).
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues that if wujub is possible (mumkin), its cessation is possible, leading to absurdity. Grasp why this is a problem.
4
Analyze the answer
The author replies by distinguishing between possibility with respect to its own essence and impossibility with respect to the essence of the Necessary. See how this resolves the objection.
The key is that the essence of the Necessary prevents the cessation of wujub.
5
Reflect on the nuance
Consider how this argument shows that possibility is not absolute but can be conditioned by another factor.
This is a common move in kalam: distinguishing between perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجوب ) صفة ( واحدة ) عندنا، فليس ثمة علة ) هي الوجوب ( ولا معلول ) هو الواجبية، نعم هذا لازم للقائل بالحال لأن الواجبية عنده صفة معللة بالوجوب، فإنه إذا قام الوجوب بذات أوجب لها الواجبية، فإن قلت لنا أن نقول إذا كان الوجوب ممكناً جاز زواله، فإذا فرض وقوع هذا الجائز لخلا الواجب عن صفة الوجوب فلا يكون واجباً، وهو محال . قلت : إذا كان الوجوب ممكناً جاز زواله نظراً إلى ذاته، لكنه يمتنع نظراً إلى ذات الواجب فيستحيل خلوه عنه فلا محذور. قوله : ( نظراً إلى ذات الواجب) بناء على كونه علة لوجود الوجوب
And wujub (necessity) is a single attribute according to us, so there is no cause which is wujub and no effect which is wajibiyya (necessity-hood). Yes, this is necessary for the proponent of al-ahwal (states) because for him wajibiyya is an attribute caused by wujub; for if wujub inheres in an essence, it necessitates for it wajibiyya. For if you say to us: we say if wujub is possible, its cessation is possible, and if this possible is assumed to occur, then the necessary being would be devoid of the attribute of wujub, so it would not be necessary, and that is absurd. I say: if wujub is possible, its cessation is possible with respect to its own essence, but it is impossible with respect to the essence of the Necessary, so its being devoid of it is impossible, so there is no absurdity. He said: (with respect to the essence of the Necessary) based on it being a cause for the existence of wujub.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the author's position that wujub is a single attribute, not a cause-effect relation. This is necessary for the proponent of states (al-ahwal) because they believe wajibiyya is an attribute caused by wujub. Then an objection is presented: if wujub is possible, it could cease, leading to the necessary being lacking wujub and thus not being necessary. The answer distinguishes between possibility with regard to the essence of wujub itself and impossibility with regard to the essence of the Necessary Being, which necessitates its own wujub. The gloss clarifies that the essence of the Necessary is the cause of the existence of wujub, so wujub's cessation is impossible due to that essence.

Key Terms

Correction: Wujub is possible in itself, but its cessation is impossible due to the essence of the Necessary Being, which necessitates its existence.

C) He denies that wujub is possible at all.

Key Takeaway
Wujub is possible in itself but its cessation is impossible because the essence of the Necessary Being necessitates it.

How would your reliance on Allah change if you constantly remembered that nothing—not even your worship's validity—is independent of His will?
?

Does your daily routine reflect that reality, or do you live as if Allah could be optional? What one concrete change can you make today to align your life with the fact that Allah is the only Necessary Being, and everything else is contingent on Him?

Refined Objection on Cessation of Wujub
Author presents and answers a refined objection on wujub cessation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a refined objection about the cessation of wujub and the critical distinction between annihilation and absolute non-existence that resolves it.

Core Idea
Cessation of wujub can mean two different things, and answering the objection depends on which meaning is intended.

This passage advances a more sophisticated objection: even if one instance of wujub ceases, another could arise, so Allah is never without wujub. The answer requires clarifying what is meant by 'cessation' — whether it is the annihilation of a real entity or simply the absence of a non-entity. The author navigates this distinction to preserve the integrity of the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial counter-response
Start with the objector's claim and the author's first reply. Notice how the objector proposes substitution of instances, and the author counters that all instances are possible and could cease, also noting the problem of loci for accidents.
The objector is trying to avoid the consequence of Allah being without wujub.
2
Examine the discussion (بحث) on the meaning of 'cessation'
The author introduces a discussion that distinguishes two interpretations: cessation as annihilation after existence, and cessation as absolute non-existence. Understand each interpretation and its implications.
This distinction is crucial for the logical flow.
3
Understand the choice of interpretation
The author chooses the second interpretation because the discussion assumes wujub is a real entity. Real entities cannot qualify a subject if they are non-existent. Appreciate the analogy of white by non-existent whiteness.
The author argues that the first interpretation is not applicable because some possible things cannot cease after existing.
4
Reinforce with the gloss about essence
The gloss explains that the essence of the Necessary necessitates the existence of its wujub, so cessation is impossible due to that essence. This shows that even if wujub is possible in itself, it is not contingent in this context.
This ties back to the broader argument about divine simplicity and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: لا نسلم لزوم خلو الواجب عن الوجوب على تقدير زواله لجواز أن يزول فرد من الوجوب، ويجيء فرد آخر. قلت : جميع الأفراد ممكنة فيمكن زوال الجميع، ويلزم الخلو، وأيضاً يلزم فيما ذكر كون الواجب تعالى محلا للحوادث. وهاهنا بحث لأنه إن أراد بزوال الوجوب على تقدير إمكانه انعدامه بعد كونه موجوداً في الأعيان، فلا نسلم أنه لو كان ممكناً لجاز زواله بهذا المعنى، فإن من الممكنات ما يستحيل عدمه بعد ،وجوده كالزمان على ما سيأتي. وإن أراد بزوال الوجوب عدمه مطلقاً، فلا نسلم لزوم خلو الواجب عن الوجوب . فإن عدم صفة الوجوب في نفسها لا يستلزم عدم اتصاف الذات بها، فإن الصفات قد تكون عدمية مع اتصاف الموصوفات بها في نفس الأمر، بل في الخارج أيضاً على ما سيذكره نعم عدمها بعد كونها موجودة يستلزم ذلك، والجواب اختيار الثاني فإن الكلام على تقدير كون الوجوب من الأمور العينية لا من الأمور الاعتبارية، ولا شك أن الأمور العينية إذا كانت معدومة لا يمكن اتصاف المحل الموجود بها، ولو جوزنا ذلك لزمنا أن نجوز كون الجسم أبيض بالبياض المعدوم، وذلك سفسطة ظاهرة البطلان . قوله : ( لكنه يمتنع نظراً إلى ذات الواجب إلخ) تحقيقه أن ذات الواجب كما يقتضي وجود نفسه يقتضي وجود وجوبه الموجود فرضاً، فالوجوب وإن كان جائزاً لزوال بالنظر إلى ذات الوجود لكونه ممكناً بالذات لكنه ممتنع الزوال نظراً إلى ذات الواجب، فلا يلزم جواز خلو الذات عن الوجوب المستحيل وإنما يلزم لو لم يقتض ذات الواجب وجود الوجوب .
For if you say: We do not accept that the necessary being must become devoid of wujub on the supposition of its cessation, because it is possible that one individual instance of wujub ceases and another instance comes. I say: All instances are possible, so it is possible for all to cease, and absence becomes necessary; also, this would entail that the Necessary Most High is a locus for contingent occurrences. And here there is a discussion: if by 'cessation of wujub' on the supposition of its possibility he means its annihilation after it had existed in external entities, then we do not accept that if it were possible, its cessation in this sense would be permissible, for some possible things cannot cease after existing, such as time as will come. And if by 'cessation of wujub' he means its absolute non-existence, then we do not accept the necessary entailment of the necessary being being devoid of wujub; for the non-existence of the attribute wujub in itself does not entail the lack of being qualified by it on the part of the subject, because attributes can be privative while subjects are qualified by them in actual fact, even externally, as he will mention. Yes, its non-existence after it had existed does entail that. And the answer is to choose the second [interpretation], because the discussion is on the supposition that wujub is among real things, not among mental considerations; and there is no doubt that real things, if they are non-existent, it is impossible for an existing subject to be qualified by them; and if we were to permit that, we would have to permit a body being white by non-existent whiteness, and that is manifestly sophistry. He said: (but it is impossible with respect to the essence of the Necessary, etc.) Its verification is that the essence of the Necessary, just as it necessitates its own existence, necessitates the existence of its wujub assuming it exists; so wujub, even though it is possible of cessation with respect to the essence of existence because it is possible in itself, yet its cessation is impossible with respect to the essence of the Necessary; so the permissibility of the essence being devoid of wujub does not follow, which is absurd; rather it would follow only if the essence of the Necessary did not necessitate the existence of wujub.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a more detailed objection and answer. The objector argues that even if one instance of wujub ceases, another could come, so the necessary being is not necessarily left without wujub. The author responds that all instances are possible, so they could all cease, leading to complete absence, and also this would make the necessary being a locus for contingents. Then the author enters a discussion (بحث) about what is meant by 'cessation of wujub': if it means annihilation after external existence, some possible things like time cannot cease after existing, so it's not necessary that wujub can cease. If it means absolute non-existence, then the absence of the attribute itself does not entail the absence of the attribute from the subject, because attributes can be privative. However, the author chooses the second interpretation because the discussion assumes wujub is a real external thing, not a mental consideration; and real things cannot qualify a subject if they are non-existent. The gloss then reinforces that the essence of the Necessary necessitates the existence of its wujub, so its cessation is impossible due to that essence.

Correction: Wujub's cessation is impossible due to the essence of the Necessary, even though it is possible in itself. The paper distinguishes between possibility in itself and impossibility due to the subject.

The Four Investigations of the Necessary Being: Clarifying the Term
Announcement of four investigations and clarification of the term 'al-Wajib li-dhatihi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'al-Wajib li-dhatihi' and why it is essential to define it correctly before investigating the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being is defined by necessary existence for its essence, not by being uncaused.

Before we can investigate the Necessary Being, we must agree on what the term means. Al-Iji announces four investigations, but the first requires a precise definition. The commentator warns against confusing 'necessary for its essence' with other meanings that would make the inquiry self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the announcement of four investigations
Read the Arabic passage that opens the third observatory. Notice that al-Iji states there are four investigations of the Necessary Being. The first investigation is incomplete in our text, but the focus is on defining the subject.
The passage cuts off after 'لا يكون', but the definition is still given.
2
Understand the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that 'investigations' here means establishing the states of the Necessary Being. Then he defines 'al-Wajib li-dhatihi' precisely: its necessity is essential to its essence.
3
Notice the rejected alternatives
The commentator rejects two alternative definitions: (1) that its existence is required by its essence, and (2) that it is independent in existence from others. These are said to be self-evident and thus not proper subjects of inquiry.
If we accept those, the judgment 'the Necessary Being exists' would be immediate, not needing proof.
4
Reflect on why precision matters
Ask yourself: why is it important to define the Necessary Being this way? Because kalam seeks rational proofs, and a self-evident proposition cannot be the subject of a proof. Precision ensures the investigation is meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثالث : أبحاث الواجب لذاته ]
في أبحاث الواجب لذاته، وهي أربعة : أحدها أنه ( أي الواجب لذاته ( لا يكون
قوله : (في أبحاث الواجب) أي إثبات أحواله له .
قوله : (أي الواجب لذاته بمعنى ما يكون ثبوت الوجوب ضرورياً لذاته، لا بمعنى يكون وجوده مقتضى ذاته ولا بمعنى ما يستغني في الوجود عن الغير، وما يمتاز به الواجب فإنه حينئذ يكون الحكم المذكور بديهياً فلا جعله مسألة .
[ The third goal: Investigations of the Necessary Being ] In the investigations of the Necessary Being, and they are four: The first of them is that He, i.e., the Necessary Being, is not... His statement: (In the investigations of the Necessary Being) i.e., establishing His states for Him. His statement: (i.e., the Necessary Being) in the sense that the affirmation of necessity is essential to its essence, not in the sense that its existence is required by its essence, nor in the sense that it is independent in existence from others. And what distinguishes the Necessary is that then the mentioned judgment would be self-evident, so it would not be made a subject of inquiry.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage introduces the third observatory (المرصد الثالث) which is the investigations of the Necessary Being (أبحاث الواجب لذاته). It states that there are four such investigations. The first one is that the Necessary Being ... (the text cuts off). The commentator explains that 'in the investigations of the Necessary Being' means establishing His states. Then he clarifies the meaning of 'الواجب لذاته': it is that whose existence is necessary for its essence, not that which is self-sufficient or uncaused, because the latter definition is too broad. The commentator emphasizes that if we take the latter meaning, the judgment would be self-evident and thus not a proper subject for inquiry.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'al-Wajib li-dhatihi' simply means 'the One who is independent of others' or 'the One who is self-sufficient'.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the correct meaning is 'that for which the affirmation of necessity is essential to its essence'. The other meanings are secondary and would make the judgment self-evident, turning the investigation into a trivial matter.

The commentator rejected two definitions because they would make the judgment of existence self-evident and thus remove the need for inquiry. Have you ever stopped investigating your faith because you assumed it was 'obvious'? This lesson challenges you: are you content with inherited beliefs, or are you willing to wrestle with the precise meaning of Allah's necessity? Make a firm intention to study the proofs of His existence with the same precision as the scholars.

First Investigation: The Necessary by Itself is Not Necessary by Another
al-Iji presents the first investigation of the Necessary Being with proof and reply to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that the Necessary by itself cannot be necessary through another, and how al-Iji defends this against objections.

Core Idea
Self-necessity excludes all dependency on another for existence.

In this first investigation, al-Iji addresses a foundational question: can the being whose existence is necessary by itself also be necessary through something else? He argues that this is impossible, and defends his proof against several objections. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the key terms like 'واجب لذاته' and 'واجب بالغير'.
The passage includes the proof and then an objection-and-answer.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction
The author assumes the opposite (that the Necessary is necessary by another) and shows it leads to a contradiction: the Necessary would then depend on that other for its existence, so it would not be necessary by itself.
This is a standard kalam reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the objection
The objector challenges a premise: removal of the effect only follows if the cause is unique and if the effect's essence is not fully sufficient. The objector also suggests that the removal of the other might be impossible.
These are logical refinements to the causal principle.
4
Study the author's response
Al-Iji argues that full self-sufficiency excludes external causation because two independent causes cannot concur on one effect. He also addresses the impossibility of removal by clarifying that the supposition of removal is not actual.
The response reinforces the uniqueness and self-sufficiency of the Necessary.
5
Connect to the broader theology
Reflect on how this proof establishes that God's necessity is intrinsic and not derived from anything else, which is a cornerstone of Islamic monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أبحاث الواجب لذاته، وهي أربعة: أحدها أنه (أي الواجب لذاته) لا يكون واجباً بالغير، وإلا لزم من ارتفاع الغير ارتفاعه لوجوب ارتفاع المعلول عند ارتفاع العلة، فلم يكن الواجب لذاته واجباً لذاته، هذا خلف، واعترض عليه: بأنا لا نسلم لزوم ارتفاعه من ارتفاع ذلك الغير إنما يلزم ذلك إذا لم تكن ذاته مقتضية لوجوده اقتضاء تاماً، وارتفاع المعلول إنما يلزم من ارتفاع العلة إذا كانت منحصرة في ذلك الواحد الذي ارتفع، أما إذا كان له علة أخرى، فلا، وأيضاً ربما كان ارتفاع ذلك الغير محالاً، والمحال جاز أن يستلزم المحال، والجواب: أن ثبوت الوجود له لما كان مقتضى ذاته اقتضاء تاماً لم لا يتصور أن يكون ذلك الثبوت معللاً بغيره، وإلا لزم توارد العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد، وهو محال. فإذا فرض أنه معلل بالغير لم يكن معللاً بذاته بل بذلك الغير فقط، فلا يكون واجباً لذاته، بل يلزم من ارتفاعه الذي هو ممكن في نفسه لامتناع تعدد الواجب ارتفاعه قطعاً، وربما يغير الدليل، فيجاب: بأن قوله: إذا لم تكن ذاته مقتضية (الخ وأما إذا كانت مقتضية له كان ضرورة الوجود ناشئة عن ذاته أيضاً فلا يلزم ارتفاعه. قوله: (وأيضاً الخ) منع لبطلان التالي يعني المحال أن لا يكون الواجب لذاته واجباً لذاته في نفس الأمر لا أن لا يكون واجباً لذاته على تقدير محال، فإن ارتفاع العقل الأول يستلزم ارتفاع الواجب لذاته لكونه محالاً.
In the investigations of the Necessary by itself, and they are four: The first of them is that it (i.e., the Necessary by itself) is not necessary by another. Otherwise, from the removal of the other its removal would follow due to the necessity of the removal of the effect when the cause is removed. Then the Necessary by itself would not be necessary by itself; this is a contradiction. And an objection was raised against it: We do not grant the necessity of its removal from the removal of that other. That only follows if its essence is not fully necessitating its existence, and the removal of the effect only follows from the removal of the cause if the cause is confined to that single one which was removed. But if it has another cause, then not. Also, perhaps the removal of that other is impossible, and the impossible can entail the impossible. And the answer: Since the establishment of existence for it is a full requirement of its essence, it is inconceivable that that establishment be caused by another. Otherwise, the concurrence of two independent causes on one effect would follow, which is impossible. So if it is supposed to be caused by another, it is not caused by itself but only by that other, so it is not necessary by itself. Rather, from its removal—which is possible in itself due to the impossibility of multiplicity of the Necessary—its removal necessarily follows. And perhaps the argument is changed, and it is answered: That his statement: 'If its essence is not necessitating...' etc., and if it is necessitating, then the necessity of existence arises from its essence as well, so its removal does not follow. His statement: 'Also...etc.' is a refutation of the falsity of the consequent, meaning that it is impossible for the Necessary by itself to not be necessary by itself in actual fact, not that it would not be necessary by itself on a supposition of impossibility. For the removal of the First Intellect entails the removal of the Necessary by itself due to its being impossible.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is the first of four investigations concerning the Necessary by itself (al-wājib bi-dhātih). The author states the thesis: the Necessary by itself cannot be necessary by another (bi'l-ghayr). The proof is by reductio ad absurdum: if it were necessary through another, then the removal of that other would entail its own removal (since an effect is removed when its cause is removed), but then it would not be necessary by itself—a contradiction. An objection is then raised: the removal only follows if the self is not fully sufficient for existence and if the cause is unique; if there is another cause or if the removal of the other is impossible, the entailment fails. The answer: because the existence is fully necessitated by the self, it cannot be caused by another (otherwise two independent causes would concur on one effect, which is impossible). If it were caused by another, it would not be caused by itself, hence not necessary by itself. Moreover, the removal of that other is possible in itself (since only one Necessary exists), so its removal necessarily follows. The answer also addresses the objection that the removal of the other might be impossible, clarifying that the impossibility in question is not actual but suppositional, and does not preserve the necessity.

Key Terms
الواجب لذاته
الواجب بالغير
المعلول
العلة
توارد العلتين
اقتضاء تام
خلف
ارتفاع
لامتناع تعدد الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Necessary by itself could derive its necessity from another being without contradiction.
Correction: The proof shows that if its necessity came from another, its existence would depend on that other, so it would not be necessary by itself—a contradiction. Self-necessity is exclusive.

Checkpoint
What does al-Iji prove in the first investigation?
A) The Necessary by itself is not necessary by another.
B) The Necessary by itself is necessary by another.
C) The Necessary by itself can have multiple causes.
D) The removal of the Necessary is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary by itself cannot depend on anything else for its necessity; self-necessity is intrinsic and exclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is the Necessary by itself. But do you secretly depend on your wealth, health, or relationships as if they were necessary for your existence? Reflect on a situation today where your actions revealed a hidden belief that something besides Allah is necessary for you. What will you do to uproot that?
?
The proof shows that if something is necessary by itself, it cannot be caused by another. Allah alone fits this. If you truly internalized that Allah is the only Necessary Being, how would your fear of people, your anxiety about provision, or your attachment to worldly status change? What is one specific area you will surrender to Him tonight?
?
The objection considered the possibility of multiple causes, but the reply shut it down by stating that two independent causes on one effect is impossible. Have you been living as if there are multiple 'necessary' sources of happiness, success, or meaning in your life? Identify one such false source and repent from it.

intermediate
10 min
Two Causes Cannot Converge on One Effect
Objection and answer on the Necessary Being's independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being cannot be caused by another, and how the concept of two independent causes on one effect is impossible.

Core Idea
Two independent causes cannot converge on one effect.

The proof that the Necessary Being must be necessary by itself (and not by another) leads to a subtle objection: perhaps its essence fully necessitates its existence, so removing the other does not affect it. The commentator answers by showing that if it were caused by another, two independent causes would converge on one effect, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks that objection and its powerful answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the objection has two parts: (1) the effect might not fall if the cause is not exclusive, (2) the other's elimination might be impossible.
2
Identify the objector's arguments
Locate the two arguments the objector raises against the original proof. The first is about the cause not being exclusive, the second about impossibility.
3
Understand the commentator's response
The answer uses the principle that two independent causes cannot cause one effect. If the Necessary Being were caused by another, its essence would not be the cause, and it would not be necessary by itself.
This is a key logical move in kalam.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Think about why this argument is crucial for establishing the absolute independence of the Necessary Being. Without it, the proof might be vulnerable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه : بأنا لا نسلم لزوم ارتفاعه من ارتفاع ذلك الغير إنما يلزم ذلك إذا لم تكن ذاته مقتضية لوجوده اقتضاء تاماً، وارتفاع المعلول إنما يلزم من ارتفاع العلة إذا كانت منحصرة في ذلك الواحد الذي ارتفع، أما إذا كان له علة أخرى، فلا، وأيضاً ربما كان ارتفاع ذلك الغير محالاً، والمحال جاز أن يستلزم المحال. والجواب : أن ثبوت الوجود له لما كان مقتضى ذاته اقتضاء تاماً لم يتصور أن يكون ذلك الثبوت معللاً بغيره، وإلا لزم توارد العلتين المستقلتين على معلول واحد، وهو محال . فإذا فرض أنه معلل بالغير لم يكن معللاً بذاته بل بذلك الغير فقط، فلا يكون واجباً لذاته، بل يلزم من ارتفاعه الذي هو ممكن في نفسه لامتناع تعدد الواجب ارتفاعه قطعاً.
And an objection was raised against it: We do not concede that its elimination follows from the elimination of that other; that only follows if its essence does not necessitate its existence fully, and the elimination of the effect only follows from the elimination of the cause if it is confined to that single one that was eliminated; but if it has another cause, then not. Also, perhaps the elimination of that other is impossible, and impossibility may entail impossibility. The answer: Since the existence belonging to it is what its essence requires fully, it is not conceivable that this existence be caused by another, for otherwise the convergence of two independent causes on one effect would follow, which is impossible. So if it is assumed that it is caused by another, it is not caused by its essence but only by that other, so it is not necessary by itself; rather, from its elimination—which is possible in itself—its elimination necessarily follows due to the impossibility of multiple necessary beings.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair concerning the proof that the Necessary Being cannot be necessary by another. The objector first challenges the premise that if the other is removed, the Necessary Being would be removed. He says that only follows if the essence of the Necessary Being does not fully necessitate its own existence; if it does, then removing the other does not remove the Necessary Being. Second, the objector points out that the other might be impossible, and the impossible may entail the impossible, so no problem. The answer replies that if the existence of the Necessary Being is fully necessitated by its essence, then it cannot be caused by something else. If it were caused by another, two independent causes would converge on one effect, which is impossible. Therefore, if it is caused by another, it is not necessary by itself but only by that other, and then its elimination (which is possible in itself) would lead to the elimination of the Necessary Being, which is absurd due to the impossibility of there being more than one Necessary Being.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
واجب بالغير
توارد العلتين
مقتضى الذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the Necessary Being's essence fully necessitates existence, it can still be caused by another without contradiction.
Correction: The answer shows that two independent causes cannot converge on one effect; if the essence fully necessitates, then it is the sole cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the answer reject the possibility that the Necessary Being could be caused by another?
A) Because that would make the Necessary Being contingent.
B) Because that would lead to two independent causes on one effect, which is impossible.
C) Because the objector's premises are false.
D) Because the Necessary Being is eternal and cannot have a cause.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be caused by another because that would require two independent causes on one effect, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that nothing other than the Necessary Being can be fully independent. In your daily life, what are the things or people you functionally treat as 'necessary' for your well-being? Are you willing to let go of the illusion that they are indispensable, and instead anchor your sense of security solely in Allah? Identify one dependency you can actively surrender this week.
?
The objector suggests that perhaps the other cause's elimination is impossible, so the effect remains. This mirrors our tendency to assume that the secondary causes in our lives are 'permanent'—your job, health, relationships. But the answer reveals that only the Necessary Being is truly immune to removal. Are you living as if any of these temporary supports are unshakeable? How would your prayer and trust change if you fully internalized that they could vanish at any moment?
?
If the Necessary Being cannot be caused by another, then every other being is contingent and dependent. This means that ultimately, your very existence at this moment is upheld directly by Allah, regardless of the means He chooses. Does this truth make you feel exposed or comforted? Commit to one concrete action today that acknowledges your complete dependence on Him—such as a prostration of gratitude or a moment of silent reflection on His sole causality.

The Necessary Being Cannot Be Composite: Second Proof
The commentator states and explains the second proof that the Necessary Being cannot be composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the Necessary Being cannot be composite, and why composition from mental parts distinct in the mind but united in existence is impossible.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being cannot be composite because composition implies dependence.

This lesson presents the second proof from the text: the Necessary Being cannot be composed of parts, whether external or mental. The commentator clarifies that composition from mental parts that are distinct in the mind but united in existence would entail real dependence, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the commentator's clarifying remarks about mental parts.
2
Identify the main claim and argument
The passage states: the Necessary Being cannot be composite from parts distinct in the external world or in the mind. The reason: if composite, it would need its parts, and a part is other than the whole.
This reasoning is the core of the proof.
3
Understand the two types of composition
Composition from external parts (e.g., body parts) and from mental parts (e.g., genus and difference) are both negated. The commentator highlights that mental parts from a simple reality are not impossible.
Mental parts abstracted from a simple essence do not imply real composition.
4
Grasp the distinction about distinctness
The phrase 'in the external and in the mind' specifies that the distinctness of parts is what is negated. Composition from mental parts that are distinct in the mind but united in existence in the external is impossible.
This is a subtle point hidden from many.
5
Reflect on the implications
Why does this matter? It upholds the absolute simplicity of the Necessary Being, which is essential to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها أنه لا يكون ( الواجب لذاته ( مركبا لا من أجزاء متمايزة ( في الخارج (ولا من أجزاء متمايزة ) في الذهن وإلا احتاج الواجب لذاته ووجوده إلى جزئه بحسب نفس الأمر ) وجزء الشيء غيره، والمحتاج في ذاته ووجوده إلى غيره ليس واجبا لذاته. قوله : (لا) من أجزاء متمايزة (الخ ) لما كان ظاهر المتن يفيد أنه لا يجوز تركيب الواجب من أجزاء خارجية أو ذهنية، ويشمل ذلك أن يكون التركيب من الأجزاء الذهنية المنتزعة من أمر بسيط لا تعدد فيه أصلاً وهو ليس بممتنع لأنه إنما يستلزم أن يكون وجوده العقلي محتاجاً إلى تلك الأجزاء لا نفسه ولا استحالة فيه فإن الواجب تعالى محتاج إلى العقل في تعقله مطلقاً سواء كان بالوجه أو بالكنه ولا يلزم منه إمكانه تعالى. جعل الشارح قدس سره قوله في الخارج وفي الذهن ظرفاً لتمايز الأجزاء إخراجاً لذلك التركيب وتخصيصاً للمدعى بنفي تركيبه من الأجزاء الخارجية والأجزاء الذهنية المتمايزة في الذهن المتحدة الوجود في الخارج، فإن ذلك محال لاستلزامه احتياجه في تقومه إلى الأجزاء بحسب نفس الأمر لأن الأجزاء الذهنية على هذا التقدير أجزاء له تعالى متقوم به في نفسه إلا أنها متحدة به في الوجود، فتدبر فإنه مما خفي على أقوام.
And secondly, it is not the case that the Necessary Being by essence is composite—neither from parts distinct in the external world nor from parts distinct in the mind. Otherwise, the Necessary Being and its existence would need its part in relation to the very same thing, and a part of a thing is other than it, and that which is in need in its essence and existence of other than it is not necessary by essence. His statement: 'from parts distinct (etc.)'—since the apparent meaning of the text indicates that it is not permissible for the Necessary Being to be composite from external or mental parts, and that includes composition from mental parts abstracted from a simple matter having no multiplicity at all, which is not impossible because it only entails that its intellectual existence is in need of those parts, not its essence, and there is no impossibility in that; indeed, the Necessary Being, exalted, is in need of the intellect in conceiving it absolutely, whether by face or by core, and that does not entail its possibility. The commentator, may his secret be sanctified, made his statement 'in the external and in the mind' a locus for the distinctness of the parts, excluding that composition, and specifying the claim to negate its composition from external parts and from mental parts distinct in the mind but united in existence in the external, for that is impossible because it entails its need in its constitution to parts in relation to the very same thing, for the mental parts on this supposition are parts of Him, exalted, constituted in Himself, except that they are united with Him in existence. So ponder, for it is something that is hidden from many people.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation that the Necessary Being cannot be composite. The author first states the second proof: the Necessary Being cannot be composed of parts, whether those parts are distinct in the external world or distinct in the mind. The reason is that if it had parts, it would need those parts for its existence, and a part of a thing is other than the whole; anything that depends on another in its essence and existence cannot be necessary by itself. Then the commentator clarifies the scope of the claim. He notes that the apparent wording might include composition from mental parts that are abstracted from a simple reality with no multiplicity; that kind of composition is not impossible because it only means that the intellectual conception of the Necessary Being depends on those mental parts, not that the essence itself depends on them. Indeed, the Necessary Being is dependent on the intellect for being conceived, but that does not imply real composition or possibility. The commentator further explains that the phrase 'in the external and in the mind' is intended to specify that the distinctness of parts is what is negated. Composition from mental parts that are distinct in the mind but united in existence in the external is what is negated, because that would entail that the Necessary Being's self- constitution depends on those parts in reality, even if they are mentally distinct. This is a subtle point that is hidden from many people.

Key Terms
مركب
أجزاء متمايزة
الوجود العقلي
تقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that mental composition (e.g., distinguishing attributes in the mind) implies real composition in the essence.
Correction: Mental composition from a simple reality is not impossible; it only affects intellectual existence. What is impossible is composition from parts distinct in the mind but united in existence externally, as that would entail real dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is composition from mental parts that are abstracted from a simple reality not impossible for the Necessary Being?
A) Because such mental parts do not exist in reality.
B) Because it only affects the intellectual existence, not the essence.
C) Because the Necessary Being can have attributes.
D) Because the commentator does not consider it.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be composite because composition entails dependence on parts, which contradicts necessity; however, mental composition from a simple reality is not problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The commentator says this subtle point is 'hidden from many people'. What does that say about your own study of God's nature? Are you content with surface-level understanding, or will you strive to grasp the deeper proofs that protect your faith?

Objection and Response on the Composite Necessary Being
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and response about composition and necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection that absolute dependence implies contingency and the response that each individual part is not the essence, so a composite being cannot be necessary by itself.

Core Idea
A composite being depends on each part, and each part is not its essence — so it cannot be necessary.

In the debate about the necessary being, one objection argues that even if a being is composed of parts that are its very essence, dependence on those parts does not make it contingent. Al-Jurjani presents this objection and then answers it by carefully distinguishing between the totality of parts as essence and each individual part as something other than the essence. This lesson walks through that dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the full argument settle in your mind before analyzing.
Notice the dialectical structure: objector's claim, then a concession, then the responder's counter.
2
Identify the objector's two moves
The objector first denies that absolute dependence implies contingency, then concedes it but argues that the parts are the essence, so dependence on them is self-dependence.
3
Understand the responder's key distinction
The responder argues that although the whole collection of parts is the essence, each individual part is not the essence but other. Therefore the composite being depends on something other than itself.
This is the critical insight: the whole is not the same as each part.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might think that if all parts are the essence, then reliance on parts is self-reliance. The passage shows this is false because each part individually is not the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال كون المحتاج إلى الغير مطلقا ممكنا ممنوع، بل المحتاج إلى العلة هو الممكن. وإن سلم أن المحتاج إلى الغير على الإطلاق ممكن، لكن جميع أجزائه هي ذاته لا غيره، فلا يخرجه الاحتياج إليها عن كونه بحيث يجب وجوده لذاته. لأنا نقول: جميع أجزائه وإن كان ذاته، لكن كل واحد من أجزائه ليس ذاته بل هو غيره، فإذا كان مركبا، فلا يكون ذاته من دون ملاحظة الغير الذي هو كل واحد من أجزائه كافيا في وجوده، بل يكون ذاته في نفسه ووجوده محتاجا إلى غيره فلا يكون واجبا.
It cannot be said: 'The fact that something is absolutely dependent on another makes it contingent' is denied; rather, that which depends on a cause is the contingent. And if it is granted that that which is absolutely dependent on another is contingent, yet all its parts are itself, not other, so dependence on them does not remove it from being such that its existence is necessary by itself. We say: All its parts, even if they are its essence, yet each one of its parts is not its essence but is other than it. Therefore, since it is composite, its essence without consideration of the other which is each one of its parts is not sufficient for its existence; rather, its essence in itself and its existence are dependent on another, so it is not necessary.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer argument in the discussion of the necessary being. The objector denies the major premise that 'absolute dependence on another entails contingency', asserting instead that only dependence on a cause makes something contingent. He then concedes for argument's sake that absolute dependence implies contingency but argues that the composite necessary being's parts are its very essence, so dependence on them does not compromise its necessity. The answer (from the author) replies that although the whole collection of parts is the essence, each individual part is not the essence but other than it. Therefore, the composite being's essence and existence depend on these parts, making it not necessary by itself. The key move is distinguishing between the totality as essence and the individual parts as other.

Key Terms

العلة

الواجب
الجزء
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if all parts are the essence, then dependence on parts is self-dependence, so necessity is preserved.
Correction: The passage shows that each individual part is not the essence, so dependence on parts is dependence on other, making the being contingent.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does a composite being (even if all its parts are its essence) fail to be necessary?
A) Because the whole is not the same as the parts.
B) Because each individual part is not the essence, so the whole depends on them.
C) Because the parts are external to the essence.
D) Because the whole cannot exist without its parts.

Key Takeaway
A composite being depends on each part, and since each part individually is not its essence, the composite being cannot be necessary by itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that Allah alone is the Necessary Being who depends on nothing. But in your daily life, what ‘part’ of your identity—your intellect, your health, your social status—do you secretly rely on as though it were self-sufficient? Are you willing to admit that every part of you is contingent and dependent on Him, and to live in that truth today?
?
The passage shows that any composite being cannot be necessary. Your own soul is composite—body, desires, thoughts. How does this reality of your own contingency press you to turn away from self-reliance and cling to the One who is not composite? What is one practical step you will take this week to act on your absolute need for Him?

Proof of Divine Simplicity
Presents the argument that a composite necessary being would depend on its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being cannot be composite and how this argument establishes divine simplicity.

Core Idea
Composition implies dependence; the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything.

This lesson presents a concise proof from kalam for divine simplicity. The author argues that if the Necessary Being had parts, it would need those parts to exist, contradicting its necessity. The passage is a classic example of how kalam thinkers deduce divine attributes from the concept of necessity itself.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the premise
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the argument starts with a conditional: 'if it is composite'. Understand that 'parts' here means any ontological constituents that are other than the essence.
The term 'ذاته' (His essence) refers to the divine essence itself.
2
Trace the logical implication
Identify why a composite being would be dependent on its parts. The key phrase is 'its essence without consideration of its parts would not be sufficient'. This means the essence relies on its parts for existence.
'كافٍ في الوجود' means 'sufficient for existence' – the essence alone would not be enough.
3
Draw the conclusion
Understand that dependence on others makes a being contingent (mumkin), not necessary. Therefore, the Necessary Being cannot be composite; it must be simple (non-composite).
This argument does not deny that God has attributes; it denies composition in the sense of having parts that are ontologically prior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان ذاته لكن كل واحد من أجزائه ليس ذاته بل هو غيره فإذا كان مركباً، فلا يكون ذاته من دون ملاحظة الغير الذي هو كل واحد من أجزائه كافياً في وجوده، بل يكون ذاته في نفسه ووجوده محتاجاً إلى غيره فلا يكون واجباً
His essence, but every one of its parts is not its essence but is other than it. So if it is composite, then its essence without consideration of the other which is each one of its parts would not be sufficient for its existence; rather, its essence in itself and its existence would be in need of another, so it would not be necessary.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the Necessary Being (al-wajib) being composite. It begins by stating a premise: if the Necessary Being had parts, each part would be other than the essence itself. The argument then concludes that if it were composite, its essence could not be sufficient for its existence without considering its parts; instead, it would need those parts, meaning it would be dependent on what is other than itself. Such dependence makes it contingent (mumkin), not necessary. Therefore, the Necessary Being must be simple, without parts.

Key Terms
ذاته
أجزاء
مركب
كافٍ في الوجود
محيتاج
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God has any attributes or distinctions.
Correction: The argument only denies composition in the sense of ontologically prior parts, not attributes that are identical with the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why would a composite necessary being not be truly necessary?
A) Because its parts would be temporal
B) Because it would depend on its parts for existence
C) Because composite things are always material
D) Because the parts would be more necessary than the whole

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot have parts because that would make it dependent and thereby contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is absolutely self-sufficient and independent of all parts, then He depends on nothing—yet you often act as if your well-being depends on people, possessions, or status. In the next 24 hours, what is one thing you can deliberately entrust solely to God, acknowledging that He alone is sufficient?
?
Consider your mental image of God: do you ever think of His mercy as separate from His justice, as if they were parts that could conflict? How can you begin to see all His attributes as a single, undivided reality that demands your complete trust?
?
The passage proves that a composed being cannot be the Necessary Being. Now look at your own heart—what 'composites' (loyalties, attachments, fears) do you rely on alongside God? Are you willing to let go of one of them today to affirm that God alone is the source of all you need?

Why Necessity Must Be the Essence Itself
Al-Jurjani defends the identity of necessity with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of necessity (wujub) cannot be accidental to the divine essence but must be identical to it.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be an accidental attribute; it is the essence itself.

Al-Jurjani argues that the Necessary Being cannot have composition, so necessity cannot be an accidental attribute. An objector suggests it could be accidental, but the author counters that if necessity were existential, it would be identical to the essence. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim that necessity might be accidental to the essence, so composition would not follow.
The objector tries to separate necessity from essence to avoid composition.
2
Analyze the response
Al-Jurjani responds by stating that necessity is not a common existential attribute. If it were existential, it would be identical to the essence.
This preserves the simplicity of the Necessary Being.
3
Understand the referral
The author says the most apparent way is to refer this judgment to the proof of tawhid to show absolute impossibility of sharing in necessity.
This connects to a broader proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهية والتعين ( وأنه محال لما مر من امتناع تركب الواجب ( لا يقال : لا نسلم أنه نفس الماهية لجواز أن يكون عارضاً لها فلا يلزم تركب الواجب ( لأنا نقول : المدعى ) هو ( أنه لا يكون ) الوجوب ) وجودياً مشتركاً ، وقد بينا أنه لو كان وجودياً كان نفس الماهية)، والأظهر أن يحال هذا الحكم على برهان التوحيد، ليظهر امتناع الاشتراك مطلقاً

قوله: ( والأظهر أن يحال هذا إلخ لبعض المتأخرين هاهنا إشكال قوي وهو أنه كيف يحيله على برهان التوحيد ولم يذكر ثمة إلا دليلين على نفي تعدد الواجب على طريقة الحكماء، وكلاهما مبني على كون الوجوب ثبوتياً ونفس الماهية كما صرح به هناك، ودليلين أيضاً على طريقة المتكلمين على نفي تعدد الإله، ولا تعرض فيهما للوجوب ونفي تعدده، وغاية ما يقال بعد تسليم أن ليس المراد بالبرهان المذكور في غير هذا الكتاب أن الوجوب الذاتي أخص أوصاف الباري تعالى وأن الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية وبالجملة هو معدن لكل كمال ومبعد عن كل نقصان كما صرحوا به فلما ثبت بدلیل المتكلمين انتفاء تعدد الإله ثبت انتفاء تعدد الواجب، سواء كان الوجوب وجودياً أو عدمياً، لأن الاشتراك في الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية المقتضية للألوهية، والحاصل أن الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الألوهية، وتعدده تعدد الآلهة والدليل الدال على انتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم .
The essence and determination: (And that it is impossible due to what has passed of the impossibility of composition of the Necessary Being. It is not said: We do not concede that it is the essence itself because it is possible that it be accidental to it, so composition of the Necessary Being would not follow. For we say: The claim is that necessity (al-wujub) is not an existential attribute common [to essence and accident], and we have shown that if it were existential, it would be the essence itself.) And the most apparent is to refer this judgment to the proof of tawhid, so that the impossibility of sharing [in necessity] absolutely may appear.

His statement: (And the most apparent is to refer this etc.) Some later scholars have a strong problem here, namely: How can he refer it to the proof of tawhid when there is mentioned there only two proofs for negating multiplicity of the Necessary Being according to the method of the philosophers, both based on necessity being a positive attribute and identical to the essence as stated there, and also two proofs according to the method of the theologians for negating multiplicity of the god, with no mention in them of necessity and its non-multiplicity? The utmost that can be said, after conceding that what is meant by the mentioned proof in other than this book is that essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is the most specific attribute of the Exalted Creator, and that sharing in the most specific attribute entails sharing in the essence, and in short He is the source of every perfection and far from every deficiency as they stated, then when the negation of multiplicity of the god is established by the theologians' proof, the negation of multiplicity of the Necessary Being is established, whether necessity is existential or non-existential, because sharing in essential necessity entails sharing in the essence that necessitates divinity. The upshot is that essential necessity entails divinity, and its multiplicity would be multiplicity of gods, and the proof that indicates the negation of the consequent indicates the negation of the antecedent.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument about the relationship between the essence of the Necessary Being and the attribute of necessity (wujub). The author first states that it is impossible for the Necessary Being to have composition, meaning its essence and its necessity are not two separate things. An objector claims that necessity could be an accidental attribute, thus not identical to the essence, so composition would not arise. The author responds by asserting that the claim is that necessity is not an existential attribute common to both essence and accident; if it were existential, it would be the essence itself. He then says the most apparent course is to refer this judgment to the proof of tawhid (the oneness of God) to show the absolute impossibility of sharing in necessity. The commentary explains that some later scholars question this referral because the proof of tawhid mentioned earlier only provides two arguments from the philosophers (based on necessity being positive and identical to the essence) and two from the theologians (negating multiple gods without discussing necessity). The resolution is that the proof of tawhid, as understood in other works, establishes that essential necessity is the most specific attribute of God, and sharing in that attribute entails sharing in the essence that necessitates divinity. Therefore, the negation of multiple gods (proved by the theologians) directly negates multiple necessary beings, regardless of whether necessity is considered existential or non-existential, because essential necessity entails divinity, and multiplicity of necessity would imply multiplicity of gods.

التعين
برهان التوحيد

A) It is impossible for the Necessary Being.
B) It is accidental to the essence.
C) It is identical to the essence.
D) It is a common existential attribute.

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be accidental to the divine essence; it is the essence itself, preserving divine simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If necessity is identical to God's essence, then His nature is unchangeable and absolute. Are there areas in your life where you wish God's nature could be different, or where you doubt His perfection? How can you embrace His unchanging necessity today?
?
This lesson argues that necessity cannot be accidental—it is essential. Do you treat God's attributes as secondary in your daily life? What would change if you truly believed His mercy, justice, and power are not separate qualities but His very being? Commit to one action today that reflects this belief.
?
The proof of tawhid is invoked to show that necessity cannot be shared. Have you ever implicitly associated partners with God by relying on created causes as 'necessary' in your heart? This week, identify one worldly reliance you place above God's necessity and replace it with trust in Him alone.

How the Proof of Tawhid Negates Multiple Necessities
Al-Jurjani explains and defends the referral to the proof of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of tawhid (God's oneness) is used to establish essential necessity and negate multiple necessary beings.

Core Idea
Essential necessity entails divinity, so negating multiple gods negates multiple necessary beings.

Al-Jurjani earlier referred the judgment to the proof of tawhid. Some later scholars found this puzzling because the proof of tawhid does not explicitly mention necessity. This passage resolves the issue by showing that essential necessity is the most specific attribute of God, and thus the theologians' proof against multiple gods equally negates multiple necessary beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the difficulty
Some scholars questioned how the proof of tawhid can be used here when it only discusses multiplicity of gods and not necessity.
The proof of tawhid has two versions: from philosophers and theologians.
2
Understand the resolution
The answer is that essential necessity is the most specific attribute of God. Sharing in that attribute entails sharing in the essence that necessitates divinity.
Thus, negating multiple gods negates multiple necessary beings.
3
Apply the logic
The upshot: essential necessity entails divinity. If there were multiple necessities, there would be multiple gods. Since the proof of tawhid negates multiple gods, it negates multiple necessities.
This shows the referral is valid regardless of how necessity is considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهية والتعين ( وأنه محال لما مر من امتناع تركب الواجب ( لا يقال : لا نسلم أنه نفس الماهية لجواز أن يكون عارضاً لها فلا يلزم تركب الواجب ( لأنا نقول : المدعى ) هو ( أنه لا يكون ) الوجوب ) وجودياً مشتركاً ، وقد بينا أنه لو كان وجودياً كان نفس الماهية)، والأظهر أن يحال هذا الحكم على برهان التوحيد، ليظهر امتناع الاشتراك مطلقاً

قوله: ( والأظهر أن يحال هذا إلخ لبعض المتأخرين هاهنا إشكال قوي وهو أنه كيف يحيله على برهان التوحيد ولم يذكر ثمة إلا دليلين على نفي تعدد الواجب على طريقة الحكماء، وكلاهما مبني على كون الوجوب ثبوتياً ونفس الماهية كما صرح به هناك، ودليلين أيضاً على طريقة المتكلمين على نفي تعدد الإله، ولا تعرض فيهما للوجوب ونفي تعدده، وغاية ما يقال بعد تسليم أن ليس المراد بالبرهان المذكور في غير هذا الكتاب أن الوجوب الذاتي أخص أوصاف الباري تعالى وأن الاشتراك في أخص الأوصاف يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية وبالجملة هو معدن لكل كمال ومبعد عن كل نقصان كما صرحوا به فلما ثبت بدلیل المتكلمين انتفاء تعدد الإله ثبت انتفاء تعدد الواجب، سواء كان الوجوب وجودياً أو عدمياً، لأن الاشتراك في الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الاشتراك في الماهية المقتضية للألوهية، والحاصل أن الوجوب الذاتي يستلزم الألوهية، وتعدده تعدد الآلهة والدليل الدال على انتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم .
The essence and determination: (And that it is impossible due to what has passed of the impossibility of composition of the Necessary Being. Al-Razi's Third Way: Time's Necessity by Another
Al-Razi proves time's necessity by another using the philosophers' own premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's proof that time's necessity is derived from another, making possibility (imkan) the cause of need for a cause, not temporal origination.

Core Idea
Time is necessary by another, not by itself — its possibility, not its beginning, explains its dependency.

Imam al-Razi constructs a proof based on the philosophers' own claim that time cannot be non-existent before or after its existence. He shows that even on that claim, time must be contingent because it is composed of passing moments. Therefore, its necessity comes from something else, and that dependency is due to its possibility, not because it had a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the matn (original text) and the commentary. Notice how the argument is built on the philosophers' own premise.
The matn is the core proof; the commentary explains each phrase.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and Razi's starting point
The philosophers assert that time's non-existence before or after its existence is impossible. Al-Razi takes this as a given and explores its implications.
This is a conditional: if that restricted non-existence is impossible, then what follows?
3
Follow the argument to its conclusion
Trace the logical steps: from the impossibility of restricted non-existence, to the conclusion that time is necessary, but then its composition shows it is possible, so its necessity must be by another.
Pay attention to the role of composition from 'passing moments'.
4
Understand the commentary's clarifications
The commentary addresses objections about the composition premise, the meaning of 'necessary', and the philosophers' 'flowing now'. Understand why the proof is considered valid despite these discussions.
The commentary also distinguishes between coercive and veridical reasoning.
5
Reflect on the implication for time's contingency
The final conclusion is that possibility (imkan) is the cause of time's need for a cause, not temporal origination (huduth). This refutes the philosophers' claim that time is self-sufficient.
This argument is a key refutation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الطريق الثالث له ) أي للإمام الرازي ذكره في الأربعين، وقد بناه على قول الفلاسفة: أنه يمتنع عدم الزمان قبل وجوده أو بعده ) أي يمتنع عدمه مقيداً بهذا القيد وهو أن يكون قبل وجوده أو بعده لا عدمه مطلقاً، وإلا كان واجباً بذاته ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يمتنع كون عدمه قبل وجوده أو بعده ،فبزمان ) أي ( فيكون تقدم العدم على وجوده أو تأخره عنه بزمان لأن المتقدم إذا لم يمكن أن يجامع المتأخر كان التقدم زمانياً. ( ويجتمع الوجود والعدم ) ؛ لأن الزمان حال ما كان معدوماً كان موجوداً، فيجتمع وجوده وعدمه معاً، هذا خلف . (فهو ) أي الزمان لامتناع عدمه كذلك (واجب) مستمر وجوده دائماً، ) وأنه ممكن لذاته؛ لتركبه من آنات منقضية)، فلا يكون وجوبه لذاته، لما مر من استحالة تركب الواجب بالذات خصوصاً إذا كانت الأجزاء منقضية متعاقبة، ( فوجوبه بالغير، فيكون الإمكان علة الحاجة إلى الغير دون الحدوث، إذ لاحدوث هاهنا . (ولا
قوله : ( كان التقدم زمانياً ) إلا أنه لأجزاء الزمان لذاتها، ولما سواه بواسطة مقارنته إياه .
قوله: (لتركبه من آنات الخ) لا يخفى أن هذه المقدمة باطلة عند الحكماء، لاستلزامه الجزء. فبناء هذا الاستدلال على قول الفلاسفة معناه استعمال مقدمة مسلمة عندهم فيها، لا أن مقدماته مسلمة عندهم هكذا قيل وليس بشيء، لأن الاستدلال حينئذ لا يكون إلزامياً؛ لبطلان هذه المقدمة عندهم ولا تحقيقيا لعدم حقية المقدمة الأولى في الواقع عند المستدل فالصواب أن يقال المراد بالآنات أجزاء الزمان الغير المنقسمة فعلاً، ومعنى تركيبه منها تحليله إليها وكونها حاصلة فيه بالقوة .
قوله : ( فيكون إلخ) اللازم مما ذكر أن يكون الممكن الغير الحادث محتاجاً إلى الغير، ولا منه أن يكون الإمكان علة، إلا أن يبنى على عدم القول بعلة ما سوى الإمكان والحدوث.
قوله : دون الحدوث أي لا يكون له مدخل أصلاً .
قوله (واجب) مستمر (وجوده) أشار بقوله : مستمر وجوده إلى أنه المراد بالوجوب، لا الوجوب الذاتي؛ لأن الواجب الذات ما يستحيل عدمه مطلقا والمستحيل هاهنا هو العدم المقيد بكونه قبل الموجود أو بعده .
قوله: (لتركبه من آنات منقضية) فيه بحث؛ لأن عدم تركب الزمان من الآنات وعدم مسلمات الحكمة وكأنه أراد من قول الفلاسفة الذي جعله مبنى للدليل مجرد الزمان موجود يمتنع عدمه المقيد، لا أن كل مقدماته قول الفلاسفة، أو أراد بالآنات الأجزاء الغير المنقسمة خارجاً وإن انقسمت فرضاً ووهماً . وفيه بعد تسليم عدم التلازم بين الانقسام الفرضي والخارجي هاهنا أن تركب الزمان من تلك الأجزاء يمنع قدمه واستمرار وجوده؛ لأن تلك الآنات أجزاء له لا أفراد حتى يدعى قدمه بالنوع بمعنى أن فرداً من أفراده موجود دائماً، والتحقيق أن الزمان المدعى قدمه عند الفلاسفة الآن السيال، وهو أمر بسيط لا تركب فيه، كما سيأتي تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى فالصواب في بيان أنه ممكن لذاته بيان عدم استحالة عدمه مطلقاً وإن استحال عدمه المقيد، كما أشرنا إليه آنفاً .
The third way – for him, i.e. Imam al-Razi, he mentioned it in al-Arba'in, and he built it upon the statement of the philosophers: that the non-existence of time before its existence or after it is impossible, i.e. its non-existence qualified by this restriction, that it be before its existence or after it, not its absolute non-existence, otherwise it would be necessary by itself. And if not, i.e. if its non-existence before its existence or after it is not impossible, then by a time, i.e. then the priority of non-existence over its existence or its posteriority to it would be by a time, because the prior, if it cannot coincide with the posterior, the priority is temporal. And existence and non-existence would combine, because time, when it was non-existent, was existent, so its existence and non-existence would combine together – this is absurd. So it, i.e. time, due to the impossibility of its non-existence in that way, is necessary, its existence perpetual always, and it is possible in itself due to its composition from passing moments, so its necessity is not by itself, due to the impossibility of the composition of the necessary by itself, especially if the parts are passing and successive, so its necessity is by another, so possibility is the cause of need for the other, not temporality, for there is no temporality here. His statement: 'the priority is temporal' – but it is for the parts of time essentially, and for other than it by virtue of its conjunction with it. His statement: 'due to its composition from moments, etc.' – it is not hidden that this premise is false according to the sages, because it entails the part. So building this inference upon the statement of the philosophers means using a premise conceded by them in it, not that its premises are conceded by them – thus it was said, and it is nothing, because then the inference would not be coercive, due to the falsity of this premise according to them, nor veridical, due to the lack of truth of the first premise in reality according to the reasoner. So the correct thing is to say that by 'moments' is meant the parts of time that are actually indivisible, and the meaning of its composition from them is its analysis into them and their being in it potentially. His statement: 'so it follows, etc.' – the necessary consequence of what was mentioned is that the non-temporal possible is in need of the other, and it is not necessary that possibility be the cause, unless it is built upon the absence of the statement of a cause other than possibility and temporality. His statement: 'not temporality' – i.e. it has no entry at all. His statement: 'necessary, its existence perpetual' – he indicated by his statement 'its existence perpetual' that what is meant by necessity is not essential necessity, because the essential necessary is that whose non-existence is absolutely impossible, whereas what is impossible here is the non-existence restricted to being before the existent or after it. His statement: 'due to its composition from passing moments' – there is discussion therein; because the non-composition of time from moments is not among the conceded premises of the wisdom, and it is as if he meant by the statement of the philosophers upon which he built the evidence merely that time is an existent whose restricted non-existence is impossible, not that all his premises are the statements of the philosophers, or he meant by 'moments' the parts that are indivisible externally, even if they are divisible by assumption and estimation. And therein, after conceding the lack of correlation between division by assumption and external division here, the composition of time from those parts prevents its pre-eternity and perpetuity of its existence; because those moments are parts of it, not individuals, such that its pre-eternity by type could be claimed, meaning that an individual of its individuals always exists. And the verification is that time whose pre-eternity is claimed by the philosophers is the flowing now, which is a simple thing without composition, as will be verified if God wills. So the correct thing in explaining that it is possible in itself is to explain the non-impossibility of its non-existence absolutely, even if its restricted non-existence is impossible, as we indicated earlier.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents a proof: Imam al-Razi's third argument, built on the philosophers' own claim that time cannot be non-existent before or after its existence. The passage is a proof followed by commentary explaining each step.

First, the matn (original text) states the argument:
- The philosophers say it is impossible for time to be non-existent before its existence or after it. This means its non-existence is impossible under that restriction (that it be before or after), not absolutely; otherwise time would be necessary by itself.
- If that restricted non-existence were not impossible, then the priority or posteriority of non-existence relative to time's existence would be temporal, requiring a time interval. Then, during that interval, time would be both non-existent (before its existence) and existent (since the interval is part of time) – a contradiction.
- Therefore, time's restricted non-existence is impossible, so time is necessary (wājib) in the sense of continuous perpetual existence. But it is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātih) because it is composed of passing moments (ānāt munqaḍiya). A necessary being cannot be composite, especially of successive parts. Hence its necessity is from another (bi'l-ghayr). Therefore, possibility (imkān) is the cause of the need for the other, not temporal origination (ḥudūth), because there is no temporality here (time itself is eternal).

The commentary explains each part:
- 'The priority is temporal' applies to the parts of time essentially, but to other things by their conjunction with time.
- The premise that time is composed of moments is false according to the philosophers (ḥukamā'), but the argument uses it as a premise they concede (musallama). This makes the argument not purely coercive (ilzāmī) nor veridical (taḥqīqī). The correct meaning of 'moments' here is the parts of time that are actually indivisible; composition is by way of potential analysis.
- The necessary consequence is that non-temporal possibles need a cause, but possibility is not necessarily the cause unless there is no other cause besides possibility and temporality.
- 'Not temporality' means temporality has no entry in explaining the need.
- The term 'necessary' here means perpetual existence, not essential necessity (al-wājib al-dhātī). The impossibility is only of restricted non-existence.
- The discussion on composition: The composition of time from parts would prevent its pre-eternity. The verification is that the time claimed to be pre-eternal by philosophers is the 'flowing now' (al-ān al-sayyāl), which is simple, not composite. So the correct way to show time is possible in itself is to show that absolute non-existence is not impossible, even if restricted non-existence is impossible.

Key Terms
الزمان
الأنات
المنقضية
الواجب بالغير
الإمكان
الحدوث
الآن السيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' claim that time is necessary might lead one to think time is absolutely self-sufficient and requires no cause.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even on their own premise, time's composition proves it is possible in itself, so its necessity must come from another, and the need is due to its possibility, not its beginning.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of al-Razi's third way regarding time?
A) Time is necessary by itself and requires no cause.
B) Time is possible in itself and necessary by another, so imkan is the cause of need.
C) Time is impossible in itself.
D) Time is composed of discrete parts proving its eternity.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's third way demonstrates that time is necessary by another, and that possibility (imkan) is the cause of its need for a cause, not its temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on time every moment of your life – but have you ever paused to consider that time itself is not self-sufficient? If time depends on another for its existence, how does that reshape your sense of reliance on the One who sustains it? Will you let this truth sink into your daily awareness, so that every tick of the clock reminds you of your utter need for Allah?
?
Al-Razi argues that possibility (imkān) is the cause of need, not the fact that something begins. What areas of your life do you assume are 'just there' or self-sufficient, when in reality they are sustained by Allah at every instant? Can you identify one such area today and consciously acknowledge your dependence?
?
The philosophers thought time's impossibility of non-existence made it necessary by itself. Where in your own thinking have you mistaken a temporary 'security' or 'permanence' for something independent of Allah? What would it take to break that illusion and live with true tawakkul?

Refuting the Objection of Intrinsic Priority
The commentator refutes the objection that intrinsic priority suffices for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that priority could be intrinsic fails, and how the dependence of non-existence on non-existence preserves the need for an efficient cause.

Core Idea
Non-existence is caused by non-existence, not by existence.

This lesson tackles a sophisticated objection: Could a possible's priority for existence be intrinsic, eliminating the need for an external cause? The commentator argues that if priority sufficed for occurrence, it would rely on the absence of the opposite cause, which itself points to dependence on another's non-existence. Thus, the argument for God's existence remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the objector's claim: priority might be intrinsic and sufficient for existence without needing absence of the opposite cause. Note the analogy with necessity by a cause not contradicting equality in essence.
The objector uses a parallel to necessity by a cause to argue that intrinsic priority is possible.
2
Identify the Commentator's Response
See how the commentator specifies that the priority in question must be the kind that suffices for occurrence. He then shows that if it requires absence of the opposite cause, it is not truly intrinsic.
The key move: the priority that suffices is not intrinsic if it depends on another's absence.
3
Examine the Illustration
Consider the example of existence: if existence becomes prior due to absence of the cause of non-existence, that would suffice for existence without an efficient agent. This would close the door to proving God's existence.
This shows the consequence of accepting the objection: atheism.
4
Understand the Principle of Non-Existence Causation
Learn that the cause of non-existence is non-existence itself, and the dependence is rational, not external. The absence of a complete cause necessitates the absence of its effect.
This principle refutes the idea that an existent (like removal of obstacle) can be a cause of non-existence.
5
Connect to the Conclusion
Realize that the possible does not have a claim to priority of one side in itself. Its existence always needs an external cause, and the argument for the Maker is thereby established.
The objection fails to undermine the proof.
