(قلنا: سبب العدم عدم لأن أعدام المعلولات مستندة إلى أعدام عللها، (فعدمه) أي عدم قوله : (مستندة إلى أعدام (عللها أي التامة بمعنى فواعلها المستجمعة لشرائط التأثير استناداً عقلياً، بمعنى أن العقل إذا لاحظ صدور شيء عن مؤثر تام حكم أن عدمه يوجب عدم ذلك الشيء، سواء كان عدم ذلك المؤثر بعدم نفسه أو بعدم شرط من شرائط تأثيره، لا استنادا خارجياً إذ لا تمايز في الأعدام في الخارج حتى يتصور استناد بعضها إلى بعض فيه، فاستناد العدم إلى العدم فرع استناد الوجود إلى الوجود فإذا كانت الأولوية الذاتية للوجود موقوفة على عدم المؤثر التام الذي هو وجوده بحسب الصدق وإن كان مغايرته في المفهوم يثبت احتياج الممكن في وجوده إلى المؤثر التام، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم أن يفهم هذا الكلام ليندفع ما قيل : لا نسلم أن سبب العدم عدم فإن من جملة علة الموجود انتفاء المانع فوجوده يكون علة العدم وما قيل أن الممكن المفروض ليس معلولاً لموجود حتى يكون عدمه مستنداً إلى عدمه، بل هو معلول لعدم سبب العدم، فيكون عدمه مسـ مستنداً إلى وجوده . جواز وقوع الممكن نظراً إلى ذاته من غير احتياج إلى غيره. وأما أن الممكن لا يستحق في ذاته حصول أولوية أحد طرفيه فلا يتعلق به غرض لأن الممكن مع هذا الاستحقاق وبدونه يحتاج في طرفيه إلى غيره، وبذلك يتم الاستدلال على وجود الصانع.
His statement: 'So that priority is not for its essence.' For if you say: It is permissible that one of the two sides of the possible is prior to it in itself, and that priority does not depend on the absence of the cause of the other side, even if the occurrence of the first side depends on it; for there is no contradiction between them. In sum: just as the necessity of one side of the possible by its cause does not contradict their equality with respect to the essence, likewise it does not contradict the priority of the other side with respect to it. I say: By this negated priority, they mean that which reaches the point of sufficing for the occurrence of that side, for the purpose of this negation is to repel the illusion of the conjunction of the absence of the cause of the other side to it. So let us suppose that that side is existence, then it becomes prior due to the conjunction of the absence of the cause of non-existence to the essence of the possible, and there is no impossibility in the occurrence of the preponderant side. So the absence of the cause of non-existence suffices for the occurrence of existence when conjoined to the essence of the possible, and that—i.e., what was mentioned of the absence of the cause of non-existence being sufficient for the existence of the possible—dispenses with the existence of the efficient agent in the existing possibles, and thus the door of proving the existence of the Maker is closed. We said: The cause of non-existence is non-existence, because the non-existences of effects are dependent on the non-existences of their causes, so its non-existence i.e., non-existence. His statement: 'Dependent on the non-existences of their causes' i.e., the complete causes, meaning their agents that have gathered the conditions of influence, in a rational dependence, meaning that when the intellect observes the emanation of something from a complete efficient agent, it judges that its non-existence necessitates the non-existence of that thing, whether that non-existence of the efficient agent is by its own non-existence or by the non-existence of a condition of its influence, not an external dependence, for there is no differentiation among non-existences externally such that the dependence of some on others could be conceived therein. So the dependence of non-existence on non-existence is a branch of the dependence of existence on existence. Thus if the intrinsic priority of existence is conditional upon the non-existence of the complete efficient agent which is its existence in terms of extension, even if its conceptual difference proves the need of the possible in its existence for the complete efficient agent, thus it should be understood... to repel what was said: 'We do not accept that the cause of non-existence is non-existence, for among the causes of existent is the removal of the obstacle, so its existence is a cause of non-existence.' And what was said: 'The assumed possible is not an effect of something existent such that its non-existence is dependent on its non-existence, but rather it is an effect of the non-existence of the cause of non-existence, so its non-existence is dependent on its existence.' The permissibility of the occurrence of the possible with respect to its essence without need for another. But the possible does not deserve in its essence the occurrence of priority of one of its sides, so there is no purpose attached to it, because the possible, with this deserving and without it, needs in its two sides another, and thereby the inference to the existence of the Maker is completed.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector argues that priority of one side might be intrinsic and sufficient for occurrence without needing the absence of the opposite cause, using the analogy of necessity by a cause not contradicting equality in itself. The commentator responds by specifying that the priority in question is that which suffices for occurrence; if it depended on absence of the opposite cause, then that priority would not be intrinsic. He illustrates with existence: if existence were prior due to absence of the cause of non-existence, then that would suffice for existence without an efficient agent, leading to the denial of God. The answer then explains that the cause of non-existence is itself non-existence, because non-existences of effects depend on non-existences of their causes in a rational sense. The commentator clarifies that this dependence is intellectual, not external, and that the intrinsic priority of existence being conditional on the absence of the complete efficient agent actually proves the need for that agent. He then refutes an objection that the cause of non-existence could be an existent (like the removal of an obstacle). Finally, he concludes that the possible does not have a claim to priority of one side in itself, and the argument for the existence of the Maker is thereby established.

Correction: The passage clarifies that non-existence is caused by non-existence, not by existence. The removal of an obstacle is itself a non-existence (of the obstacle), and the dependence of non-existence on non-existence is rational, not external.

B) Because it would mean that non-existence can be a cause of existence.
C) Because it would dispense with the need for an efficient agent.
D) Because it introduces an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The possible's existence always requires a complete efficient cause; the absence of that cause is the cause of its non-existence, and this preserves the proof for God's existence.

Imagine a situation where you feel trapped, seeing no way out. This lesson teaches that non-existence has a cause. What does that imply about your hope in Allah's power to bring existence from non-existence?
?
The objection in this lesson arises from a desire to avoid acknowledging a Creator. In what areas of your life do you subtly resist acknowledging your complete dependence on Allah? What concrete step can you take today to change that?

Resolving the Objection: Non-Existence as Cause
The objection and its answer regarding non-existence as a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that non-existence can be a cause of existence is resolved through the distinction that non-existence is a cause of non-existence, not of existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence never causes existence; it is only removed.

The objector attempts to undermine the previous argument by claiming that the absence of an obstacle (a non-existence) is part of the cause of existence. The commentator clarifies that non-existence is only a cause of non-existence, and that existence occurs precisely by the removal of non-existence. This clarification is essential for the subsequent proof of the Maker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection carefully: the objector argues that absence of an obstacle (a non-existence) is part of the complete cause of existence, so non-existence can be a cause of existence.
The objector quotes the earlier claim 'the cause of non-existence is non-existence' and challenges it.
2
Examine the first response
Notice that a weak response is mentioned: that the insufficiency of non-existence is known by intuition. This is not the final answer.
The commentator immediately corrects this with a more precise answer.
3
Understand the commentator's final answer
The commentator explains that the earlier statement did not limit causes of non-existence to non-existence, but affirmed that non-existence is among them. Existence is realized by removing causes of non-existence, and the non-existence of non-existence is existence.
This preserves the principle that non-existence is never a cause of existence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( قلنا سبب العدم عدم إلخ ) فإن قلت : سبب العدم قد يكون وجوداً، فإن عدم المانع جزء من علة الوجود، فعدم هذا العدم أعني وجود المانع علة العدم قطعاً. فحينئذ إذا كان ذات الممكن اقتضى الوجود مع عدم المانع ،فقط كان ما يتوقف عليه الوجود الذات والعدم، ولزم المحذور، فالأولى أن يجاب بأن عدم كفاية العدم في الوجود قد علم بالبديهة السابقة المشتركة بين الصبيان والمجانين والحيوانات قلت ليس مراده أن سبب العدم منحصر في العدم، بل أن العدم من أسباب العدم قطعاً، فالوجود إنما يتحقق بانتفاء أسباب العدم التي من جملتها عدم جزء العلة التامة للوجود وعدم العدم وجود، فيحصل المطلوب. وهو استناد الممكن إلى مؤثر موجود وكون العالم دليلاً على الصانع، إذ ليس وجود ذلك المؤثر لذاته لما سبق بعينه ولا يتسلسل، فتعين الانتهاء إلى الواجب تعالى والشبهة إنما ترد إذا ثبت في مادة انحصار علة العدم في المانع إذ لو تحقق العلة التامة لم يتحقق العدم مطلقاً، وهذا ضروري على أن المصنف سيذكر أن عدم المانع كاشف عن شرط وجودي البتة.
His statement: 'We said: The cause of non-existence is non-existence, etc.' For if you say: The cause of non-existence may be existence, for the absence of the obstacle is part of the cause of existence, so the non-existence of this non-existence, i.e., the existence of the obstacle, is a cause of non-existence definitively. Then if the essence of the possible necessitated existence with the absence of the obstacle only, what existence depends on would be essence and non-existence, and the problematic consequence would follow. So it is better to respond by saying that the insufficiency of non-existence for existence is known by the preceding intuition shared by children, the insane, and animals. I say: He does not mean that the cause of non-existence is limited to non-existence, but rather that non-existence is definitively among the causes of non-existence. For existence is only realized by the removal of the causes of non-existence, among which is the absence of part of the complete cause of existence, and the non-existence of non-existence is existence. Thus the desired result is obtained, namely that the possible depends on an existent efficient agent, and that the world is evidence for the Maker, since the existence of that agent is not for its own essence due to what has preceded exactly, nor does it regress infinitely, so it is determined to terminate at the Almighty Necessary. The doubt only arises if it is established in a particular case that the cause of non-existence is restricted to the obstacle, for if the complete cause were realized, non-existence would not occur absolutely. And this is necessary, given that the author will mention that the absence of the obstacle is a revealer of an existential condition, definitively.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer that concludes the argument for the existence of the Necessary Being. The objector argues that non-existence of an obstacle (i.e., removal of a hindrance) is a cause of existence, so non-existence can cause existence, undermining the previous claim that non-existence is only a cause of non-existence. The commentator responds by clarifying that the previous statement did not limit causes of non-existence to non-existence, but affirmed that non-existence is among them. He then explains that existence occurs by removing causes of non-existence, and that the non-existence of non-existence is existence. Thus, the possible's dependence on an existent efficient agent is proven, and the world points to the Maker. The argument then shows that this agent cannot be self-existent (due to previous proofs) and cannot regress infinitely, so it must end in the Necessary Being. The doubt raised by the objector only applies if the cause of non-existence is exclusively the obstacle, but in reality the complete cause of existence includes positive conditions, so the absence of the obstacle is merely a revealer of an existential condition, not a cause by itself.

Key Terms
سبب العدم
مانع
علة تامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the absence of an obstacle (a non-existence) is a direct cause of existence.
Correction: The absence of an obstacle is merely a condition; the true efficient cause is an existent agent. Non-existence never causes existence directly.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator's final answer, what is the role of the absence of an obstacle in existence?
A) It is a direct cause of existence.
B) It is a cause of non-existence that must be removed.
C) It is the complete cause of existence.
D) It is irrelevant to existence.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence never causes existence; it is only a cause of non-existence, and existence is the removal of non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you blamed your lack of spiritual progress on something that was absent (e.g., no good teacher, no time). Does this lesson challenge your assumption that removing that absence alone would bring progress? How does the passage redefine what truly causes existence?
?
The passage teaches that non-existence never causes existence—it is only removed. Examine your own heart: Are you waiting for a sin to 'push' you toward repentance, as if non-existence (the absence of repentance) will somehow cause a change? What must you actually remove for repentance to exist?
?
The objector confused the absence of an obstacle with a cause. In your daily life, do you treat the absence of problems as the cause of peace? Write down one example where you can now see that peace comes not from removing negatives but from actively establishing something positive.

Conclusion: The World Points to the Maker
The conclusion of the proof for the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument concludes that the possible depends on an existent efficient agent, who cannot be self-caused nor part of an infinite regress, thus must be the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The world's dependence leads inevitably to the Necessary Being.

Having resolved the objection about non-existence, the commentator now draws the final conclusion: the possible relies on an existent efficient agent. This agent cannot be self-existent (due to previous proofs) nor can the chain regress infinitely, so it must terminate in the Almighty Necessary Being. This completes the proof of the Maker.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conclusion of dependence
The commentator states that the possible depends on an existent efficient agent, and the world is evidence for the Maker.
This follows from the previous analysis that existence is realized by an existent agent.
2
Examine why the agent cannot be self-sufficient
The agent's existence is not for its own essence 'due to what has preceded' – referring to earlier proofs that nothing can cause its own existence.
This eliminates self-causation.
3
Examine why there is no infinite regress
The chain of causes cannot go on infinitely; it must terminate at a first cause.
This is a standard argument against infinite regress.
4
Identify the endpoint: the Necessary Being
The argument terminates at the Almighty Necessary Being (Allah).
This is the final conclusion of the proof.
5
Note the final remark about the doubt
The doubt raised earlier only applies if the cause of non-existence is restricted to the obstacle, but in reality the complete cause includes positive conditions, so the absence of an obstacle is merely a revealer of an existential condition.
This reinforces that the objection does not undermine the proof.

?

?

In your spiritual life, do you think that merely avoiding sins is enough without actively seeking the positive cause of God's guidance? What condition might you be neglecting?

The Argument from Absence of Cause of Non-Existence
Initial argument and objection about causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument that absence of the cause of non-existence could make existence preponderant, and the objection that this would close the proof of God.

Core Idea
Absence alone cannot replace an efficient cause for existence.

Sanusi begins by presenting a potential argument: if we add the absence of the cause of non-existence to a possible entity, existence becomes preponderant. This might seem to dispense with the need for an efficient cause, which would undermine the proof of God. This lesson sets up the problem that the rest of the passage will resolve.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the argument is presented as a conditional: if absence of cause of non-existence is added, then existence occurs.
The passage also includes the objection that this would make the proof of God impossible.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has two clear parts: the argument (from 'انضمام' to 'الطرف الراجح') and the objection (from 'فيكفي' to 'الصانع').
The objection is introduced by 'وإنه' which signals the consequence.
3
Understand the implication
The objector claims that if absence of cause of non-existence suffices, then we no longer need an efficient cause. This would destroy the standard proof for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انضمام عدم علة الطرف الآخر إليه، فلنفرض أن ذلك الطرف هو الوجود، فيصير أولى بسب انضمام عدم علة العدم إلى ذات الممكن ولا استحالة في وقوع الطرف الراجح، فيكفي في وقوع الوجود عدم سبب العدم منضماً إلى ذات الممكن وإنه أي ما ذكر من كون عدم سبب العدم كافياً في وجود الممكن يعني عن وجود المؤثر في الممكنات الموجودة فينسد باب إثبات وجود الصانع. قلنا: سبب العدم عدم لأن أعدام المعلولات مستندة إلى أعدام عللها، فعدمه أي عدم قوله: مستندة إلى أعدام عللها أي التامة بمعنى فواعلها المستجمعة لشرائط التأثير استناداً عقلياً، بمعنى أن العقل إذا لاحظ صدور شيء عن مؤثر تام حكم أن عدمه يوجب عدم ذلك الشيء، سواء كان عدم ذلك المؤثر بعدم نفسه أو بعدم شرط من شرائط تأثيره، لا استنادا خارجياً إذ لا تمايز في الأعدام في الخارج حتى يتصور استناد بعضها إلى بعض فيه، فاستناد العدم إلى العدم فرع استناد الوجود إلى الوجود فإذا كانت الأولوية الذاتية للوجود موقوفة على عدم المؤثر التام الذي هو وجوده بحسب الصدق وإن كان مغايرته في المفهوم يثبت احتياج الممكن في وجوده إلى المؤثر التام، هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا الكلام ليندفع ما قيل: لا نسلم أن سبب العدم عدم فإن من جملة علة الموجود انتفاء المانع فوجوده يكون علة العدم وما قيل أن الممكن المفروض ليس معلولاً لموجود حتى يكون عدمه مستنداً إلى عدمه، بل هو معلول لعدم سبب العدم، فيكون عدمه مستنداً إلى وجوده. جواز وقوع الممكن نظراً إلى ذاته من غير احتياج إلى غيره. وأما أن الممكن لا يستحق في ذاته حصول أولوية أحد طرفيه فلا يتعلق به غرض لأن الممكن مع هذا الاستحقاق وبدونه يحتاج في طرفيه إلى غيره، وبذلك يتم الاستدلال على وجود الصانع. قوله: قلنا سبب العدم عدم إلخ فإن قلت: سبب العدم قد يكون وجوداً، فإن عدم المانع جزء من علة الوجود، فعدم هذا العدم أعني وجود المانع علة العدم قطعاً. فحينئذ إذا كان ذات الممكن اقتضى الوجود مع عدم المانع فقط كان ما يتوقف عليه الوجود الذات والعدم، ولزم المحذور، فالأولى أن يجاب بأن عدم كفاية العدم في الوجود قد علم بالبديهة السابقة المشتركة بين الصبيان والمجانين والحيوانات. قلت ليس مراده أن سبب العدم منحصر في العدم، بل أن العدم من أسباب العدم قطعاً، فالوجود إنما يتحقق بانتفاء أسباب العدم التي من جملتها عدم جزء العلة التامة للوجود وعدم العدم وجود، فيحصل المطلوب. وهو استناد الممكن إلى مؤثر موجود وكون العالم دليلاً على الصانع، إذ ليس وجود ذلك المؤثر لذاته لما سبق بعينه ولا يتسلسل، فتعين الانتهاء إلى الواجب تعالى والشبهة إنما ترد إذا ثبت في مادة انحصار علة العدم في المانع إذ لو تحقق العلة التامة لم يتحقق العدم مطلقاً، وهذا ضروري على أن المصنف سيذكر أن عدم المانع كاشف عن شرط وجودي البتة وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما قيل: من أن الممكن المفروض ليس معلوماً لشيء حتى يكون عدمه مستنداً إلى عدمه. سبب العدم وجود؛ لأن عدم العدم وجود قطعاً، ويحصل المطلوب وهو استناد وجود الممكن إلى مؤثر موجود، وكون العالم دالاً على وجود الصانع.
The joining of the absence of the cause of the other side to it. So let us suppose that that side is existence. Then it becomes more preponderant due to the joining of the absence of the cause of non-existence to the essence of the possible, and there is no impossibility in the occurrence of the preponderant side. So the absence of the cause of non-existence is sufficient for the occurrence of existence when joined to the essence of the possible. And that is, what was mentioned, that the absence of the cause of non-existence being sufficient for the existence of the possible dispenses with the existence of an efficient cause in existing possibles, thus the door of proving the existence of the Maker is closed. We said: the cause of non-existence is non-existence, because the non-existences of effects are dependent on the non-existences of their causes. So its non-existence, i.e., the non-existence of... He said: 'dependent on the non-existences of their causes' meaning the complete causes, i.e., their efficient agents that have gathered the conditions of effect, with a rational dependence, meaning that the intellect, when it observes the emanation of something from a complete efficient cause, judges that its non-existence necessitates the non-existence of that thing, whether that non-existence of the efficient cause is by its own non-existence or by the non-existence of a condition of its effect, not an external dependence, since there is no distinction among non-existences in the external world such that dependence of some on others could be imagined therein. Thus the dependence of non-existence on non-existence is a branch of the dependence of existence on existence. So if the essential preponderance of existence is dependent on the non-existence of the complete efficient cause, which is its existence according to truth [صدق], even if its conceptual difference establishes the need of the possible for the complete efficient cause. This is how this statement should be understood to rebut what was said: we do not concede that the cause of non-existence is non-existence, for among the causes of existence is the absence of the impediment, so its existence is the cause of non-existence. And what was said: that the supposed possible is not an effect of something existent such that its non-existence would be dependent on its non-existence; rather it is an effect of the absence of the cause of non-existence, so its non-existence is dependent on its existence. The permissibility of the occurrence of the possible in view of its essence without need of another. As for the possible not deserving in its essence the acquisition of preponderance of one of its two sides, that does not relate to the purpose, because the possible, with this deserving and without it, needs in its two sides another, and by that the inference to the existence of the Maker is completed. He said: we said the cause of non-existence is non-existence, etc. For if you say: the cause of non-existence may be existence, for the absence of the impediment is part of the cause of existence, so the non-existence of this non-existence, i.e., the existence of the impediment, is a cause of non-existence certainly. Then when the essence of the possible requires existence with the absence of the impediment only, what existence depends on would be the essence and non-existence, and the problem follows. So it is better to answer that the insufficiency of non-existence for existence is known by the prior self-evidence shared by children, madmen, and animals. I said: his intention is not that the cause of non-existence is confined to non-existence, but that non-existence is certainly among the causes of non-existence. So existence only occurs by the removal of the causes of non-existence, among which is the non-existence of a part of the complete cause of existence, and the non-existence of non-existence is existence. Thus the desired conclusion is obtained: namely, the dependence of the possible on an existent efficient cause, and the world being a proof of the Maker, since the existence of that efficient cause is not from its essence according to what preceded, nor does it regress infinitely, so termination upon the Necessary (exalted) is determined. And the doubt only arises if it is established that the cause of non-existence is confined to the impediment, for if the complete cause were realized, non-existence would not occur at all. This is necessary, since the author will mention that the absence of the impediment reveals a existential condition absolutely. And by what we have mentioned, what was said is repelled: that the supposed possible is not an effect of anything such that its non-existence would be dependent on its non-existence. The cause of non-existence is existence, because the non-existence of non-existence is existence certainly, and the desired conclusion is obtained: namely, the dependence of the existence of the possible on an existent efficient cause, and the world being indicative of the existence of the Maker.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a complex kalam argument about the relationship between existence, non-existence, and causality, specifically addressing whether the absence of a cause of non-existence can suffice for existence without an efficient cause. The argument begins by considering a possible entity: if we join the absence of the cause of the opposite side (non-existence) to the possible, then existence becomes preponderant. The objector claims that if absence of cause of non-existence is sufficient for existence, then we no longer need an efficient cause, destroying the proof of God. The commentator replies by clarifying that the cause of non-existence is indeed non-existence, because non-existences of effects depend on non-existences of their complete causes. He explains that this dependence is rational, not external, and that the intellect judges that non-existence of the cause necessitates non-existence of the effect. This shows that the possible still depends on a complete efficient cause, because even if absence of one cause suffices, the need for a positive cause remains. Then the commentator addresses a further objection: that the cause of non-existence can be existence (e.g., the existence of an impediment causes non-existence of an effect). He responds by stating that non-existence is not confined to non-existence as a cause, but non-existence is certainly among the causes of non-existence. The desired conclusion is that the possible depends on an existent efficient cause, and the world is a proof of the Maker. The argument thus upholds the need for a necessitating cause for existence, avoiding the implication that absence alone suffices.

Key Terms
سبب العدم
عدم
وجود
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the absence of the cause of non-existence alone is sufficient for existence, eliminating the need for an efficient cause.
Correction: The text presents this as an objection to be refuted. The author will argue that such absence actually presupposes a complete efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what would be the consequence if absence of cause of non-existence were sufficient for existence?
A) The possible would not need a cause.
B) The proof of God's existence would be closed.
C) The possible would be eternal.
D) The cause of non-existence would be existence.

Key Takeaway
The objection claims that absence of cause of non-existence alone would suffice for existence, which would undermine the proof of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the possibility that the universe might not need a Creator because the absence of a cause of non-existence is 'sufficient', does your heart tremble at the thought that your entire faith might be based on a flawed argument? Are you truly convinced that this objection is false, not just intellectually but with your whole being, and what difference would that conviction make in your daily life?
?
If the objection presented here were true and the door to proving God were closed, would you still believe in Him? What does your answer reveal about the foundation of your faith—is it built on rational proof or something else? And if rational proof is important to you, how would you respond to someone who uses this very objection to dismiss your belief in a Creator?
?
This passage raises the question: Can the absence of a cause ever be sufficient for anything to come into existence? In your own life, have you ever acted as if the absence of obstacles is all that is needed to achieve something, ignoring your own responsibility or dependence on God? How does this lesson challenge you to recognize your utter need for God as the active, efficient cause of your existence and every good in your life?

Allah's Existence

8 min
Reply: Cause of Non-Existence is Non-Existence
Reply establishing that non-existence is the cause of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reply that the cause of non-existence is non-existence, and how this rebuts the objection by showing that the possible still depends on a complete efficient cause.

Core Idea
Non-existences depend on non-existences of their complete causes.

The commentator now answers the objection. He asserts that the cause of non-existence is non-existence itself, because non-existences of effects are rationally dependent on non-existences of their complete causes. This establishes that the possible still requires an efficient cause, because the absence of the cause of non-existence is itself dependent on the existence of a complete cause. The proof of God remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the reply
The reply begins with 'قلنا: سبب العدم عدم' and extends to the end of the passage. Read it carefully, noting the key distinction between rational dependence and external dependence.
Pay attention to the explanation of 'استناداً عقلياً'.
2
Understand the principle
The commentator states that cause of non-existence is non-existence because non-existences of effects depend on non-existences of their causes. This dependence is rational, not external.
3
Apply the principle to the objection
This shows that the absence of cause of non-existence is not something independent; it is contingent on the absence of a cause. Therefore, the possible still needs a complete efficient cause, which preserves the proof of God.
4
Confirm the conclusion
The passage ends with 'وبذلك يتم الاستدلال على وجود الصانع' indicating that the reply successfully defends the inference to God's existence.

?

Further Clarification: Cause of Non-Existence Can Be Existence
Addressing an objection about the cause of non-existence being existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that cause of non-existence can be existence (e.g., existence of an impediment) and the answer that this does not undermine the argument, as non-existence is still a cause and existence requires removal of all causes of non-existence.

Core Idea
Existence occurs only when all causes of non-existence, including absence of parts of the complete cause, are removed.

A further objection is raised: the cause of non-existence could be existence, such as the existence of an impediment. The commentator answers by clarifying that his point is not that non-existence is the only cause, but that non-existence is certainly among the causes. Existence occurs only when all causes of non-existence are removed, which includes the absence of parts of the complete cause. This reaffirms that the possible depends on an existent efficient cause, leading to the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The objection starts with 'فإن قلت: سبب العدم قد يكون وجوداً' and the answer follows. Read both parts.
The objector gives the example of the absence of an impediment being part of the cause of existence.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says that if the absence of an impediment is part of the cause, then its non-existence (i.e., existence of impediment) is a cause of non-existence. So the cause of non-existence can be existence.
3
Grasp the answer
The commentator replies that he does not claim that non-existence is the only cause, but that it is certainly a cause. Existence requires the removal of all causes of non-existence, including the absence of part of the complete cause. This means that the possible still requires a complete cause, and thus the world points to a Creator.
4
Confirm the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that the doubt only arises if the cause of non-existence is confined to the impediment, but the complete cause prevents non-existence entirely.

The joining of the absence of the cause of the other side to it. So let us suppose that that side is existence. So the absence of the cause of non-existence is sufficient for the occurrence of existence when joined to the essence of the possible. And that is, what was mentioned, that the absence of the cause of non-existence being sufficient for the existence of the possible dispenses with the existence of an efficient cause in existing possibles, thus the door of proving the existence of the Maker is closed. We said: the cause of non-existence is non-existence, because the non-existences of effects are dependent on the non-existences of their causes. So its non-existence, i.e., the non-existence of... He said: 'dependent on the non-existences of their causes' meaning the complete causes, i.e., their efficient agents that have gathered the conditions of effect, with a rational dependence, meaning that the intellect, when it observes the emanation of something from a complete efficient cause, judges that its non-existence necessitates the non-existence of that thing, whether that non-existence of the efficient cause is by its own non-existence or by the non-existence of a condition of its effect, not an external dependence, since there is no distinction among non-existences in the external world such that dependence of some on others could be imagined therein. Thus the dependence of non-existence on non-existence is a branch of the dependence of existence on existence. So if the essential preponderance of existence is dependent on the non-existence of the complete efficient cause, which is its existence according to truth [صدق], even if its conceptual difference establishes the need of the possible for the complete efficient cause. This is how this statement should be understood to rebut what was said: we do not concede that the cause of non-existence is non-existence, for among the causes of existence is the absence of the impediment, so its existence is the cause of non-existence. And what was said: that the supposed possible is not an effect of something existent such that its non-existence would be dependent on its non-existence; rather it is an effect of the absence of the cause of non-existence, so its non-existence is dependent on its existence. The permissibility of the occurrence of the possible in view of its essence without need of another. As for the possible not deserving in its essence the acquisition of preponderance of one of its two sides, that does not relate to the purpose, because the possible, with this deserving and without it, needs in its two sides another, and by that the inference to the existence of the Maker is completed. Then when the essence of the possible requires existence with the absence of the impediment only, what existence depends on would be the essence and non-existence, and the problem follows. So it is better to answer that the insufficiency of non-existence for existence is known by the prior self-evidence shared by children, madmen, and animals. I said: his intention is not that the cause of non-existence is confined to non-existence, but that non-existence is certainly among the causes of non-existence. So existence only occurs by the removal of the causes of non-existence, among which is the non-existence of a part of the complete cause of existence, and the non-existence of non-existence is existence. Thus the desired conclusion is obtained: namely, the dependence of the possible on an existent efficient cause, and the world being a proof of the Maker, since the existence of that efficient cause is not from its essence according to what preceded, nor does it regress infinitely, so termination upon the Necessary (exalted) is determined. And the doubt only arises if it is established that the cause of non-existence is confined to the impediment, for if the complete cause were realized, non-existence would not occur at all. This is necessary, since the author will mention that the absence of the impediment reveals a existential condition absolutely. And by what we have mentioned, what was said is repelled: that the supposed possible is not an effect of anything such that its non-existence would be dependent on its non-existence. The cause of non-existence is existence, because the non-existence of non-existence is existence certainly, and the desired conclusion is obtained: namely, the dependence of the existence of the possible on an existent efficient cause, and the world being indicative of the existence of the Maker.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a complex kalam argument about the relationship between existence, non-existence, and causality, specifically addressing whether the absence of a cause of non-existence can suffice for existence without an efficient cause. The argument begins by considering a possible entity: if we join the absence of the cause of the opposite side (non-existence) to the possible, then existence becomes preponderant. The objector claims that if absence of cause of non-existence is sufficient for existence, then we no longer need an efficient cause, destroying the proof of God. The commentator replies by clarifying that the cause of non-existence is indeed non-existence, because non-existences of effects depend on non-existences of their complete causes. He explains that this dependence is rational, not external, and that the intellect judges that non-existence of the cause necessitates non-existence of the effect. This shows that the possible still depends on a complete efficient cause, because even if absence of one cause suffices, the need for a positive cause remains. Then the commentator addresses a further objection: that the cause of non-existence can be existence (e.g., the existence of an impediment causes non-existence of an effect). He responds by stating that non-existence is not confined to non-existence as a cause, but non-existence is certainly among the causes of non-existence. The desired conclusion is that the possible depends on an existent efficient cause, and the world is a proof of the Maker. The argument thus upholds the need for a necessitating cause for existence, avoiding the implication that absence alone suffices.

Why a Cause Must Necessitate
Al-Jurjani presents a reductio argument against mere priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a cause that only produces priority (أولوية) without necessity is insufficient to bring a possible being into existence.

Core Idea
A cause that only makes something probable cannot explain why it exists now rather than later.

Al-Jurjani now turns to a critical question: does a cause need to make its effect necessary, or is it enough for it to give a 'priority' or preference? This lesson unpacks his reductio argument showing that priority alone leads to absurdity. We will follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if priority were sufficient, then...'
The argument uses a supposition to derive a contradiction.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'الأولوية' (priority) and 'الوجوب' (necessity). The argument hinges on the difference between making something merely more likely and making it necessary.
3
Follow the reductio
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: either the specification of time is due to a preponderator or not. Both lead to a contradiction of the initial assumption.
The assumption was that priority without necessity is sufficient.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why must a cause necessitate? What would happen if a cause only made its effect probable? This argument grounds the principle of sufficient reason.
This is a foundational argument in kalam for the necessity of the causal connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : أي مر. ثالث تلك الأبحاث ( أن الممكن لاحتياجه إلى (العلة ) االمؤثرة في وجوده لما ) وكون الأولوية ( الناشئة من تلك العلة إذا لم تصل إلى حد الوجوب ( غير كافية ) في وقوعه لأنه إذا صار الوجود بسبب تلك العلة أولى بلا وجوب، وكان ذلك كافياً في وقوعه، فلنفرض مع تلك الأولوية الوجود في وقت والعدم في وقت آخر، فإن لم يكن اختصاص أحد الوقتين بالوجود لمرجح لم يوجد في الآخر لزم ترجح أحد المتساويين بلا سبب، وإن كان لمرجح لم تكن الأولوية الشاملة للوقتين كافية للوقوع، والمقدر خلافه .
And the third of them: i.e., [something]. The third of those researches is that the possible, due to its need for the efficient cause for its existence, and that the priority arising from that cause, if it does not reach the limit of necessity, is insufficient for its occurrence. For if existence becomes, by that cause, more likely without necessity, and that were sufficient for its occurrence, then let us suppose along with that priority existence at one time and non-existence at another time. Then if the specification of one of the two times for existence is not due to a preponderator, and it does not exist in the other, then a preponderance of one of two equals without a cause would be necessary. And if it is due to a preponderator, then the priority inclusive of the two times would not be sufficient for occurrence, but the assumption is the opposite.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage states the third research regarding the possible in itself. The author argues that the possible requires an efficient cause for its existence, and that the priority (أولوية) produced by that cause, if it does not reach the level of necessity (وجوب), is insufficient to bring the possible into existence. The argument proceeds by a supposition: if existence becomes more likely due to the cause but not necessary, and if that priority were sufficient, then we could imagine that the possible exists at one time and does not exist at another time, while the cause remains the same. If the specific time of existence is not due to a preponderator (مرجح), then this would be a preponderance of one of two equals without a cause, which is impossible. If it is due to a preponderator, then the priority that was supposed to be sufficient (and common to both times) is not actually sufficient, because the preponderator is what determines the specific time, not the priority. Thus the assumption that priority alone is sufficient leads to a contradiction. The phrase 'والمقدر خلافه' means 'the assumed is the opposite,' i.e., the assumption that priority is sufficient is false.

Key Terms
الممكن لذاته
الأولوية
الوجوب
المرجح
ترجح أحد المتساويين
العلة التامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause can bring about its effect by making it merely more probable or preferable, without necessitating it.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that if a cause only gives priority without necessity, the effect could still be undetermined in time, leading to an impossible preponderance without a cause. Hence, the cause must necessitate.

Checkpoint
In the argument from the third research, what is the consequence if priority (أولوية) without necessity were sufficient for the possible's existence?
A) The possible would exist at all times.
B) It would lead to preponderance of one equal over another without a cause.
C) The cause would be unnecessary.
D) The possible would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
A cause must necessitate its effect; otherwise, the effect's occurrence in one moment rather than another would be inexplicable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat your reasons for decisions as merely making an outcome 'more likely,' or as necessitating your choice? Al-Jurjani's argument suggests that without necessity, outcomes are arbitrary. Where in your life have you settled for probability when only certainty could give you peace?
?
This argument forces you to face the question: if God's causality necessitates every event, then where is your free will? Do you believe that your choices are necessitated by God's will, or do you find a way to reconcile the two? Be honest—are you truly comfortable with the answer, or do you avoid the question?
?
If nothing in your life happens without a necessitating cause, then every trial you face is precisely determined. How does this change the way you approach suffering? Instead of asking 'why me?', what would it mean for you to see every difficulty as exactly what must be, and to trust the One who necessitated it?

First Proof: Possibility Must Be Necessary for Essence
Al-Jurjani presents a proof by contradiction defending the necessity of possibility for essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that possibility is necessary to the essence of a contingent being, and why assuming otherwise leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
If possibility were accidental, it would lead to infinite regress or atheism.

The first proof in this section tackles a crucial premise: that possibility is not just an accidental property but is necessary to the essence of a contingent being. Al-Jurjani constructs a contradiction argument that shows rejecting this necessity leads to either an infinite chain of possibilities or the denial of a Creator. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall structure of the proof.
The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The argument presents two outcomes if possibility is accidental: either an infinite regress or the denial of the Maker. Locate these in the text.
These correspond to 'تسلسل' and 'نفي الصانع'.
3
Understand why both are absurd
Think about why infinite regress is impossible in kalam and why uncaused events undermine the proof for God's existence.
Infinite regress violates the principle of causation; uncaused events negate the need for a Creator.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Realize that the only way to avoid both absurdities is to affirm that possibility is necessary to the essence.
This establishes the lemma needed for later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يحتج عليه أي على لزوم الإمكان لماهية الممكن بأن الإمكان إن لم يكن لازماً لها بل حادثاً فنقول: إن حدوث الإمكان لها واتصافها به إما أن يكون لأمر يقتضي ذلك الاتصاف وهو ممكن؛ لحدوثه بهذا الاعتبار، واستناده إلى الغير، فيكون للإمكان إمكان فتتسلسل الإمكانات إلى غير النهاية أو لا يكون حدوث الإمكان لها لأمر يقتضيه فيلزم نفي الصانع أي لا يثبت وجوده؛ لجواز حدوث الحوادث حينئذ من غير استناد إلى شيء يقتضيها.
And perhaps one may argue for it, i.e., for the necessity of possibility (imkan) to the essence (mahiyya) of the contingent (mumkin), that if possibility is not necessary to it but rather accidental (hadith), then we say: its occurrence (huduth) to it and its being described by it is either due to something that necessitates that description, and it (i.e., that possibility) is contingent; because of its occurrence in this respect and its being dependent on another, so there would be a possibility for the possibility, and thus possibilities would regress ad infinitum; or its occurrence to it is not due to something that necessitates it, resulting in the denial of the Maker (nafy al-sani'), i.e., His existence is not established; because it would permit occurrences of events then without reliance on anything that necessitates them.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction for the claim that possibility (imkan) is necessary (lazim) to the essence (mahiyya) of a contingent being. The argument begins by assuming the opposite: that possibility is not necessary but rather accidental (hadith). If it is accidental, then its occurrence requires a cause. If that cause is something else, then the resulting possibility is itself contingent, requiring another possibility, leading to an infinite regress of possibilities. If, on the other hand, the occurrence is uncaused, then the existence of events without a cause would be possible, which would deny the necessity of a Maker (sani') and thus negate the proof of the Creator's existence. Both outcomes are absurd, so the assumption is false; therefore possibility must be necessary to the essence.

Key Terms
إمكان
ماهية
لازم
حادث
تسلسل
نفي الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is an accidental attribute that could be absent from a contingent essence.
Correction: If possibility were accidental, it would require a cause, leading to infinite regress or uncaused events, both of which are impossible. Hence, possibility must be necessary to essence.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof conclude if possibility is not necessary to the essence?
A) It is possible to have a world without a Creator.
B) It leads either to infinite regress or denial of the Creator.
C) Possibility becomes impossible.
D) Essence becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
Possibility must be necessary to the essence of a contingent being; otherwise, either infinite regress or the denial of the Creator follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument forces you to admit that your very possibility of existing must be essential to your nature. If that is true, then you were not meant to be nothing. How does grounding your existence in necessity change the way you see your life’s purpose and the One who gave you that essence?
?
If possibility were accidental, your existence could have been random. Are there areas of your life where you live as if your existence is an accident, rather than a necessary sign pointing to the Creator? How can you realign your daily thoughts to reflect that both your being and your potential are held by the Necessary Being?
?
The proof eliminates the possibility of uncaused events. In your life, where have you taken credit for good outcomes as if they were uncaused? How can you use this argument to increase your reliance on Allah and gratitude for every moment of existence?

The Eternal Does Not Depend on the Free Agent: Opening the Fifth Section
Opening of the fifth section stating the first investigation: the eternal does not depend on the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first investigation of the eternal (al-qadīm) according to the theologians: that it does not originate from the free agent, and how this contrasts with the philosophical view.

Core Idea
The eternal is not an effect of the free agent — agreed by theologians, debated by philosophers.

This passage opens the fifth major section of the work, dedicated to the investigations of the eternal (al-qadīm). Before delving into detailed proofs, the author states the first principle: the eternal does not depend on the free agent. This is presented as a point of agreement among theologians, but a philosophical counterexample is noted. Let's read the original text and unpack its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage once, noting the structure: the section title, the two investigations, and the first one.
The passage includes both the original matn and a short commentary.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that the eternal does not depend on the free agent, and that this is agreed upon by theologians.
Look for the phrase 'اتفاقاً من المتكلمين'.
3
Understand the commentator's note
The commentary explains that 'refer back to them' is an interpretation, then contrasts the philosophers' view that the eternal can be an effect of the free agent (example: celestial spheres).
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.
4
Reflect on the significance
This sets up a foundational doctrine of kalam: that God's eternal attributes are not caused by Him, and that the philosophical view will be refuted later.

Source Text

The Fifth Intention: On the Investigations of the Eternal. (The investigations of the eternal are two matters) i.e., they refer back to them. (One of them is that the eternal does not depend on the powerful free agent) i.e., it is not an effect proceeding from him (by agreement) of the theologians. Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth section of the work, titled 'On the Investigations of the Eternal'. The original text (matn) states that these investigations revolve around two points: first, that the eternal (qadīm) does not originate from the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār)—meaning it is not an effect proceeding from him—and this is agreed upon by the theologians. The commentator then explains that the phrase 'refer back to them' is an interpretation, indicating that the apparent claim of 'two matters' is not straightforward. He then notes that the statement 'by agreement' extends to theologians and others, but the philosopher al-ʾUstādh al-Muḥaqqiq in al-Dhakhīra pointed out that the philosophers consider the eternal to be an effect of the free agent, as they hold the motion of each celestial sphere to be eternal yet volitional. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is to set up the theological consensus against the philosophical view.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The eternal being independent of the free agent means it is uncreated (i.e., not created at all).
Correction: The passage is about logical dependence: the eternal does not proceed from the free agent as an effect. This does not necessarily mean it is uncreated, but rather that it is not caused by God's free will in the way temporal things are. The philosophical view that eternal motions are volitional shows that something can be eternal yet dependent on choice.

Key Takeaway
The theologians agree that the eternal does not depend on the free agent, while philosophers like those cited in al-Dhakhīra hold that eternal things can be effects of volition, setting up a key debate in kalam.

The Root of the Dispute: Necessitating or Free Agent?
Al-Rāzī identifies the root dispute as necessitating vs. free agent, and an objection is raised.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razī traces the dispute over the world's eternity to the nature of the divine agent, and the objection that theologians reverse the logical order.

Core Idea
The eternity debate hinges on whether God is a necessitating or free agent.

Al-Rāzī offers a crisp diagnosis of the philosophical-theological dispute: both sides agree the world is caused by God, but disagree on the modality of that causation. He argues that if God were a necessitating cause, the world would be eternal; if free, it must be originated. However, an objection points out that theologians do not reason from God's freedom to the world's origination — they do the opposite. This passage clarifies a foundational methodological divide.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the flow of the argument: first al-Rāzī's diagnosis, then the objection.
2
Identify al-Rāzī's claim
Al-Rāzī says the dispute is not about whether an eternal can be caused, but about whether the agent is necessitating (mūjib) or free (mukhtār). Underline that statement.
3
Understand the objection
The objection says that al-Rāzī's account implies theologians first prove God's freedom to prove the world's origination. But actually, they prove the world's origination first, then infer God's freedom.
4
Reflect on the direction of inference
Contrast the two logical directions: (1) agent attribute → world's state (al-Rāzī's presentation) vs. (2) world's state → agent attribute (actual theological method). Why does this difference matter?
This shows a methodological disagreement beyond just conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنزاعهم في قدم العالم وحدوثه مع كونه مستنداً إلى الله تعالى اتفاقاً، ليس مبنياً على أن الحكماء جوزوا استناد القديم إلى الفاعل فحكموا بأن العالم قديم ومع قدمه مستند إليه تعالى، وأن المتكلمين لم يجوزوا استناد القديم إلى الفاعل فحكموا بأن العالم حادث مستند إليه تعالى، بل هذا النزاع بينهم عائد إلى كون الفاعل الموجد للعالم موجباً أو مختاراً، حتى لو اتفقوا كلهم على أنه موجب، أو على أنه مختار لاتفقوا على قدم العالم على التقدير الأول، وعلى حدوثه على التقدير الثاني هكذا ذكره الإمام الرازي، ورد عليه بأنه يدل على أن المتكلمين بنوا مسألة الحدوث على مسألة الاختيار، وليس الأمر كذلك، بل بالعكس فإنهم استدلوا أولاً على كون العالم حادثاً من غير تعرض لفاعله أصلاً، فضلاً عن كونه مختاراً، ثم بنوا على حدوثه أن موجده يجب أن يكون مختاراً.
Thus their dispute over the eternity and origination of the world, while it is unanimously attributed to God, is not based on the philosophers allowing the attribution of the eternal to an agent, thus ruling that the world is eternal and with its eternity it is attributed to Him, and that the theologians did not allow the attribution of the eternal to an agent, thus ruling that the world is originated and attributed to Him. Rather, this dispute between them returns to whether the agent who brings the world into existence is necessitating or free. Even if they all agreed that He is necessitating, or that He is free, they would agree on the eternity of the world on the first supposition and on its origination on the second. Thus Imam al-Rāzī mentioned it, and it was objected to him that this indicates that the theologians based the question of origination on the question of freedom, but it is not so; rather, the opposite. They first argued that the world is originated without any reference to its agent at all, let alone His being free, then based on its origination they argued that its creator must be free.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage discusses the root of the disagreement between philosophers and theologians regarding the eternity or origination of the world. The author states that the disagreement is not about whether an eternal being can be caused by an agent (both sides agree the world is caused by God), but whether the agent is necessitating (mūjib) or free (mukhtār). If the agent were necessitating, the effect would be eternal; if free, the effect would be originated. This is attributed to al-Rāzī. However, an objection is raised: this presentation suggests that theologians based their proof of the world's origination on their proof of God's freedom. Actually, they prove the world's origination first, without any mention of the agent's attributes, and then from that origination they infer that the agent must be free. So the flow is from origination to freedom, not the reverse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theologians first prove that God is free, then infer the world is originated.
Correction: They first prove the world is originated, then infer that its creator must be free.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the world's eternity really comes down to whether God is a necessitating or free agent, but theologians start from the world's origination to prove God's freedom.

Defense of Theologians: From Origination to Freedom
Al-Ṭūsī's objection and a defense of the theologians' argument sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct logical sequence of the theologians' argument for God's freedom: they first prove the world's origination, and then deduce that its creator must be free.

Core Idea
The theologians prove origination first, then conclude freedom.

This passage clarifies a common misunderstanding about the kalam argument for God's freedom. Some critics claim that theologians assume God's freedom before proving the world's creation. Here, the author shows that the opposite is true: the world's origination is established first, and freedom follows as a necessary conclusion. Additionally, a brief note explains the implications of holding that need for a cause is due solely to origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the objection and the response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection and the defense
The first part of the passage describes an objection: that theologians built origination on freedom. Notice how the author refutes this by stating the reverse order.
The key phrase is 'بل بالعكس' (rather the opposite).
3
Trace the argument sequence
Follow the steps: (1) Prove the world is originated without referencing the agent. (2) Conclude that the agent must be free, because a necessitating agent would yield an eternal world, contradicting origination.
This shows the logical flow: origination → freedom.
4
Understand the note on the cause of need
The passage adds a clarification: if one holds that need for a cause is solely due to origination (or origination plus possibility), then the eternal (non-originated) can have no cause at all. Thus, such a person cannot consistently say the eternal is caused by a necessitating agent unless they shift to considering possibility alone.
This is a subtle point about the grounding of causal need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد عليه بأنه يدل على أن المتكلمين بنوا مسألة الحدوث على مسألة الاختيار، وليس الأمر كذلك، بل بالعكس فإنهم استدلوا أولاً على كون العالم حادثاً من غير تعرض لفاعله أصلاً، فضلاً عن كونه مختاراً، ثم بنوا على حدوثه أن موجده يجب أن يكون مختاراً؛ إذ لو كان موجباً لكان العالم قديماً، وهو باطل. واعلم أن القائل بأن علة الحاجة هي الحدوث وحده، أو مع الإمكان حقه أن يقول، إن القديم لا يستند إلى علة أصلاً، إذ لا حاجة له إلى مؤثر قطعاً، فلا يتصور منه القول بأن القديم يجوز استناده إلى الموجب إلا أن يتنزل من اعتبار الحدوث إلى اعتبار الإمكان وحده.
And it was objected to him that it indicates that the theologians based the question of origination on the question of freedom, but it is not so; rather, the opposite. They first argued that the world is originated without any reference to its agent at all, let alone His being free. Then, based on its origination, they argued that its creator must be free; because if He were necessitating, the world would be eternal, which is false. And know that the one who says that the cause of need is origination alone, or along with possibility, ought to say that the eternal does not have a cause at all, since it has absolutely no need for an agent. Thus, it cannot be imagined from him to say that the eternal can be attributed to the necessitating, unless he descends from considering origination to considering possibility alone.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage continues the response to al-Rāzī. It clarifies the correct sequence of the theologians' argument: first they prove the world is originated (ḥādiṯ) using proofs that do not mention God, then from that origination they conclude that its creator must be free (mukhtār), because if He were necessitating (mūjib), the world would be eternal, which contradicts the proven origination. The passage then adds an important note: those who hold that need (ḥājah) for a cause is solely due to origination (or origination plus possibility) must logically deny that an eternal being has any cause. Therefore, they cannot consistently say that an eternal can be caused by a necessitating agent, unless they shift from considering origination to considering possibility alone as the ground for need.

Key Takeaway
The theologians' proof for God's freedom is built on the prior proof of the world's origination, not the other way around.

The Theologians' Argument for Freedom from Origination
Theologians' argument from world's origination to creator's freedom, with a clarification on causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the theologians prove that God is free based on the observed origination of the world, and the logical steps that lead from contingency to a free creator.

Core Idea
The world's origination forces the conclusion that its creator is free, not necessitating.

Theologians have long argued that the very fact of the world's coming into existence points to a creator who acts with freedom. In this passage, the author presents a concise version of that argument: first establish that the world is originated, then infer that its cause must be free. He also clarifies a subtle point about what it means for something to need a cause. Let's read the passage and unpack its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and literal translation to get the overall sense of the argument.
2
Identify the two-part argument
Notice the argument has two stages: first proving the world's origination, then inferring freedom from that.
The first sentence covers both stages.
3
Understand the technical note
Pay attention to the note about the cause of need, which refines the premise that need requires origination.
This clarifies the logical basis for causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنهم استدلوا أولاً على كون العالم حادثاً من غير تعرض لفاعله أصلاً، فضلاً عن كونه مختاراً، ثم بنوا على حدوثه أن موجده يجب أن يكون مختاراً؛ إذ لو كان موجباً لكان العالم قديماً، وهو باطل. واعلم أن القائل بأن علة الحاجة هي الحدوث وحده، أو مع الإمكان حقه أن يقول، إن القديم لا يستند إلى علة أصلاً، إذ لا حاجة له إلى مؤثر قطعاً، فلا يتصور منه القول بأن القديم يجوز استناده إلى الموجب إلا أن يتنزل من اعتبار الحدوث إلى اعتبار الإمكان وحده.
For they argued first that the world is originated without any reference to its agent at all, let alone His being free. Then, based on its origination, they argued that its creator must be free; because if He were necessitating, the world would be eternal, which is false. And know that the one who says that the cause of need is origination alone, or along with possibility, ought to say that the eternal does not have a cause at all, since it has absolutely no need for an agent. Thus, it cannot be imagined from him to say that the eternal can be attributed to the necessitating, unless he descends from considering origination to considering possibility alone.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a concise statement of the standard kalām argument for God's free will (ikhtiyār) based on the contingency of the world. First, the theologians prove that the world is originated (ḥādiṯ) using proofs that do not rely on any attributes of the agent (e.g., the argument from accidents and change). Then, having established origination, they argue that the creator must be free, because if the agent were necessitating (mūjib), the world would be co-eternal with Him (qadīm), which contradicts its proven origination. The passage then adds a technical note about the basis of need for a cause: if need is solely due to origination (or origination plus possibility), then an eternal being cannot have any cause. Therefore, those who hold this view cannot coherently allow an eternal to be caused by a necessitating agent unless they adopt possibility (imkān) as the ground for need.

Key Terms
حدوث
اختيار
موجب
علة الحاجة
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument for God's freedom depends on a prior assumption about God's attributes.
Correction: The argument follows solely from the proven origination of the world; no assumption about God's nature is needed prior.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The world's origination logically requires its creator to be free because a necessitating cause would produce an eternal effect.

Why the Creator Must Be Voluntary Not Necessitating
Proof that the Eternal must be voluntary (mukhtār), not necessitating (mūjib).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the Creator must be a voluntary agent (mukhtār) rather than a necessitating cause (mūjib), based on the temporality of the world.

Core Idea
The temporality of the world proves that God acts by will, not by necessity.

This lesson unpacks a key argument from classical kalām: if the Creator were a necessitating cause (mūjib), the world would be eternal, which is false. Instead, He must be voluntary (mukhtār), acting by will and intention. We will examine the proof and its implications for understanding divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument sink in before you interpret.
The passage is dense; focus on the conditional structure.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The proof is built on a conditional: 'if He were necessitating, the world would be eternal' and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. Identify the premise and the conclusion.
This is a standard logical form in kalām.
3
Understand the three explanations for the will's attachment
The commentary explains why a voluntary act can be delayed: because the attachment of will (taʿalluq al-irādah) is originated, or is eternal but attached to a specific time, or is valid but not obligatory. Reflect on these.
These show that delay does not imply necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب أن يكون مختاراً؛ إذ لو كان موجباً لكان العالم قديماً ، وهو باطل. قوله : (يجب أن تكون مختاراً لئلا يكون إيجاده بالقصد الذي هو مسبوق بالعدم، ولا يلزم التخلف لأن تعلق الإرادة حادث أو لأنه تعلق في الأزل بوجوده في وقت مخصوص، أو لأن التعلق يقع على سبيل الصحة لا على سبيل الوجوب . قوله : ( لكان العالم قديماً لامتناع التخلف فيما يكون مستنداً إلى ذاته ابتداء أو بواسطة قديمة مشخصة يكون قديماً بالشخص كالمبادي العالية والأفلاك وما يكون مستنداً إليه بواسطة الحوادث المتعاقبة بلا نهاية أعني الحركات تكون حادثة بالشخص على ما قالوا .
He must be voluntary; for if He were necessitating, the world would be eternal, and it is false. He said: (He must be voluntary) so that His bringing into existence is not by intention that is preceded by non-existence, and non-occurrence is not necessary because the attachment of will is originated, or because it attached in pre-eternity to its existence at a specific time, or because the attachment falls under the category of validity not obligation. He said: (the world would be eternal) due to the impossibility of non-occurrence in what depends on His essence [directly] or through an eternal differentiating intermediary that is eternal in its individuality, like the higher principles and the heavens, and what depends on it through an infinite succession of temporal events, namely movements, are temporal in their individuality according to what they said.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the Creator must be a voluntary agent (mukhtār) rather than a necessitating cause (mūjib). The proof begins with the premise: if He were necessitating, the world would be eternal (qadīm), but that is false (since the world is temporal). The commentary then explains two points. First, it clarifies why He must be voluntary: so that His act of creation is by intention (qaṣd) which is preceded by non-existence; the delay in the effect is not necessary because the attachment (taʿalluq) of the will to the act is temporal (ḥādith) or because the attachment in pre-eternity was to a specific time, or because the attachment is valid but not obligatory. Second, it explains why the world would be eternal if He were necessitating: from a perfect eternal cause, the effect cannot be delayed; it is either eternal as an individual (like higher principles and celestial spheres) or, if dependent on an infinite series of temporal events (like movements), each event is temporal in its individuality. Thus the temporality of the world refutes the necessitating cause.

Key Terms
مختار (mukhtār)
موجب (mūjib)
قديم (qadīm)
تعلق الإرادة (taʿalluq al-irādah)
حاجة (ḥājah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is voluntary, His will must occur in time, implying change in God.
Correction: The commentary shows that the will is attached in pre-eternity to a specific time, so no change in God's essence occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the consequence if the Creator were a necessitating cause (mūjib)?
A) The world would cease to exist.
B) The world would be eternal.
C) The world would be created by chance.
D) The world would have a temporal beginning.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of the world refutes the idea of a necessitating creator and establishes that God creates by will, not by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God were a necessitating cause, would you feel less responsible for your actions? How does knowing He chooses to create you change your relationship with Him?
?
Reflect on a time you assumed God's actions were automatic or mechanical. How does this passage correct that view, and what will you do to remind yourself that every moment of your existence is a free act of divine will?
