The Eternal Cannot Depend on a Cause
Objection that the eternal cannot depend on a cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the eternal cannot depend on any cause because the cause of need is origination, and how it challenges the theologians' position.

Core Idea
Need arises only from origination; the eternal, having no origination, has no need and cannot depend on a cause.

In this passage, the commentator examines an objection against the theologians who allow the eternal to depend on a cause. The objector argues that if need is caused by origination, then the eternal cannot be in need, so it cannot depend on a cause. This lesson unpacks that objection and the initial responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read from the beginning to the first 'قوله' to understand the main objection: the eternal does not depend on any cause because the cause of need is origination.
Notice that the objector says the theologians cannot consistently agree with the author's statement.
2
Understand the clarification
The commentator clarifies that negation of dependence follows from negation of need, not circularly. Notice the phrase 'وهو فرع الحاجة'.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
The commentator suggests the objection is better directed at the theologians' principle itself, not just the author. This is a key shift in perspective.
The objection becomes: the theologians contradict their own principle.
4
Examine the specification response
The text considers and rejects a specification that 'cause of need is origination' applies only to non-attributes, arguing the proofs are general.
This ends with a rhetorical question: 'so what is the justification for specification?'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( واعلم أن القائل إلخ إيراد على قوله : والحاصل جواز استناده إلى الموجب اتفاقاً بين الفريقين وحاصله أنه لا يتصور هذا الاتفاق من القائل من المتكلمين بأن علة الحاجة الحدوث، بل حقه أن يقول بعدم استناد القديم إلى علة . قوله : (لا يستند) أي لا يكون أثراً صادراً عنه على ما فسره الشارح قدس سره في أول المقصد ، وهو فرع الحاجة، فيصح تعليل نفيه بنفي الاحتياج، وليس نفي الاحتياج على ما وهم . قوله : ( واعلم أن القائل إلخ) ظاهره اعتراض على قول المصنف : والمتكلمون لو سلموا إلخ، بأنه غير مطابق للواقع، فقوله : فإِن قلت جواب عن هذا الاعتراض بأنه مطابق له، لكن يلزم هذا السياق أن لا يندفع اعتراض الشارح عن المصنف؛ لاندفاع جواب جوابه كما لا يخفى . فالأولى أن يجعل اعتراضا على أصل الكلام من المتكلمين أعني تجويزهم استناد القديم إلى الموجب فقوله : فإن قلت اعتراض آخر عليهم متفرع على الوجه الأول حاصله : أنهم خالفوا أصلهم في هذا القول أيضاً، فإن قلت: قولهم : علة الاحتياج الحدوث مخصوص بغير الصفات، قلت : أدلة نفي علية الإمكان تفيد العموم، فما وجه التخصيص ؟ . قوله : ( أن القديم لا يستند إلى علة إلخ) قيل وكذا الأزلي ولهذا قالوا الأعدام الأزلية لا تستند إلى العلة لاستمرارها . قوله : (إذ لا حاجة له إلى مؤثر إلخ فإن قلت : فيه مصادرة ظاهرة؛ لأن الاحتياج إلى الفاعل هو المجعولية كما صرح به في بحث الماهية وهي عين الاستناد إلى المؤثر . قلت : قد سبق في خاتمة أبحاث الممكن أن الحاجة متقدمة على الإيجاد المتقدم على الوجود، والاستناد إلى العلة هو وجوده منها فلا مصادرة. هذا والأظهر في التعليل أن يحمل على حذف المضاف
His statement: ('Know that the one who says...') is an objection to his statement: 'In sum, the possibility of its depending on the necessitating cause is agreed upon between the two groups.' The gist of it is that this agreement cannot be conceived from one who holds that the cause of need is origination; rather, he must say that the eternal does not depend on any cause. His statement: ('does not depend') i.e. it is not an effect emanating from Him according to what the commentator explained at the beginning of this objective; and it is a branch of need, so it is valid to negate it by negating need, and the negation of need is not as was imagined. His statement: ('Know that the one who says...') apparently is an objection to the author's statement: 'And the theologians, even if they conceded...' that it does not correspond to reality; so his statement: 'Then say' is an answer to this objection that it does correspond, but this context entails that the commentator's objection against the author is not repelled, because the answer to it is countered by another answer, as is not hidden. So it is better to make it an objection to the very speech of the theologians, namely their allowing the eternal to depend on the necessitating cause; so his statement: 'Then say' is another objection against them branching from the first face, the gist of which is that they also contradicted their own principle in this statement. If you say: 'Their statement that the cause of need is origination is specific to other than attributes,' I say: 'The proofs negating the causality of possibility indicate generality, so what is the justification for specification?' His statement: ('The eternal does not depend on any cause') it was said: likewise the pre-eternal, and therefore they said: pre-eternal non-existents do not depend on a cause because of their continuity. His statement: ('since there is no need for an effecter...') If you say: 'There is an obvious circularity in it; because need for an agent is the 'made-ness' as he clarified in the discussion of quiddity, and it is exactly the dependence on the effecter.' I say: 'It has already preceded in the conclusion of modal investigations that need is prior to bringing into existence, which is prior to existence, and dependence on the cause is its existence from it, so there is no circularity. This, and the more apparent in the explanation is to assume the deletion of the complement.'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence regarding the claim that the eternal (qadīm) can depend on a cause. The commentator notes that if the cause of need (ḥājah) is origination (ḥudūth), then one who holds that cannot consistently say that the eternal depends on a cause; he must say it does not. The text then clarifies that the negation of dependence follows from negation of need, not circularly. The commentator then offers an alternative interpretation: the objection is better directed at the theologians who allow the eternal to depend on a necessitating cause, as they contradict their own principle that need arises from origination. When it is said that their principle applies only to other than attributes, the response is that the proofs negating causality of possibility are general, so specification is unjustified. Further, it is argued that pre-eternal non-existents (like non-existence before creation) do not depend on a cause because they are continuous. A final objection about circularity—need for an agent is identical to being an effect—is answered by distinguishing that need precedes bringing into existence, while dependence is existence from the cause. The passage ends by suggesting the best explanation is to assume the deletion of a complement (i.e., the phrase is elliptical).

Key Terms
علة الحاجة
حدوث
قديم
استناد
موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the theologians' agreement on the eternal's dependence on a cause is consistent with their own principle that need arises from origination.
Correction: The passage shows that if need arises from origination, then the eternal—which is not originated—cannot be in need, so it cannot depend on a cause. Hence the theologians contradict their own principle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main implication for a theologian who holds that the cause of need (ḥājah) is origination (ḥudūth)?
A) The eternal can depend on a cause.
B) The eternal cannot depend on any cause.
C) The eternal is self-necessitating.
D) The eternal is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The eternal cannot depend on a cause because need arises from origination, and the eternal has no origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though Allah, the Eternal, depends on nothing—or do your anxieties reveal a hidden reliance on created causes, as if they were independent sources of provision? Will you examine your heart today and uproot any trace of attributing need to Him?
?
When you face a situation with no apparent means, do you panic because you see only secondary causes, or do you trust that the One who has no need can bring about the outcome from nothing? How can you train your heart to rely solely on the Necessitator?
?
The theologians struggled to admit that the eternal cannot depend on a cause. Do you struggle to admit that you must depend on Allah alone, without any intermediary? What created thing have you been depending on that you must now sever your attachment to, in light of this truth?

Circularity and Pre-Eternal Non-Existents
Response to circularity and pre-eternal non-existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator responds to the charge of circularity by distinguishing the priority of need over existence, and the argument regarding pre-eternal non-existents.

Core Idea
Need precedes existence, so need for an agent is not identical to being that agent's effect.

This lesson continues the discussion on the eternal's dependence on a cause, addressing a possible circularity: if need for an agent is the same as being an effect, then the reasoning is circular. The commentator clarifies that need is prior to bringing into existence, while dependence is existence from the cause. Additionally, the case of pre-eternal non-existents is examined.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim about pre-eternal non-existents
The passage states that pre-eternal non-existents (like non-existence before creation) do not depend on a cause because they are continuous.
This extends the principle to pre-eternal entities.
2
Identify the circularity objection
The objection says: need for an agent is 'made-ness' (majʿūliyyah), which is exactly being an effect. So arguing that the eternal has no need and therefore no dependence is circular.
The objection uses technical terms from quiddity discussions.
3
Understand the answer distinguishing priority
The answer: need is prior to bringing into existence, which is prior to existence. Dependence is existence from the cause, not the same as need. Thus no circularity.
This is based on earlier modal investigations.
4
Consider the ellipsis suggestion
The commentator ends by suggesting the best explanation is to assume a deleted complement in the phrase, indicating a concise style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
How does the commentator respond to the charge of circularity?
A) By denying that need exists in the eternal.
B) By stating that need is prior to existence, and dependence is existence from the cause.
C) By arguing that 'made-ness' is irrelevant.
D) By redefining 'need' as a property of quiddity.

Key Takeaway
Need is prior to existence, thus need for an agent is not identical to being an effect, and the eternal's independence is maintained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that need is prior to existence, not identical to it. Is there a deep need in your life — for approval, control, or purpose — that you have let define your identity? How would your life change if you saw that need as a prior condition rather than the core of who you are?
?
The objection in this lesson tried to reduce the believer's reasoning to circular thinking. Are there times when you dismiss God's signs as 'just circular reasoning' to avoid confronting Him? What would it take for you to honestly examine whether your hesitation is about logic or about surrender?
?
The passage distinguishes between the eternal God and pre-eternal non-existents — the state before creation. When you reflect on your own existence, do you truly accept that you were once non-existent and that God brought you into being solely from His will? How does that realisation reshape your gratitude and your urgency to live for Him?

Al-Razi's Argument Against an Eternal Caused Being
Al-Razi's argument against eternal caused being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of al-Razi's argument that an eternal being cannot be caused by an eternal cause.

Core Idea
An eternal effect would require the cause to act on something already existing or non-existent, both impossible.

Al-Razi presents a powerful argument against the possibility of an eternal being that is caused. He focuses on the timing of the cause's influence—either during the effect's persistence (re-creation) or during its non-existence (making it temporal). Both lead to contradiction. This passage is crucial for understanding the debate between philosophers and theologians on eternal causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Argument Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the cause's influence is examined in two possible states.
The argument assumes the cause is eternal and the effect is assumed eternal.
2
Identify the Two Possibilities
Al-Razi enumerates two possibilities for the cause's influence: either during the effect's persistence or during its non-existence/origination. Identify each and understand why each leads to a contradiction.
The first possibility is 'إيجاد الموجود' (bringing into existence what already exists).
3
Understand the Contradiction (Khalaf)
The conclusion is that in both cases the effect becomes temporal (حادث), contradicting the assumption that it is eternal (قديم). This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
This is a key argument in kalām against the philosophers' claim of an eternal world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استناده إلى الموجب القديم - قيد الموجب بالقديم؛ لأن استناد القديم إلى الموجب الحادث مستحيل بالضرورة، إنما الكلام في استناده إلى الموجب القديم - فمنعه الإمام الرازي لأن تأثيره فيه - أي تأثير الموجب في القديم - إما في حال بقائه - أي بقاء القديم - وفيه إيجاد الموجود وهو محال، وإما في حال عدمه أو حدوثه، وعلى التقديرين يكون حادثاً وقد فرضناه قديماً هذا خلف. فإن قلت: قد يحتاج ذلك القديم بالضرورة إلى أو بالقصد على ما قاله المتكلم يؤيد ما قلنا ما نقل في المباحث المشرقية عن بطلميوس من أن المختار إذا طلب الأفضل ولزمه لم يكن بينه وبين الطبيعة فرق.
As for its being dependent on the eternal necessitating cause – he restricts 'necessitating cause' to the eternal because the dependence of the eternal on a temporal necessitating cause is impossible by necessity; the discussion is only about its dependence on the eternal necessitating cause – Imam al-Rāzī denied it because its influence upon it – i.e., the influence of the necessitating cause on the eternal – is either in the state of its persistence – i.e., the persistence of the eternal – and in that case it would be bringing into existence something already existent, which is impossible – or in the state of its non-existence or its origination, and in both cases it would be temporal, but we have assumed it to be eternal; this is a contradiction. If you say: that eternal one necessarily needs ... or by intention as the mutakallim said, that supports what we said: what is narrated in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya from Ptolemy that if the voluntary agent seeks the best and it is necessary for him, then there is no difference between him and nature.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents al-Rāzī's argument against the possibility of an eternal being (qadīm) being caused by an eternal cause. The cause's influence must be either at a time when the effect already exists (impossible, as it would be re-creation) or at a time when it does not exist (which would make the effect temporal). The response suggests that the eternal effect is always in need of the cause, even in persistence, citing Ptolemy that a voluntary agent who always does the best becomes like a natural agent. The argument shows the tension between eternal causation and genuine contingency.

Key Terms
موجب قديم
بقاء
إيجاد الموجود
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's argument only applies to temporal causes, not eternal ones.
Correction: The argument explicitly assumes an eternal cause and shows that even then, the effect cannot be eternal; the cause's action would still require a temporal relation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what happens if the cause's influence occurs while the effect already persists?
A) It would be a natural process
B) It would be bringing into existence something already existent (impossible)
C) It would make the effect temporal
D) It would confirm the effect's eternity

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument shows that the concept of an eternal caused being leads to logical impossibility because the cause's action would either re-create the being or temporalize it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe in a God who creates. But if His act of creation were to produce an eternal world, would you be comfortable with a Creator whose act is either redundant (re-creating what already exists) or forces His creation to become temporal? How does al-Razi's argument affect your understanding of God's creative power? Are you willing to re-examine your belief in the light of this logical challenge?
?
Al-Razi's argument forces you to choose: either the world is temporal and caused, or it is eternal and uncaused. Which do you truly hold? And if you hold both, can you continue to live with that contradiction without confronting it?
?
This passage reveals that even the greatest theologians like al-Razi did not shy away from logical rigor. Are you equally rigorous in your own beliefs, or do you accept doctrines without testing them against reason? What would it cost you to follow the argument wherever it leads?

The Response: The Eternal Effect's Need in Persistence
Response defending eternal causation through perpetual need and Ptolemy's analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counter-argument that an eternal effect is always in need of its cause, even in persistence.

Core Idea
An eternal caused being perpetually needs its cause, similar to a voluntary agent who always chooses the best.

The response to al-Razi's argument suggests that the eternal effect is not independent but always in need of the cause. The citation from Ptolemy blurs the line between voluntary and natural agency. This response attempts to salvage the possibility of eternal causation. Note that the Arabic is incomplete in the original due to OCR issues.

Guided Path
1
Read the Response
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the response begins with 'فإن قلت' (if you say) indicating a rebuttal. It introduces the idea that the eternal being always needs the cause.
The Arabic has a missing word after 'إلى'; the meaning is reconstructed.
2
Understand the Concept of Need in Persistence
The response argues that the eternal effect is constantly in need of the cause for its persistence, not just for its origination. This is the key move to avoid al-Razi's dilemma.
This idea is similar to the kalām concept of continuous creation.
3
Analyze the Ptolemaic Analogy
Ptolemy is quoted: a voluntary agent who always seeks the best and acts accordingly becomes like a natural agent (determined). This suggests that if an eternal cause always produces the effect, it is not truly voluntary but necessary—thus the effect is necessary, not contingent.
The analogy supports the idea that the cause's action is constant, so the effect never becomes independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استناده إلى الموجب القديم - قيد الموجب بالقديم؛ لأن استناد القديم إلى الموجب الحادث مستحيل بالضرورة، إنما الكلام في استناده إلى الموجب القديم - فمنعه الإمام الرازي لأن تأثيره فيه - أي تأثير الموجب في القديم - إما في حال بقائه - أي بقاء القديم - وفيه إيجاد الموجود وهو محال، وإما في حال عدمه أو حدوثه، وعلى التقديرين يكون حادثاً وقد فرضناه قديماً هذا خلف. فإن قلت: قد يحتاج ذلك القديم بالضرورة إلى أو بالقصد على ما قاله المتكلم يؤيد ما قلنا ما نقل في المباحث المشرقية عن بطلميوس من أن المختار إذا طلب الأفضل ولزمه لم يكن بينه وبين الطبيعة فرق.
As for its being dependent on the eternal necessitating cause – he restricts 'necessitating cause' to the eternal because the dependence of the eternal on a temporal necessitating cause is impossible by necessity; the discussion is only about its dependence on the eternal necessitating cause – Imam al-Rāzī denied it because its influence upon it – i.e., the influence of the necessitating cause on the eternal – is either in the state of its persistence – i.e., the persistence of the eternal – and in that case it would be bringing into existence something already existent, which is impossible – or in the state of its non-existence or its origination, and in both cases it would be temporal, but we have assumed it to be eternal; this is a contradiction. If you say: that eternal one necessarily needs ... or by intention as the mutakallim said, that supports what we said: what is narrated in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya from Ptolemy that if the voluntary agent seeks the best and it is necessary for him, then there is no difference between him and nature.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents al-Rāzī's argument against the possibility of an eternal being (qadīm) being caused by an eternal cause. The cause's influence must be either at a time when the effect already exists (impossible, as it would be re-creation) or at a time when it does not exist (which would make the effect temporal). The response suggests that the eternal effect is always in need of the cause, even in persistence, citing Ptolemy that a voluntary agent who always does the best becomes like a natural agent. The argument shows the tension between eternal causation and genuine contingency.

Key Terms

Misconception: The response concedes that the eternal effect is temporal because it needs a cause.
Correction: The response argues that need in persistence does not imply temporality; the need is eternal and continuous.

C) To prove that the eternal effect does not need a cause

Al-Razi's Denial and the Objection of Need in Continuance
Al-Razi's denial of the eternal being caused with objector's counter using need in continuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument against the eternal being caused, and the objector's reliance on the self-evident need for continuance.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot be caused without contradiction, but its continuance still requires a cause.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology: whether the eternal (God) can be said to have a cause. Imam al-Razi denies it on logical grounds, while an objector counters that need for continuance is self-evident. You will see both the argument and the objection laid out with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the argument's structure. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the parenthetical explanations inserted into the Arabic.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two clear sections: al-Razi's denial (up to 'this is a contradiction') and the objector's counter-argument (from 'For you might say...').
3
Analyze al-Razi's proof
Al-Razi gives a dilemma: if the cause acts on the eternal while it exists, that is impossible (bringing the existent into existence); if while it does not exist, the eternal would be originated, contradicting its eternity.
Focus on why both horns are impossible.
4
Analyze the objection
The objector argues that need in continuance is known by necessity, giving three examples: the ring's motion needing the hand, knowledge needing life, and worldliness needing knowledge.
These examples illustrate that persistence can depend on a cause without implying origination.
5
Reflect on the tension
Consider how the objection does not directly refute al-Razi but points to a different aspect (need in continuance vs. origination). This prepares for further discussion.
The passage does not resolve the contradiction; it sets up a debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنعه الإمام الرازي لأن تأثيره فيه (أي تأثير الموجب في القديم) إما في حال بقائه (أي بقاء القديم) وفيه إيجاد الموجود وهو محال. وإما في حال عدمه أو حدوثه، وعلى التقديرين يكون حادثاً، وقد فرضناه قديماً هذا خلف. فإن قلت : قد يحتاج ذلك القديم (بالضرورة) إلى الموجب (في البقاء) فيكون مستمراً دائماً بدوام علته الموجبة، وذلك لأن الاحتياج في البقاء أمر معلوم بالضرورة لا يجوز إنكاره، كالمعلول الباقي فإنه محتاج في بقائه إلى علته كاحتياج حركة الخاتم في بقائها إلى حركة اليد، والمشروط الباقي فإنه أيضاً محتاج في بقائه إلى الشرط كالعلم المحتاج في بقائه إلى الحياة، والعالمية المحتاجة في بقائها إلى العلم.
So Imam al-Razi denied it, because its effect upon it (i.e., the effect of the efficient cause on the eternal) is either during its state of continuance (i.e., the continuance of the eternal), and in that case it is bringing into existence the already existent, which is impossible. Or it is during its state of non-existence or origination, and on both hypotheses it would be originated, whereas we have assumed it to be eternal—this is a contradiction. For you might say: That eternal necessarily needs the efficient cause in continuance, so it would be perpetual always with the perpetuity of its efficient cause, and that is because the need in continuance is a matter known by necessity, the denial of which is impermissible, like the persistent effect, for it is in need in its continuance of its cause, as the ring's motion in its continuance is in need of the hand's motion, and the persistent conditioned thing, it too is in need in its continuance of the condition, like knowledge in need in its continuance of life, and worldliness in need in its continuance of knowledge.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer structure. It first states Imam al-Razi's proof that the eternal cannot be dependent on an efficient cause: if the cause acts on the eternal while it exists, that is bringing the existent into existence (impossible); if it acts while the eternal does not exist or is originated, then the effect would be originated, contradicting its eternity. Then an objector raises a counter-argument: the eternal does need a cause for its continuance, as continuance is a contingent aspect. The response is that this need in continuance is self-evident and known by necessity, as illustrated by three examples: (1) an effect like a ring's motion needs the hand's motion to persist; (2) a conditioned thing like knowledge needs life to persist; (3) worldliness (the attribute of being knowledgeable) needs knowledge to persist. The passage does not explicitly resolve the tension but implies that need in continuance is necessary and does not entail that the eternal is originated in its essence.

Key Terms
القديم
الموجب
البقاء
الاحتياج
المعلول
المشروط
العالمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that need in continuance implies the eternal is originated in its essence.
Correction: The passage shows that need in continuance is a self-evident characteristic of persistent entities like effects and conditioned things, and does not entail that the eternal's essence is originated.

Checkpoint
What is Imam al-Razi's reason for denying that the eternal can be caused?
A) Because causing it would require either bringing the existent into existence or making it originated, both impossible.
B) Because the eternal is simple and cannot have a cause.
C) Because the eternal is self-sufficient and does not need anything.
D) Because the cause would have to be eternal as well.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi denies the eternal can be caused because any causal action would contradict its eternity, but the objector insists that need in continuance is a self-evident, non-originating dependency.

The lesson argues that need in continuance is self-evident, yet we often ignore it. Can you recall a moment when you felt your own existence or a blessing was sustained only by Allah's direct care? How should that reshape your gratitude and reliance?
?

Definition of Continuance (Baqa) as Identical or Additional to Existence
Defines 'baqa' (continuance) as either identical to existence or an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible interpretations of continuance (baqa) and why this distinction matters in Kalam.

Core Idea
Continuance is either existence itself in the second moment or a separate attribute.

This short passage introduces a key term in Kalam: baqa (continuance). Before theologians can debate whether God's continuance requires a cause, they must first agree on what continuance means. The text presents a clear disjunction: either continuance is identical to the thing's existence at a later time, or it is an additional attribute that itself must exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'either it is identical... or if not, then it is additional'.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two possibilities for continuance. List them: (1) it is the same as existence in the second moment, (2) it is additional to existence and thus must be an existent entity.
3
Reflect on the implication
If continuance is additional, then it must itself exist and thus require a cause. If it is identical, then no additional cause is needed beyond the cause of existence. This distinction is crucial for arguments about divine persistence.
This connects to broader debates about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذ قد يراد بقاء الشيء على وجوده، وهو نفس وجوده في الزمان الثاني، وإلا بل كان زائداً عليه فلا بد أن يكون موجوداً حاصلاً في ذلك الزمان.
And since continuance of a thing may be intended [as] its existence, and it is the same as its existence in the second moment, and if not, but rather it is additional to it, then it must necessarily be existent and occurring in that moment.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage provides a definition by disjunction. It states that when we speak of 'continuance' (baqa) of a thing, there are two possible interpretations: either continuance is identical to the thing's existence in the second moment of time (i.e., the thing's ongoing existence is nothing other than its existence at that later time), or it is an attribute additional to existence. In the latter case, if continuance is a distinct attribute, then it must itself be an existent entity that comes into being (occurs) at that second moment. The argument implies that if continuance is additional, it requires an efficient cause to bring it about, which has implications for the discussion of whether the eternal needs a cause for its persistence.

Key Terms
البقاء
الوجود
الزمان الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Continuance is always a distinct attribute from existence.
Correction: The passage shows that continuance may be identical to existence in the second moment, so it is not always a separate attribute.

Checkpoint

A) It is either the same as existence in the second moment or something additional to existence.
B) It is either a mental concept or a real attribute.
C) It is either eternal or originated.
D) It is either necessary or contingent.

Key Takeaway
Continuance (baqa) can be understood either as the thing's existence in the second moment or as an additional attribute that itself must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage suggests that if continuance were an additional attribute, it would need a cause. What does that imply about your own need for God's constant preservation? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient?
?
Consider the phrase 'the second moment'—your life is a series of such moments. Does your faith rest on the assumption that God will grant you the next moment, or do you presume upon His grace? How will you actively remember your dependence on Him today?

Fourth Objection: Eternal Effect or Regress
Presents the fourth objection: if the Necessary has eternal conditions, His effect is eternal; otherwise regress occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the theory that the world is created in time, which argues that an eternal cause with eternal conditions must produce an eternal effect, otherwise infinite regress follows.

Core Idea
Eternal cause with eternal conditions yields eternal effect; otherwise, endless regress.

This lesson presents a crisp dilemma that challenges the view that the world began in time. The objection forces us to examine whether the Necessary Being possesses the prerequisites for effecting from eternity or not, and draws out the consequences of each alternative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic and the English translation. Note the two conditional statements that form the dilemma.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents two alternatives: either the Necessary Being has the conditions of effecteriness from eternity, or He does not. Trace each alternative's consequence.
3
Understand the first alternative
If the conditions are eternal, then the effect must be eternal because a complete cause cannot be without its effect. Ask why this is problematic for the opponent.
4
Understand the second alternative
If the conditions are not eternal, then the effect depends on a temporal event, which itself depends on another, leading to infinite regress. Note why regress is considered false.
5
Analyze the conclusion
The argument concludes that since regress is false, the first alternative (eternal effect) is determined. Reflect on the implications for the origination of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابع: الواجب تعالى لو استجمع في الأزل شرائط المؤثرية ) في أثر من الآثار ( قدم أثره ) المستند إلى تلك المؤثرية الأزلية؛ لامتناع تخلف المعلول عن علته التامة، ( وإلا ) وإن لم يستجمع تلك الشرائط في الأزل (توقف) تأثيره في أي أثر فرض، (على) أمر (حادث) معتبر في مؤثريته فننقل الكلام إلى ذلك الحادث، ( وتسلسل ) لتوقف كل حادث على حادث آخر إلى غير النهاية، والثاني باطل فتعين
(The fourth: If the Necessary Exalted gathered in pre-eternity the conditions of effecteriness) with respect to any effect (He would make His effect eternal) dependent on that eternal effecteriness; because of the impossibility of the effect lagging behind its complete cause. (And if not) i.e., if He did not gather those conditions in pre-eternity, (His effecting would be dependent) for any effect assumed, (upon) a temporal matter considered in His effecteriness, so we transfer the discourse to that temporal matter, (and there would be regress) because each temporal matter depends on another temporal matter ad infinitum, and the second is false, so it is determined
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection, which uses a dilemma. If the Necessary Being (al-wājib taʿālā) possesses the conditions for effecting (sharāʾiṭ al-muʾaththiriyya) from eternity (azal), then His effect must be eternal because a complete cause cannot be without its effect. If He does not possess those conditions from eternity, then His effecting depends on a temporal event, and that temporal event itself depends on another, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). Since regress is false, the first option (eternal effect) is determined, implying that an eternal being can be caused in eternity.

Key Terms
واجب
أزل
شرائط المؤثرية
معلول
علة تامة
تسلسل
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that an eternal effect cannot be caused because it would require a temporal beginning.
Correction: The argument shows that if the cause is complete and eternal, the effect is also eternal without temporal priority; causal dependence does not require temporal priority.

Checkpoint
What is the second alternative in the fourth objection if the Necessary Being does not gather conditions in pre-eternity?
A) The effect is eternal
B) The effect depends on a temporal matter leading to regress
C) The effect is impossible
D) The effect is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that if the Necessary Being's effect is temporally originated, then either His conditions are eternal (forcing an eternal effect) or not (leading to regress), both devastating to the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every effect must either be eternal with God or lead to an infinite chain, what does that mean for your own existence? Are you living as a necessary consequence of His will, or do you see yourself as independent? How should this affect your daily submission to Him?
?
This argument shows that a complete cause cannot have a delayed effect. When you sin, do you recognize that your actions are caused by your choices, and that those choices flow from your inner state? What would it take for you to become a complete cause of obedience, with no delay between intention and action?

The Evenness of Four Argument
Al-Iji presents the sixth argument using the evenness of four to show the eternal can be dependent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of evenness of four is used to argue that the eternal can be dependent on another.

Core Idea
Dependence does not require a beginning in time.

This lesson presents a clever argument using a mathematical property to challenge a key assumption in theology. The opponent uses the evenness of four to show that something eternal (the evenness) can still depend on something else (the essence of four). This argument is designed to undermine the claim that the eternal must be independent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument's flow.
This is a concise argument; read it twice.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the subject (four), its property (evenness), the assumption of eternity, and the conclusion of dependence.
The evenness is 'always with' four and cannot be separated.
3
Understand the dialectical purpose
Realize that this argument is presented by the opponent to show that the eternal (qadīm) can be dependent, contrary to what the theologian (al-Iji) might argue.
This is a counterexample to the claim that the eternal cannot be dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : زوجية الأربعة ( مثلا ( معللة بذاتها من حيث ) دائمة معها ( بحيث يستحيل انفكاكها عنها، فلو فرض أن الأربعة ثابتة أزلاً كان زوجيتها أزلية أيضاً مع كونها مستندة إلى ذات الأربعة، فقد استناد ما لا أول له إلى غيره .
The sixth: The evenness of four (for example) is caused by its essence in that it is always with it, such that its separation from it is impossible. If four were assumed to be established eternally, its evenness would also be eternal while being dependent on the essence of four. Thus, something that has no beginning is dependent on another.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument from the opponent's side (those claiming the eternal can be dependent on an effect). It uses a simple mathematical example: the evenness of the number four is a property that is always concomitant with four and cannot be separated from it. If we suppose that four exists eternally, then its evenness would also be eternal. Yet, the evenness is dependent on the essence of four as its cause. Therefore, something that has no beginning (the evenness) is dependent on another (the four). This directly counters the claim that the eternal cannot be dependent. The argument is meant to show that the eternal (qadīm) can be dependent in its continuation, just as a property depends on its subject.

Key Terms
زوجية الأربعة
أزلاً
مستندة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that the number four itself is eternal.
Correction: The argument only assumes four is eternal hypothetically to make the point that even an eternal property can depend on its subject.

Checkpoint

A) The eternal cannot be dependent
B) The eternal can be dependent on another
C) The number four is eternal
D) Evenness is not a real property

Key Takeaway
The evenness of four argument demonstrates that an eternal property can be dependent on its subject, undermining the claim that the eternal cannot be dependent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

6 min
The Eternal Cannot Be Dependent
Imam al-Razi answers the objection, arguing the eternal cannot be dependent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal (al-bāqī) cannot be dependent on an effect during its continuation, based on the argument that the effect would either be ineffective or produce what already exists (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil).

Core Idea
The eternal cannot depend on a cause because that cause would be redundant or impossible.

Imam al-Razi presents a powerful response to the claim that the eternal could be dependent. He argues that if the eternal were sustained by an effect during its continuation, that effect would either have no real influence or would attempt to bring about what already exists—both impossible. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure of the argument: it begins with 'We say' and presents a dichotomy.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The argument splits the scenario into two cases: either the effect has no impact, or it would be a repetition of what already exists. Understand why both are problematic.
3
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Al-Razi claims his proof is stronger than the opponents'. Consider why this reasoning is considered decisive in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قلنا ) جواب لقوله : فإن قلت أي قلنا في جواب كل ما ذكرتموه ( دليلنا ) الدال على أن الباقي لا يجوز استناده حال بقائه إلى المؤثر ( أقوى ) مما تمسكتم به في جوازه، وذلك لأن المؤثر في الباقي ( حال البقاء إما لا أثر له ) فيه أصلاً فلا يكون مؤثراً فيه قطعاً، والمقدر خلافه (وهو) أي تأثيره في الباقي تحصيل ( الحاصل ) فيكون أيضاً باطلاً بالضرورة ، كما مر ) . هكذا أجاب الإمام الرازي
We say in answer to his statement: 'For if you say' — i.e., we say in answer to everything you have mentioned — our proof indicating that the enduring (al-bāqī) is not allowed to be dependent during its continuation upon the effect (al-muʼaththir) is stronger than what you have clung to in deeming it permissible. That is because the effect upon the enduring during its continuation either has no effect on it at all, so it is certainly not an effect on it, or else its effect on the enduring would be the acquisition of what already exists (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is also necessarily false, as has passed. Thus Imam al-Razi answered.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the response from the side denying that the eternal can be dependent. It begins with 'We say' (qulnā) as an answer to the opponents' arguments. The proof is stated: The effect (muʼaththir) on an enduring entity (al-bāqī) during its continuation is either (a) it has no effect at all, in which case it is not truly an effect, or (b) it would be 'the acquisition of what already exists' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible because the thing already exists. Therefore, the eternal cannot be dependent on an effect during its continuation. This is declared stronger than the opponents' proofs. The passage ends by attributing this answer to Imam al-Razi.

Key Terms
قلنا
الباقي
المؤثر
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response, why can't the enduring (al-bāqī) be dependent on an effect during its continuation?
A) Because the effect would be non-existent
B) Because the effect would either be ineffective or produce what already exists
C) Because the effect must be temporal
D) Because the enduring is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The eternal cannot be dependent on a cause during its continuation because any such cause would be either ineffective or redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does al-Razi's argument expose the futility of such reliance? What would you need to change today to truly depend only on the Eternal?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah is self-sufficient and that nothing can add to Him, why do you still feel anxious when secondary causes fail? How can this proof renew your trust in Him as al-Qayyum? What is one step you can take this week to act on that conviction?
?
Consider a habit or attachment you have: do you treat it as a 'cause' that sustains your wellbeing? How would you live differently if you knew with certainty that nothing besides Allah can truly cause or sustain? What would you need to let go of first?

The Flaw in the Proof: No Repetition of What Exists
al-Jurjani identifies the flaw in the previous proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the effect on an eternal entity does not involve 'tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil' and why the disputed proof fails.

Core Idea
The eternal's continuance with its cause avoids repeating what already exists.

The author begins by exposing a flaw in the previous proof that sought to show the need for an effecter. He argues that the effect on an enduring entity, even if eternal, can be understood as the entity's continuance due to the continuance of its effecter. This understanding avoids the problem of 'acquisition of what already exists' (tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil) and dissolves the proof. In this lesson, we will examine this critique and see why the proof is not valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the flaw in the previous proof
Read the passage and find the author's critique: the effect on the enduring is its continuance due to the continuance of the cause, which is not a repetition.
The key term is 'tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil'.
2
Understand the avoidance of tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil
Notice that the author argues that the effect is not a new act at a specific time, but the continuous duration.
3
Recognize the invalidity of the proof
The proof based on the impossibility of repetition fails because the scenario described does not involve repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : ( وقد عرفت ما فيه ) أي ما في هذا الدليل من الخلل، وهو أن التأثير في الباقي وإن كان قديماً هو أن يكون دوامه لدوام المؤثر فلا يكون تحصيلاً للحاصل، ولا في أمر متجدد لا تعلق له بالباقي من حيث هو باق، فلا يتم هذا الدليل فضلاً عن أن يكون أقوى، فلذلك أورد الأجوبة المفصلة بقوله : (بل الجواب إما عن دعوى الضرورة ) في قوله : قد يحتاج بالضرورة في البقاء، ( فالمنع) لازم لأن دعوى الضرورة في محل الخلاف غير مسموعة، (وحكاية) المعلول) المستند إليها في البقاء، (و) حكاية ( الشرط) مع المشروط الذي يستند إليه في بقائه ( فرع ،ثبوتهما، و نحن لا نقول (به ) أي بثبوتهما؛ إذ لا علية ولا شرطية عندنا بين الأشياء، بل كلها صادرة عن المختار ابتداء بمجرد اختياره

قوله : (وهو أن التأثير إلخ) يعني أن أثره البقاء في تمام مدته، وتحصيل الحاصل إنما يتوهم من اعتبار التأثير في وقت معين فإنه لكونه قديماً يتقدم البقاء على ذلك الوقت المعين. قوله : ( في قوله : قد يحتاج إلخ) يعني في دليل قوله : قد يحتاج نظراً إلى تحرير الشارح
منع
يصح
قدس سره وهو قوله : لأن الاحتياج في البقاء أمر معلوم بالضرورة، لأن المقدمة المدللة لا إلا باعتبار منع مقدمة من دليله وأما على ما قررنا فلا حاجة إلى هذه العناية . قوله : ( لأن دعوى الضرورة إلخ) محل الخلاف وإن كان احتياج القديم في البقاء لا الاحتياج في البقاء إلا أن المستدل إنما يقوم بعدم احتياجه لأجل بقائه كما يفصح عنه دليله، فكان الاحتياج مطلقاً في البقاء محل الخلاف .

قوله : ( وحكاية العلة مع المعلول إلخ) أشار إلى أن منع تلك المقدمة راجع إلى منع دليلها
أعني قوله : كالعلة والمعلول.

قوله: (بين الأشياء) أي الموجودات الممكنة، فالاستشهاد بحركة اليد وحركة الخاتم والعلم والحياة غير صحيح إذ ليس بينهما إلا مجرد الدوران وهو لا يفيد العلية.

يشفي

قوله : ( وهو أن التأثير في الباقي وإن كان قديماً إلخ ) قال الأستاد المحقق: هذا الجواب لا عليلاً لأن ذلك المؤثر إما أن يعطيه أصل الوجود أي يجعله متصفاً به، كما يفيده دوامه أولا، فإن كان الأول فليبن أنه في أية حالة يعطي القديم أصل الوجود، وإعطاؤه البتة يقتضي حالة لم يتحقق الوجود قبلها وإلا كان تحصيلاً للحاصل، ولا يتصور للقديم هذه الحالة، وإن كان الثاني لم يكن المؤثر مؤثراً لأن المؤثر إما الفاعل أو العلة المستقلة، وأياً ما كان يلزم أن يعطيه أصل الوجود ومحصلاً له، كيف وإنه قول بأن الممكن القديم لا يفتقر في أصل وجوده إلى المؤثر فمن أين يلزم افتقاره في دوام ذلك الوجود إلى المؤثر. نعم يرد على الإمام أنه قائل بأن علة الافتقار إلى المؤثر هو الإمكان، وبالصفات القديمة لله تعالى ولا شك أن الصفات ليست واجبة لذواتها فتكون ممكنة فيلزم افتقارها إلى المؤثر واستفادة وجوداتها منه فيلزم تأثير المؤثر في
The author said: 'You have known what is in it' — that is, what flaw is in this proof. It is that the effect upon the enduring, even if it is eternal, is that its continuance be due to the continuance of the effecter, so it would not be an acquisition of what already exists, nor in a renewed matter that has no relation to the enduring insofar as it is enduring. So this proof is not valid, let alone being stronger. Therefore he gave the detailed answers with his statement: 'Rather, the answer is either by denying the claim of necessity' in his statement: 'it needs with necessity in continuation' — so the denial is necessary because the claim of necessity in the place of disagreement is not accepted. And the report of the effect (maʻlūl) to which it is dependent in continuation, and the report of the condition with the conditioned that depends on it in its continuation, are branches of their establishment, and we do not say that they are established, since there is no causality or conditionality among things, but rather all are originated by the Free Agent (al-mukhtār) initially by mere choice. His statement: 'That is, the effect etc.' means that its effect is the continuation for its entire duration, and the acquisition of what exists is imagined only from considering the effect at a particular time, because, since it is eternal, the continuation precedes that particular time. His statement: 'In his statement: it needs etc.' means in the proof of his statement: it needs, in view of the commentator's exposition... Denial. Correct. May his soul be sanctified. And it is his statement: 'Because the need in continuation is a matter known by necessity, because the premised premise is not except by considering the denial of a premise from his proof; but according to what we have established, there is no need for this care.' His statement: 'Because the claim of necessity etc.' The place of disagreement, even if it is the need of the eternal in continuation—not the need in continuation—yet the arguer only argues by its lack of need due to its continuation, as his proof indicates; thus the need absolutely in continuation is the place of disagreement. His statement: 'And the report of the cause with the effect etc.' indicates that the denial of that premise reverts to the denial of its proof, namely his statement: 'like the cause and effect.' His statement: 'Among things' means among possible existents. So the citation of the movement of the hand and the movement of the ring and knowledge and life is not correct, because there is only mere concomitance between them, which does not establish causality. He heals. His statement: 'And that the effect upon the enduring even if it is eternal etc.' The master investigator said: This answer is not sound, because that effecter either gives it the origin of existence, i.e., makes it qualified by it, as its continuance indicates, or not. If the first, then it must be clarified in which state it gives the eternal the origin of existence; and its giving it absolutely requires a state in which existence was not realized before it, otherwise it would be acquisition of what exists, and this state is inconceivable for the eternal. If the second, then the effecter is not an effecter, because the effecter is either the agent or the independent cause; in either case, it must give it the origin of existence and make it exist. Moreover, it would be saying that the possible eternal does not need the effecter in the origin of its existence, so how would it need it in the continuance of that existence? Yes, it is objected to Imam that he says the cause of need for the effecter is possibility, and with the eternal attributes of God—there is no doubt that the attributes are not necessary in themselves, so they are possible. Thus they need the effecter and derive their existences from it, so the effect of the effecter is necessitated in
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a critique and elaboration of the previous response. The author (al-Jurjani) points out the flaw in the response's proof: the effect on an enduring entity, even if eternal, can be understood as the continuance of the entity due to the continuance of its cause, which is not a 'repetition of what exists' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) nor a new event unrelated to the entity's endurance. Thus the proof fails. The author then presents detailed alternative answers: first, denying the claim of necessity that the need in continuation is known by necessity, because that very need is the point of disagreement. Second, denying the analogy of cause and effect, as there is no necessary causal relation between things; all things are created directly by the Free Agent (al-mukhtār). The commentary then explains the nuances of the author's statements, including that the need in continuation is what is disputed, and that citing examples like hand and ring movement does not prove causality. Finally, the master investigator (al-Ustādh al-Muḥaqqiq) offers a further objection: the eternal possible cannot depend in continuation if it did not depend in origin, and the attributes being possible implies they depend on an effect. The passage ends incomplete.

Key Terms
تحصيل الحاصل
الباقي
المؤثر
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the eternal's continuance requires a repeated act of giving existence at each moment
Correction: The eternal's continuance is a single continuous effect due to the continuance of its cause, not a repetition.

Checkpoint
What is the flaw in the previous proof according to the author?
A) The proof assumes the eternal cannot be affected
B) The effect on the enduring is its continuance with the cause, not a repetition
C) The proof relies on an unprovable premise
D) The author does not find any flaw

Key Takeaway
The effect on an eternal entity is its continuance with the cause, not a repeated acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your own existence is sustained moment by moment by Allah, do you truly feel that dependence in your heart, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of your continuous need for Him?
?
The flaw discussed is that the eternal's continuance is not a repeated act. Reflect: Do you treat your relationship with Allah as a continuous connection or as a series of disconnected acts of worship? What would it mean to see your entire life as a single, continuous act of servitude?
?
Al-Jurjani critiques a proof that missed the mark. In your own life, where do you hold onto weak 'proofs' or justifications for your actions that do not align with the truth? What would it take for you to humbly accept correction like the author does here?

Denying the Claim of Necessity in Continuation
al-Jurjani presents the first alternative answer by denying the necessity of the need in continuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first alternative answer: denying the claim that the need in continuation is known by necessity.

Core Idea
The need in continuation is the very point of disagreement, not a self-evident necessity.

After exposing the flaw in the previous proof, the author presents detailed answers. The first answer is to deny the claim of necessity. The previous arguer asserted that the need in continuation is known by necessity, but the author argues that this very need is the point of disagreement. Hence, claiming necessity is not permissible. In this lesson, we will explore this answer and the reasoning behind denying the claim of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first answer: denying necessity
The author says the answer is to deny the claim of necessity in the need for continuance.
2
Understand why necessity is not accepted
The need in continuation is exactly the point of disagreement, so claiming it as necessary is circular.
The arguer's proof itself depends on denying a premise from his own argument.
3
See the conclusion: need in continuation remains disputed
The passage ends by affirming that the need in continuation is not settled.

?

Denying Causality and the Objection of the Master Investigator
al-Jurjani denies causality among things and presents an objection to the very notion of eternal dependent existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second alternative answer that denies causality between things and the further objection from the master investigator regarding the origin vs continuance of existence.

Core Idea
Things are not causally linked; all are created directly by the Free Agent.

The second alternative answer addresses the claim that the relationship between cause and effect is necessary. The author denies that there is any real causality among possible existents; rather, all are originated directly by the Free Agent (al-mukhtār). He then criticizes the examples like hand and ring movement. Finally, the master investigator (al-Ustādh al-Muḥaqqiq) raises a powerful objection: if the eternal possible does not depend on its cause at its origin, how can it depend in continuation? This leads to a brief discussion on the attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Examine the denial of causality
The author denies any real causal tie between things; all are directly from the Free Agent.
Examples like hand and ring are mere concomitance.
2
Understand the objection from the master investigator
If an eternal possible does not need a cause for its origin, it cannot need one for its continuance.
3
Apply to divine attributes
The attributes are possible, so they require a cause; this leads to the conclusion that God is the effecter of His own attributes.
The passage ends incomplete but the argument continues.

قوله : ( وهو أن التأثير في الباقي وإن كان قديماً إلخ ) قال الأستاد المحقق: هذا الجواب لا عليلاً لأن ذلك المؤثر إما أن يعطيه أصل الوجود أي يجعله متصفاً به، كما يفيده دوامه أولا، فإن كان الأول فليبن أنه في أية حالة يعطي القديم أصل الوجود، وإعطاؤه البتة يقتضي حالة لم يتحقق الوجود قبلها وإلا كان تحصيلاً للحاصل، ولا يتصور للقديم هذه الحالة، وإن كان الثاني لم يكن المؤثر مؤثراً لأن المؤثر إما الفاعل أو العلة المستقلة، وأياً ما كان يلزم أن يعطيه أصل الوجود ومحصلاً له، كيف وإنه قول بأن الممكن القديم لا يفتقر في أصل وجوده إلى المؤثر فمن أين يلزم افتقاره في دوام ذلك الوجود إلى المؤثر. نعم يرد على الإمام أنه قائل بأن علة الافتقار إلى المؤثر هو الإمكان، وبالصفات القديمة لله تعالى ولا شك أن الصفات ليست واجبة لذواتها فتكون ممكنة فيلزم افتقارها إلى المؤثر واستفادة وجوداتها منه فيلزم تأثير المؤثر في
The author said: 'You have known what is in it' — that is, what flaw is in this proof. It is that the effect upon the enduring, even if it is eternal, is that its continuance be due to the continuance of the effecter, so it would not be an acquisition of what already exists, nor in a renewed matter that has no relation to the enduring insofar as it is enduring. So this proof is not valid, let alone being stronger. Therefore he gave the detailed answers with his statement: 'Rather, the answer is either by denying the claim of necessity' in his statement: 'it needs with necessity in continuation' — so the denial is necessary because the claim of necessity in the place of disagreement is not accepted. And the report of the effect (maʻlūl) to which it is dependent in continuation, and the report of the condition with the conditioned that depends on it in its continuation, are branches of their establishment, and we do not say that they are established, since there is no causality or conditionality among things, but rather all are originated by the Free Agent (al-mukhtār) initially by mere choice. His statement: 'That is, the effect etc.' means that its effect is the continuation for its entire duration, and the acquisition of what exists is imagined only from considering the effect at a particular time, because, since it is eternal, the continuation precedes that particular time. His statement: 'In his statement: it needs etc.' means in the proof of his statement: it needs, in view of the commentator's exposition... Denial. Correct. May his soul be sanctified. And it is his statement: 'Because the need in continuation is a matter known by necessity, because the premised premise is not except by considering the denial of a premise from his proof; but according to what we have established, there is no need for this care.' His statement: 'Because the claim of necessity etc.' The place of disagreement, even if it is the need of the eternal in continuation—not the need in continuation—yet the arguer only argues by its lack of need due to its continuation, as his proof indicates; thus the need absolutely in continuation is the place of disagreement. His statement: 'And the report of the cause with the effect etc.' indicates that the denial of that premise reverts to the denial of its proof, namely his statement: 'like the cause and effect.' His statement: 'Among things' means among possible existents. So the citation of the movement of the hand and the movement of the ring and knowledge and life is not correct, because there is only mere concomitance between them, which does not establish causality. He heals. His statement: 'And that the effect upon the enduring even if it is eternal etc.' The master investigator said: This answer is not sound, because that effecter either gives it the origin of existence, i.e., makes it qualified by it, as its continuance indicates, or not. If the first, then it must be clarified in which state it gives the eternal the origin of existence; and its giving it absolutely requires a state in which existence was not realized before it, otherwise it would be acquisition of what exists, and this state is inconceivable for the eternal. If the second, then the effecter is not an effecter, because the effecter is either the agent or the independent cause; in either case, it must give it the origin of existence and make it exist. Moreover, it would be saying that the possible eternal does not need the effecter in the origin of its existence, so how would it need it in the continuance of that existence? Yes, it is objected to Imam that he says the cause of need for the effecter is possibility, and with the eternal attributes of God—there is no doubt that the attributes are not necessary in themselves, so they are possible. Thus they need the effecter and derive their existences from it, so the effect of the effecter is necessitated in
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a critique and elaboration of the previous response. The author (al-Jurjani) points out the flaw in the response's proof: the effect on an enduring entity, even if eternal, can be understood as the continuance of the entity due to the continuance of its cause, which is not a 'repetition of what exists' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) nor a new event unrelated to the entity's endurance. Thus the proof fails. The author then presents detailed alternative answers: first, denying the claim of necessity that the need in continuation is known by necessity, because that very need is the point of disagreement. Second, denying the analogy of cause and effect, as there is no necessary causal relation between things; all things are created directly by the Free Agent (al-mukhtār). The commentary then explains the nuances of the author's statements, including that the need in continuation is what is disputed, and that citing examples like hand and ring movement does not prove causality. Finally, the master investigator (al-Ustādh al-Muḥaqqiq) offers a further objection: the eternal possible cannot depend in continuation if it did not depend in origin, and the attributes being possible implies they depend on an effect. The passage ends incomplete.

Correction: Only mere concomitance exists, not causality.

Possible things depend on God directly; causality is not inherent in creation.

Distinction Between Two Types of Enduring Things and Refutation of the Objection
Refutation of the objection regarding enduring things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two types of enduring things and why the objection that effect during persistence in the temporal implies effect in the eternal is invalid.

Core Idea
Not all enduring things are alike: the possibility of an initial effect determines the possibility of its continuation.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection that might seem compelling at first glance. The objector argues that if an effect can occur during the persistence of a temporal thing, then the same should hold for an eternal thing. The author's refutation hinges on a crucial distinction between two types of enduring things. Let's explore this distinction and see why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the objection and response.
Notice the structure: objection, then 'قلت' indicating the start of the refutation.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence that presents the objection: 'إذا جاز التأثير حال البقاء هاهنا جاز هناك أيضاً'. Understand its logical structure: if it's permissible here, it should be permissible there.
3
Understand the distinction between two types of enduring things
The author distinguishes between 'الباقي الذي له أول' (enduring with a beginning) and 'الباقي الذي لا أول له' (enduring without a beginning). Grasp the difference and why initial effect is possible in the first but not the second.
4
See how the refutation works
The author denies the implication (الملازمة) by showing that the two cases are fundamentally different. Because no initial effect is conceivable in the eternal, its continuation is also inconceivable.
This is the key logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أردتم به الباقي الذي له أول، وهو في حال بقائه ممكن، ومستند إلى المؤثر، فهو مسلم، ولا يجديكم نفعاً . فإن قلت : إذا جاز التأثير حال البقاء هاهنا جاز هناك أيضاً. قلت: هذه الملازمة ممنوعة، فإن الباقي الذي له أول قد يتصور فيه التأثير ابتداء، فيتصور دوامه بخلاف الباقي الذي لا أول له إذ لا يتصور فيه ابتداء تأثير، فكيف يتصور دوامه.
And if you intend by it the enduring thing that has a beginning, and it is possible in its state of persistence and dependent on the effect, then it is conceded and does not benefit you. For if you say: If effect is permissible in the state of persistence here, it is permissible there as well. I say: This implication is prevented, for the enduring thing that has a beginning may be conceived to have an effect initially, so its continued effect is conceivable, contrary to the enduring thing that has no beginning, since no initial effect is conceivable in it, so how can its continuation be conceived?
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objector argues that if an effect can occur during the persistence of a temporal thing, it should also be possible for an eternal thing. The author responds by distinguishing between two types of enduring things: one that has a beginning (al-bāqī alladhī lahu awwal) and one that has no beginning (al-bāqī alladhī lā awwal lahu). For the temporal thing, an initial effect is conceivable, and therefore a continued effect is also conceivable. For the eternal thing, no initial effect is conceivable because there is no first moment, so a continued effect is also inconceivable. Thus the objection fails because the premise about the temporal thing, even if conceded, does not apply to the eternal.

Key Terms
الباقي الذي له أول
الباقي الذي لا أول له
التأثير ابتداء
دوام التأثير
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an effect is possible during the persistence of a temporal thing, it must also be possible during the persistence of an eternal thing.
Correction: The author explains that the possibility of an initial effect in the temporal (which has a beginning) does not imply the same for the eternal (which has no beginning), because no initial effect can be conceived for the latter, so its continuation is also inconceivable.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation in this passage?
A) The eternal thing cannot have any effect at all.
B) The eternal thing cannot have an initial effect, so its continuation is also impossible.
C) The temporal thing cannot have an effect during persistence.
D) The eternal thing is not dependent on any effect.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of an initial effect is the key difference between temporal and eternal enduring things, making the analogy invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something holds in one situation, it must hold in another, without examining the underlying differences? Think of a specific instance in your life. How can this lesson train you to be more precise in your thinking? Will you commit to pausing and checking for hidden assumptions before drawing analogies?
?
This passage distinguishes between the temporal and the eternal. Do you truly grasp that the eternal is fundamentally different from the temporal? In your heart, do you treat God as if He were just a longer-lived version of created things? What steps can you take today to deepen your awe of His eternity?
?
The objection fails because it ignores the necessity of an initial effect. In your own beliefs about the world's origin, do you clearly see that the universe's beginning demands a beginningless Creator? Or do you unconsciously evade this conclusion? What will you do to align your conviction with this logical necessity?

The Immediate Intellect: The Eternal Needs No Effect
Argument from immediate intellect that the eternal needs no effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the self-evident rational judgment that the eternal, being continuously existent, does not require an external effect to bestow existence.

Core Idea
The eternal needs no effect because it already exists.

This passage presents a powerful argument from immediate intellect. Since the eternal has always existed, it is impossible to bestow existence upon what already exists. This simple intuition suffices to establish the independence of the Eternal. We will also see a preliminary concession about temporality, but the core argument stands firm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage fully
Read the entire Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then focus on the first sentence.
The passage contains both the argument and a subsequent discussion.
2
Identify the intuitive judgment
Locate the phrase 'العقل ببديهته يحكم بأن القديم ... لا يحتاج إلى مؤثر'. This is the claim: the intellect judges intuitively.
3
Understand the reason
The reason is 'استحالة إيجاد الموجود'. Contemplate why this is impossible. This is a self-evident truth.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The author says 'وهذا هو مطلوبنا' indicating this is the desired conclusion. Realize that this argument demonstrates God's independence from any cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثالثة أن العقل ببديهته يحكم بأن القديم الذي هو مستمر الوجود في الأزل لا يحتاج إلى مؤثر يفيده الوجود ; لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود، وهذا هو مطلوبنا . ولا يجب منه كون الحدوث شرطاً للحاجة ومعتبراً فيها وحده أو مع غيره، على أنا قد نلتزم شرطية الحدوث في قبول التأثير; إذ قد أجبنا عن إبطال اعتبار الحدوث بما سبق. وهاهنا بحث : وهو أن القديم إذا لم يقبل التأثير أصلاً كان قبوله موقوفاً على انتفاء القدم الذي هو الحدوث من حيث الصدق، فيكون التوقف على انتفاء القدم توقفاً على الحدوث. وبما حررنا اندفع ما قيل التوقف بمعنى المعلولية والتأخر غير مسلم والاستلزام مسلم ولا فساد فيه لأنه لا يثبت شرطية الحدوث وما قيل لا نسلم أن انتفاء القدم عين الحدوث فإن الأول عدمي ومفهوم إضافي بخلاف الثاني. غاية الأمر التلازم ولا يلزم من شرطية أحد المتلازمين شرطية الآخر.
And regarding the third: the intellect by its intuition judges that the eternal, which is continuously existent in pre-eternity, does not need an effect that gives it existence, due to the impossibility of bringing into existence what already exists, and this is our goal. And it is not necessary from it that temporality is a condition for need and considered in it alone or with others, though we may concede temporality as a condition for accepting effect; for we have answered the invalidation of considering temporality by what preceded. And here is a discussion: that if the eternal does not accept effect at all, its acceptance is dependent on the negation of eternity, which is temporality in terms of truth, so the dependence on the negation of eternity is dependence on temporality. By what we have corrected, it is repelled that it is said: 'the dependence in the sense of effectness and posteriority is not conceded, entailment is conceded, and there is no corruption in it because it does not establish temporality as a condition', and what is said: 'we do not concede that the negation of eternity is identical to temporality, for the first is non-existential and an additive concept unlike the second'. At most there is correlation, and conditionality of one does not entail conditionality of the other.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument and a subsequent discussion. The author states that the intellect intuitively judges that the eternal, which has always existed, does not need an effect because it is impossible to bring into existence what already exists. This is the desired conclusion. The author then addresses the objection that this might imply that temporality is a condition for need. He concedes that temporality could be a condition for accepting effect, but not for need itself. A further discussion (bahth) follows: if the eternal does not accept effect, its acceptance depends on the negation of eternity, which is temporality in terms of truth. This might suggest that temporality is a condition. However, the author refutes this by clarifying that the negation of eternity is not identical to temporality in concept; the first is non-existential and an additive concept, while the second is existential. They are correlates, but conditionality of one does not entail conditionality of the other. Thus the objection is resolved without conceding that temporality is a genuine condition for the eternal's independence.

Key Terms
القديم
البديهة
إيجاد الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the eternal's independence requires it to have been created or caused.
Correction: The passage shows that the intellect judges the opposite: the eternal does not need an effect because it already exists.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the eternal not need an effect?
A) Because it is temporally prior to all effects
B) Because it is impossible to give existence to what already exists
C) Because the intellect cannot conceive of an effect
D) Because the eternal is beyond time

Key Takeaway
The eternal's existence is self-sufficient; it needs no creator because giving existence to what already exists is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage warns against confusing conditions that Allah has not placed. In your life, are there conditions you have unconsciously added to your faith or worship—thinking you must be 'good enough' before turning to Allah? How can you let go of those and approach Him directly?

Temporality as a Condition for Need? The Discussion
Discussion on whether temporality is a condition for need, with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced discussion about whether temporality is a condition for the need of an effect, and how the author refutes this using the distinction between negation of eternity and temporality.

Core Idea
Temporality is not a genuine condition for need; it is merely correlated.

After establishing the core argument, the author anticipates an objection: if the eternal accepts effect only upon negation of eternity (i.e., temporality), then temporality seems to be a condition. The author engages in a detailed discussion (bahth) to refute this. The key move is to show that the negation of eternity (a non-existential concept) is not identical to temporality (an existential one), so conditionality does not transfer.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage again
Focus on the part beginning with 'وهاهنا بحث'. Understand the structure: an objection, a reply, and a further distinction.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that if the eternal does not accept effect, then its acceptance depends on the negation of eternity, which is temporality in truth. This seems to make temporality a condition.
Note the terminology: انتفاء القدم = negation of eternity, الحدوث = temporality.
3
Understand the reply
The author refutes by saying that the dependence (tawaqquf) is in terms of effectness and posteriority, which is not conceded. But even if entailment is conceded, it does not establish temporality as a condition.
4
Grasp final distinction
The final refutation: negation of eternity is a non-existential, relative concept, whereas temporality is existential. They are merely correlated (talāzum). Conditionality of one does not imply conditionality of the other.
This is a subtle metaphysical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
