And regarding the third: the intellect by its intuition judges that the eternal, which is continuously existent in pre-eternity, does not need an effect that gives it existence, due to the impossibility of bringing into existence what already exists, and this is our goal. And it is not necessary from it that temporality is a condition for need and considered in it alone or with others, though we may concede temporality as a condition for accepting effect; for we have answered the invalidation of considering temporality by what preceded. And here is a discussion: that if the eternal does not accept effect at all, its acceptance is dependent on the negation of eternity, which is temporality in terms of truth, so the dependence on the negation of eternity is dependence on temporality. He concedes that temporality could be a condition for accepting effect, but not for need itself. A further discussion (bahth) follows: if the eternal does not accept effect, its acceptance depends on the negation of eternity, which is temporality in terms of truth. This might suggest that temporality is a condition. However, the author refutes this by clarifying that the negation of eternity is not identical to temporality in concept; the first is non-existential and an additive concept, while the second is existential. They are correlates, but conditionality of one does not entail conditionality of the other. Thus the objection is resolved without conceding that temporality is a genuine condition for the eternal's independence.

Key Terms
القديم
الحدوث
شرط الحاجة
انتفاء القدم
التلازم
المفهوم الإضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the eternal's acceptance depends on the negation of eternity, temporality is a condition.
Correction: The passage explains that the negation of eternity is not identical to temporality; they are merely correlated, so conditionality does not transfer.

Checkpoint
What is the main distinction used to refute the conditionality of temporality?
A) Temporality is a condition for need, not for acceptance
B) The negation of eternity is a non-existential concept while temporality is existential
C) The eternal does not accept effect at all
D) The intellect cannot judge temporality

Key Takeaway
Temporality is not a condition for the eternal's independence; the correlation between negation of eternity and temporality does not entail conditionality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is the Eternal who needs nothing, why do you still act as though your sense of worth depends on things that are not Him? What correlation are you mistaking for a condition in your life?
?
This lesson shows that correlation does not imply conditionality. In your relationship with Allah, do you assume that if a certain worldly blessing is missing, you are lacking His favor? Where does that assumption come from, and how can you align your heart with the truth of His absolute sufficiency?
?
You believe in the intellect's judgment that the Eternal needs nothing. Yet your anxieties reveal you often live as if you are not sustained by Him alone. What specific fear are you holding onto that contradicts this truth, and what is one concrete step you can take today to trust His independence over your own illusions?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
5 min
Free Agency vs. Necessitating Cause
Al-Iji gives the core response and clarifies its coherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's free agency (ikhtiyar) allows temporal delay of the effect, unlike a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat).

Core Idea
A free agent can delay action; a necessary cause cannot.

The fourth objection claimed that if God were the ultimate cause, the world must be eternal. Al-Iji's response hinges on a crucial distinction: God is a free agent, not a necessary cause. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how a free agent's will can attach to a future time without requiring co-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening response
Read the first few lines where the author states that God has all conditions but is a free agent, so delay is possible.
Notice the key phrase 'فاعل مختار' (free agent).
2
Understand why this refutes the objection
The objection only forces co-eternity if God were a necessitating cause. The author denies that premise.
3
Analyze the 'choosing' clarification
The commentator explains that choosing free agency does not abandon necessitation; the objection itself assumed necessitation.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

إذا كان موجباً يكون المعلول لازماً لذاته وما قيل إن ذلك الوقت الذي سيوجد فيه كان من جملة ما يتوقف عليه فلم يكن مستجمعاً لجميع شرائط الفاعلية في الأزل فوهم لأن ذلك لازم من لوازم التأثير تحققه بدونه فيها . قوله : (فقد عرفت ما فيه من أنه لا تعلق له بهذا المقام إذ المقصود بيان علة الحاجة لا بيان علة التصديق . قوله : ( لكنه فاعل (مختار قيل الجواب ليس بسديد لأنه لما ادعى الإمام أن أثر الموجب لا يكون قديماً وأقام الدليل عليه ادعى المعارض أن أثر الموجب قديم لما ذكره فالقول بأنه مختار رجوع عن الإيجاب إلى الاختيار فهو خارج عن قانون التوجيه وإنما لم يتعرض له الشارح اكتفاء بما سبق وأجيب بأن المعارضة إنما هي في ملخص الدعوى وهي أن أثر المؤثر لا يكون إلا حادثاً يصح قولهم أن العالم قديم مستند إلى موجب وملخص كلام المعارض أن المؤثر موجود عندكم وإن كان مختاراً ونحن نلتزم قدم أثره فبأي وجه حصل التأثير في And concerning the fourth (objection): we choose that He—i.e., the Necessary Being, exalted—has all the conditions of agency in pre-eternity, but He is a free agent, so He may delay the action to any time He wills, so the pre-eternity of His effect is not necessary; that would only be necessary if He were a necessary agent by essence, which is denied. His saying: 'we choose'—it is not hidden from you that if the fourth objection held, it would indicate the dependence of the eternal on the Necessary Being exalted, not the dependence on it on the supposition of its being a necessitating cause, but rather it would establish the dependence on the necessitating cause based on the impossibility of the eternal depending on the free agent. So choosing that He—exalted—is free is not a reversion from necessitation to choice, as some mistakenly thought. And it was said that the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) only left it here because he had already treated it in what preceded, so reflect. His saying: 'so He may delay the action to any time He wills' is by His will in pre-eternity relating to its existence in the future, and there is no delay of the effect from the complete cause in this, because delay in volitional causing is that it does not occur in the manner intended, not that it is temporally delayed. For that (temporal delay) is in necessitative causing, necessarily, because if the essence is a necessitating cause, the effect is concomitant with its essence. And what was said—that the time at which it will come to exist is among the things on which it depends, so He did not have all conditions of agency in pre-eternity—is a false fancy, because that (time) is a necessary concomitant of the act of influence, actualized without it, and not dependent on it. Similarly, what was said—we transfer the discussion to the temporal time and it leads to regress because time for us is an imagined renewed thing by which renewed things are measured—can be answered by choosing the second alternative by saying that the attachments of will are renewed and accepting regress in them. His saying: 'you have already known what is in it' is that it has no connection to this context, for the intended point is to explain the cause of need, not the cause of judgment. His saying: 'but He is a free agent'—it is said the answer is not sound because when the Imam claimed that the effect of a necessitating cause cannot be eternal and established proof for it, the opponent claimed that the effect of a necessitating cause is eternal due to what he mentioned. So saying He is free is a reversion from necessitation to choice, and that is outside the law of refutation. The commentator did not address it, relying on what preceded. And it is answered that the opposition is about the summary of the claim: that the effect of an agent can only be temporal; they say the world is eternal, dependent on a necessitating cause. The summary of the opponent's statement is: the agent exists according to you, even if free, and we affirm the eternity of its effect; so in whatever manner the effect occurred in that eternal according to you, that is the manner of the effect of the necessitating cause according to us. So the author answered that if He is free, He may delay the action even if He has all conditions. This—and you have known that the more appropriate in the context is that the question and all objections are on the assumption of necessitation. So it is appropriate to say: among the conditions is the attachment of the will, and then the effect must occur; if all conditions are present, delay is impossible, otherwise regress or necessitation follows. And it is answered that it is possible that the will in pre-eternity attached to the existence of the determined thing at a specific time in the future, so the conditions—including the attachment—are all present in pre-eternity while the determined thing is temporal. And there is a discussion in this, for from...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer passage responding to the fourth objection: that if God were a free agent, His effect need not be co-eternal with Him. The response is that God is indeed a free agent (mukhtar), not a necessitating cause (mujib bi al-dhat). A free agent can delay action, so the eternity of the effect is not required; only a necessitating cause would have a co-eternal effect. The commentator then clarifies that this choice does not abandon the position of necessitation (ijab) for choice; rather, the objection itself assumes necessitation. He explains that the free agent's delay is possible through the will's attachment in pre-eternity to the future existence of the effect, and this does not constitute a temporal gap in causation—temporal delay only occurs in necessitative causation. He refutes the claim that the future time is a condition that was absent in pre-eternity, arguing it is a necessary concomitant of influence, not a condition. He also addresses a potential regress argument about time, suggesting an answer involving renewal of volitional attachments. Finally, a discussion arises about the appropriate framing of the objection, concluding that all objections should be considered under the assumption of necessitation, and that the free agent's delay is consistent with having all conditions in pre-eternity, since the will's attachment to a future time is itself a condition present in pre-eternity. The passage ends abruptly with 'and there is a discussion in this, for from...'

Key Terms
موجب بالذات (mujib bi al-dhat)
فاعل مختار (fa'il mukhtar)
تأخير (ta'khir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming free agency (ikhtiyar) abandons the position of necessitation (ijab) entirely.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the objection itself assumed necessitation; the response does not revert to ikhtiyar but rather denies the premise.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the effect need not be eternal?
A) Because God lacks conditions of agency in pre-eternity.
B) Because God is a free agent who can delay.
C) Because the effect must be temporal due to its nature.
D) Because the objection is invalid on other grounds.

Key Takeaway
God's free agency breaks the necessity of a co-eternal effect, as a free agent can postpone action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that God is a free agent who can postpone actions without any deficiency, how would your daily reliance on Him change? Are there areas in your life where you demand immediate results from God, exposing a hidden belief in necessitation?
